Franchise Tax Board v. Municipal Court for Los Angeles Judicial Dist.


Civ. No. 42410


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


45 Cal. App. 3d 377; 119 Cal. Rptr. 552; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1694



February 18, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 7, 1975, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 1, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 26458, Ernest J. Zack, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged a writ and order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which required respondent municipal court to: (1) vacate an order directing the return to appellant of funds improperly seized pursuant to an invalid search warrant; and (2) enter a new order requiring that the funds be delivered to respondent franchise tax board pursuant to a jeopardy assessment of income tax due from appellant.


OVERVIEW: Following seizure of money from appellant taxpayer, an assignee made a demand for the funds and respondent franchise tax board entered a jeopardy assessment of personal income tax against appellant. Appellant challenged the validity of the seizure and respondent municipal court entered an order directing the funds be returned to appellant. Respondent franchise tax board filed an unverified petition for writ of mandate and for a temporary restraining order, and the superior court filed a writ directing respondent municipal court to vacate its order and entered an order prohibiting the return of funds during the pendency of proceedings. Affirming the judgment, the court held that the petition was proper because the failure to verify was cured by amendment, the order was proper because it was a stay order and not subject to the limitations applicable to temporary restraining orders, and the writ was proper because respondent municipal court lacked power to order return of the funds. The court found that respondent franchise tax board's remedy by appeal was inadequate and further proceedings by respondent municipal court were not required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court that respondent municipal court acted in excess of its jurisdiction in issuing an order that seized funds be returned to appellant taxpayer and found that the issuance of a writ prohibiting the return of funds pursuant to a petition by respondent franchise tax board was therefore proper.

 

638 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Board of Supervisors v. Dolan


Civ. No. 36076


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


45 Cal. App. 3d 237; 119 Cal. Rptr. 347; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1681



February 11, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 10, 1975.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent to cause to be printed residential rehabilitation bonds as required by section 11.13 of the bond resolution.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent clerk of petitioner San Francisco Board of Supervisors refused to comply with petitioner's bond resolution, asserting that the issuance of bonds and the use of bond proceeds as proposed would violate Cal. Const. art XIII, § 40, Cal. Const. art XXXIV, § 1, and Cal. Const. art XIII, § 25. Petitioner appealed to the Supreme Court (California), which submitted the cause for original jurisdiction by the superior court.


OVERVIEW: Respondent clerk of petitioner San Francisco Board of Supervisors refused to comply with petitioner's bond resolution, asserting violations of Cal. Const. art XIII, § 40, Cal. Const. art XXXIV, § 1, and Cal. Const. art XIII, § 25. The Supreme Court submitted the cause for original jurisdiction by the superior court, which issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent to print residential rehabilitation bonds as required under petitioner's bond resolution. Such bonds were deemed under the Marks-Foran Residential Rehabilitation Act of 1973, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 37910 et seq., not to constitute a debt or liability of the local agency and the bond resolution contained similar provisions. The city's program merely encouraged owners of property in the areas to be rehabilitated to repair and improve their own properties. Neither the Act nor the bond resolution adopted pursuant thereto violated Cal. Const. art XIII, § 25, which prohibited the giving or lending of public monies to private persons. The primary purpose of the program and the bond resolution was to promote the compelling public interest of preventing deterioration of urban neighborhoods.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent clerk to cause to be printed residential rehabilitation bonds as required by the bond resolution of petitioner San Francisco Board of Supervisors. Neither the Residential Rehabilitation Act nor the bond resolution enacted pursuant thereto contravened the California Constitution.

Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four

February 11, 1975

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found that it was liable to respondent materialman for unpaid work on a project.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city awarded a bid for street improvements to a contractor. Respondent materialman furnished labor and materials, but was not paid. The same surety that executed the labor and materialmen's bond also filed a release bond with appellant. Plaintiff made a claim for unpaid sums due it from the contractor. The trial court found that appellant was liable to plaintiff. The reviewing court agreed. The surety on any release bond furnished pursuant to Cal. SSTs. & High. Code § 5295 must be a different surety than the surety on the labor and materialmens' bond furnished under Cal. SSTs. & High. Code § 5290.


OUTCOME: A decision that found that appellant city was liable to respondent materialman for unpaid work on a project was affirmed because the same surety that executed the labor and materialmen's bond also filed a release bond with appellant.

People v. Browning


Crim. No. 24735


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


45 Cal. App. 3d 125; 119 Cal. Rptr. 420; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 1672



February 6, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 2, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 551530, Robert M. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As so modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sentenced him to prison after he was convicted in a court trial of first degree murder, first degree robbery, first degree burglary, felony joy riding, and second degree burglary. Defendant had been determined unfit to be treated as a juvenile under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707and was tried as an adult.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile court found defendant unfit to be handled as a juvenile, and he was tried and convicted as an adult of first degree murder, first degree robbery, first degree burglary, felony joy riding, and second degree burglary and sentenced to life in prison. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the trial court, with modification to sentences, holding that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707 was not unconstitutionally vague and finding no err in defendant's unfitness to be treated within the juvenile court system. Defendant's behavior pattern, the nature and circumstances of the crimes allegedly committed, his past record, and the degree of sophistication as related to criminal activities were properly considered. The court rejected the argument that improper treatment by juvenile authorities contributed to defendant's conduct. While a finding of unfitness was not appealable, it was reviewable by writ and by appeal from a criminal conviction. Cal. Evid. Code § 1235 did not deny defendant his right to be confronted with the witnesses against him under U.S. Const. amend. VI. The court modified the sentences to run concurrently because they arose out of the same transaction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment sentencing defendant to prison, after he was convicted in a court trial of first degree murder, first degree robbery, first degree burglary, felony joy riding, and second degree burglary, with modification to the sentence. The court held that all sentences were to run concurrently for the term prescribed by law because all crimes arose out of the same transaction.

Community Television of So. Cal. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 43851


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


44 Cal. App. 3d 990; 119 Cal. Rptr. 276; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 990



January 30, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 26, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 32582, Campbell M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city and county challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment to respondent public television station in respondent's action to recover paid real property taxes pursuant to the statutory exemption under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 214.


OVERVIEW: Respondent public television station filed an action against appellant city and county to recover real property taxes paid under protest. The lower court granted summary judgment to respondent, and the court affirmed. The court determined that respondent was exempt from the taxes pursuant to the welfare exemption of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 214. The court found that the statute under which respondent filed for its exemption, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 271.4 was not unconstitutional as a gift of public monies or as a violation of the equal protection clause. The court ruled that the legislature could constitutionally permit application of the exemption to property acquired after the March 1st lien date, and no specification of how the funds, which would have been paid in taxes, would be used was necessary to save the statute from attack. Because respondent was entitled to the exemption and the procedural statute for the exemption was constitutional, the court held that summary judgment for respondent was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of summary judgment in favor of respondent public television station in respondent's action against appellant city and county to recover real property taxes paid because the procedural statute for claiming the welfare exemption from the taxes was not unconstitutional.

643 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


De Bottari v. Melendez


Civ. No. 13659


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


44 Cal. App. 3d 910; 119 Cal. Rptr. 256; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 984



January 28, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 26, 1975. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 107438, Elwood M. Rich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant councilmen sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which denied their petition for a writ of mandate to compel appellee clerk to place their names on the ballot for a general election that was to be held within a year after their recall.


OVERVIEW: Appellant councilmen who were recalled in an election and replaced by newly elected councilmen attempted to file nomination papers to regain their offices, but the city clerk refused to accept them on the ground that Cal. Elec. Code § 27521 disqualified appellants from seeking councilman's office for one year following their recall. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment as moot. However, the court held that § 27521 was unconstitutional under the strict scrutiny test for violation of fundamental freedoms because § 27521 was not necessary to promote a governmental interest. The court ruled that the state's interest in the stability of municipal government may not be promoted by measures directly aimed at curbing debate on issues of public importance, no matter how divisive they may be. The state's interest may be furthered by preventing too frequent recalls, but § 27521 was not a necessary or appropriate means to that end.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order which denied appellant councilmen's petition and declared the issue moot because appellants were replaced by newly elected councilmen. The court held the statute in question was unconstitutional because, under a strict scrutiny analysis, the governmental interest involved could easily and conveniently be protected by means less burdensome to fundamental rights.

Adoption of Ahmed


Civ. No. 34315


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


44 Cal. App. 3d 810; 118 Cal. Rptr. 853; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 976



January 23, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. A 6729, Louis B. Dematteis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural father sought review of a decree of adoption of the Superior Court, San Mateo County (California) by which his two sons were adopted by respondent stepfather. The decree was granted despite appellant's opposition after the trial court determined that appellant's consent was not required by reason of his failure to contribute to his sons' support for more than one year.


OVERVIEW: Appellant natural father sought review of a decree of adoption, granted despite his opposition, by which his two sons were adopted by respondent stepfather. The trial court had found that appellant's consent was not required under Cal. Civ. Code § 224 because of his failure to pay child support, as ordered under a prior decree of dissolution, for more than one year. Such prior decree also had awarded custody to the mother. He urged that § 224 was unconstitutional, under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 21, as it denied equal protection to parents by providing different standards for loss of parental rights. The court affirmed the adoption decree as § 224 did not unconstitutionally discriminate against appellant where he had not met his obligation of support and the children had an urgent need for an alternative provision as by the adoption. Thus, the court found that § 224 was in accord with the interests of the children and furthered the state's compelling interest in making adoptions possible in such cases, and also found the determination, that appellant's failure to provide support, was willful and without lawful excuse, and supported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The decree of adoption, whereby respondent stepfather adopted appellant natural father's sons without his consent, was affirmed where the statute, under which the adoption was permitted, did not unconstitutionally discriminate against appellant as he had failed to pay child support, ordered under a prior decree of dissolution, for over a year, and the statute comported with the interests of the boys who urgently needed alternative provision.

 

645 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hopkins


Crim. No. 12357


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


44 Cal. App. 3d 669; 119 Cal. Rptr. 61; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 965



January 20, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 56110, Richard W. Rhodes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed as to counts one, two, three and four. However, the superior court will order the sentences as to counts one and three stayed, pending completion of the sentences on counts two and four, at which time the stay will become permanent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that convicted him of robbery within intentional great bodily injury and assault with force likely to do great bodily harm, arguing that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress his confession under Cal. Evid. Code § 1014.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery with intentional great bodily injury and assault with force likely to do great bodily harm. He appealed the judgment of conviction, arguing that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress his confession under Cal. Evid. Code § 1014, which held a patient's confidential communications with a psychotherapist to be privileged. In affirming defendant's conviction, the appellate court concluded that this privileged was qualified by Cal. Evid. Code § 1024, which indicated that no privileged existed if the psychotherapist believed that the patient was a danger to another and if communication was necessary to prevent the threatened harm. Where defendant's psychotherapist had reason to believe that defendant posed a danger to another and that disclosure was necessary to prevent such harm, defendant held no privilege under Cal. Evid. Code § 1014, and the trial court properly denied his suppression motion. In modifying the judgment, however, the appellate court concluded that because the offenses were an indivisible transaction incidental to one objective, the robbery of his victim, defendant could be punished only for one offense.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed, as the trial court properly denied defendant's suppression motion. Defendant held no privilege where his psychotherapist believed that he posed a threat to another and that disclosure was necessary to prevent such harm. The sentence was modified, however, as defendant could be punished for only one offense where his offenses were part of an indivisible transaction incidental to the robbery.

 

646 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Serna


Crim. No. 25153


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


44 Cal. App. 3d 717; 118 Cal. Rptr. 904; 1975 Cal. App. LEXIS 969



January 20, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied March 19, 1975. Tobriner, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A432503, Ralph A. Biggerstaff, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged the judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of the sale of heroin pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352 and sentenced them to imprisonment for five years to life with ineligibility for parole for three years.


OVERVIEW: Appellants pled guilty to the sale of heroin pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code §11352. They appealed the judgments, contending the sentences of imprisonment for five years to life with ineligibility for parole for three years constituted cruel or unusual punishment within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. I, § 6. The court affirmed the judgments, applying a three-prong test that included the nature of the crime and the criminals, the degree of danger presented to society, and a comparison of the punishment with punishments for other crimes in the same jurisdiction. The court held that the nature of the crime was a serious and deadly offense against society. The court noted that the constitution did not require perfect symmetry between the various acts defined as crimes and the corresponding punishments assigned. The court further held that appellants' punishment, while not totally reconcilable with the punishment for every other crime, fell within the permissible range of punishment for similar serious and dangerous crimes. The sentence was not so disproportionate to the crime that it shocked the conscience and offended fundamental notions of human dignity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments convicting and sentencing appellants for the sale of heroin. The court found that the sentences satisfied a three-prong test for determining whether a particular punishment was cruel and unusual. The court held that in light of the nature of the crime, and in comparing punishments both within and outside the court's jurisdiction, the sentences were proper and constitutional.

 

647 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Advance Transformer Co. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 45037


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


44 Cal. App. 3d 127; 118 Cal. Rptr. 350; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 746



December 27, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The alternative writ shall, therefore, be discharged and a peremptory writ of mandate shall issue directing respondent court to vacate and set aside its order denying petitioner's application and to grant petitioner a new hearing on said application.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff supplier appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted attachment against attachable assets of defendant corporation that received supplies, but denied attachment against assets of defendant corporation officers and loan guarantors.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff supplier sought to attach assets of defendant corporation, the party that received supplies, and defendant loan guarantors, who were corporation officers. The trial court granted attachment as to the assets of defendant corporation but not as to assets of defendant guarantors. The court reversed, finding that the trial court applied the wrong standard and that the correct standard for determining if the assets of defendant guarantors could be attached was to determine whether the guarantee was a part of their normal business so as to make issuable an attachment against them. To determine if defendant guarantors were engaged in business, the trial court was to find whether they were engaged generally in the business of guaranteeing for a consideration the debts of others, and if so, any liability incurred as a result was a debt arising out of the conduct of such business. The court remanded because it found that plaintiff was precluded from presenting evidence on the issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded for rehearing because the trial court applied the wrong standard for determining whether defendant guarantors' assets could be attached.

People v. Cummings


Crim. No. 6396


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


43 Cal. App. 3d 1008; 118 Cal. Rptr. 289; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1372



December 18, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 13, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-11303, John Neblett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted defendant of possession of marijuana for sale and for transportation of marijuana resulting from evidence being found in defendant's trunk at a checkpoint 70 miles north of the United States-Mexican border.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was directed to pull off the road for a secondary inspection at Temecula checkpoint located 70 miles north of the United States-Mexican border. When border patrol performed a routine immigration inspection, marijuana was found under a sleeping bag, and defendant was subsequently convicted of possession of marijuana for sale and of transportation of marijuana. On appeal, defendant argued that the search was unconstitutional because this was a permanent checkpoint, and the discovery of marijuana was unlawful. The court affirmed and held that similar convictions were consistently and repeatedly upheld based upon evidence seized during searches made at fixed checkpoints. The court held that the authority for the constitutionality of permanent checkpoints had long been established and that in balancing U.S. Const. amend. IV right of the individual against the constitutional mandate to secure the nation internally from aliens, and the permanent checkpoint activity of the border patrol satisfied the constitutional test of reasonableness. The court reasoned that the need for such checkpoints was obvious, and the intrusion to the individual was slight.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for possession of marijuana for sale and for transportation of marijuana were because the intrusion upon defendant by border check was slight when balanced against the protection of the entire population from the invasion of illegal aliens. The court reasoned that illegal aliens infiltrated the nation daily and constituted a serious threat to the general welfare of the nation and residents California particularly.

 

649 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thomas


Crim. No. 12512


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


43 Cal. App. 3d 862; 118 Cal. Rptr. 226; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1362



December 13, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 7133-C, Kenneth M. Eymann, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury found him guilty of taking his employer's calves, defendant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California) for grand theft in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487(3). Defendant contested the lack of the court's advice on the self-incrimination privilege, the failure of giving the jury a lesser-included offense instruction, ineffective assistance of counsel, and the constitutionality of § 487(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested after his employer's two newborn calves were discovered in his shed. After waiving his Miranda rights, defendant signed a written confession. Defendant was convicted of grand theft in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487(3). On appeal, the court held that because defendant was represented by counsel, the trial court had no obligation to advise him of his privilege against self-incrimination before he took the stand. Defendant claimed that he was denied effective assistance of counsel based on the failure to challenge the admission of his Miranda waiver and signed confession. Noting that the defense theory was the lack of intent by defendant to permanently deprive his employer of his property, the court found that allowing the statement's admission was a reasonable tactic to support defendant's posture that he had nothing to hide. Because § 487(c) defined the taking of calves as grand theft without regard to value, the court held that a lesser- included instruction on petty larceny was properly refused. Finding no merit to the constitutional challenge of § 487(c), the court affirmed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction on grand theft for stealing his employer's calves. The court held that the trial court did not have to advise on the self-incrimination privilege, that strategy rather than ineffective assistance could explain counsel not objecting to the confession being admitted, and that a lesser-included instruction was precluded by the statutory definition of the offense, which survived a constitutional challenge.

Chambers v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 14304


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


43 Cal. App. 3d 809; 118 Cal. Rptr. 120; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1357



December 10, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition for writ of prohibition is denied; the stay order, previously issued, is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County (California) from proceeding with her preliminary exam. Defendant contended that she could not prepare an effective defense due to her mental illness and that Cal. Penal Code § 1368.1 (1974) was unconstitutional. She was charged with battery on peace officer and resisting arrest under Cal. Penal Code §§ 243, 148.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied defendant's request to suspend the proceedings under Cal. Penal Code § 1368 (a). Defendant contended that her mental illness prevented her from preparing an effective defense. She also contended that Cal. Penal Code § 1368.1 (1974) was unconstitutional. Defendant requested a writ of prohibition to stop the proceedings in which she was charged with battery on a peace officer under Cal. Penal Code § 243 and resisting arrest under Cal. Penal Code § 148. The court denied the writ and held that it was in defendant's best interest for the exam to proceed because it eliminated the possibility of her being indefinitely committed when probable cause had not been established. The court also concluded that Cal. Penal Code § 1368.1 was constitutional because it advanced defendant's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's request for a writ of prohibition to suspend the criminal proceedings against her in respondent trial court and ordered that her preliminary exam on charges of battery on a peace officer and resisting arrest should proceed as it was improper to detain a mentally ill defendant indefinitely without a determination of probable cause.

Zetterberg v. State Dept. of Public Health


Civ. No. 43578


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


43 Cal. App. 3d 657; 118 Cal. Rptr. 100; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1345



December 4, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 2, 1975, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 29, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. EAC12751, Robert Firth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens sought review of summary judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) dismissing plaintiffs' action for declaratory judgment that recent legislation had not deprived defendant State Department of Public Health of duties to regulate air pollution or transferred these duties to defendant California Air Resources Board.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff citizens sought declaratory judgment that the Mulford-Carrell Air Resources Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 3900 et seq., had not preempted to defendant California Air Resources Board the regulatory authority of defendant State Department of Public Health over air pollution. Plaintiffs also sought a declaration that defendants had continuing discretions and duties in the field of pollution from all sources. The trial court granted summary judgment for defendants. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court. The court held that plaintiffs did not have standing from their status as citizens and taxpayers. The court held that the allocation of regulatory responsibilities and powers as between two government agencies was a matter of legislative determination and was not a justiciable case or controversy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff citizens' declaratory judgment action by summary judgment. Plaintiffs lacked standing and their action for declaratory judgment specifying the duties of defendant State Department of Public Health and defendant California Air Resources Board was not a justiciable case or controversy.

 

652 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pinell


Crim. No. 12848


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


43 Cal. App. 3d 627; 117 Cal. Rptr. 913; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1341



December 3, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied January 2, 1975, and respondents' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied January 29, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court Marin County, No. 4094, Vernon Stoll, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order setting aside the indictment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the order of the Superior Court Marin County (California), which granted defendants' motion to quash an indictment, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 995, on the ground that the grand jury that returned the indictment was improperly chosen.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, inmates charged with murder, conspiracy, and assault with a deadly weapon, moved to quash the indictment pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 995, on the ground that the grand jury that returned the indictment was improperly chosen. The trial court granted the motion, and the state appealed. The court reversed, holding that defendants failed to show purposeful discrimination by any of the five superior court judges who were the selectors of the panel, nor was there any inference of discrimination. The judges, in soliciting names for the grand jury, sought to obtain a fair cross-section of the community, including old and young, male and female, black and white, rich and poor, and those with a knowledge of each section of the county. Blacks constituted 6.7 percent of the panel of 30, where only 2.4 percent of the population of the county was black. Defendants, at most, showed that a statistical imbalance in this one grand jury by which one small racial group, one age group, and one earning group were underrepresented in proportion to the total population of the county, but the mere fact of inequality in the number selected did not in itself show discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court order setting aside an indictment, finding that defendants failed to establish purposeful discrimination or an inference of discrimination in the selection of the grand jury that returned the indictment. The mere fact of inequality in the number of a particular population selected did not establish discriminatory selection.

National Delivery Systems, Inc. v. City of Inglewood


Civ. No. 42459


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


43 Cal. App. 3d 573; 117 Cal. Rptr. 791; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1336



November 29, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 48769, David A. Thomas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a private postal system, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which refused to grant a preliminary injunction and dissolved a temporary restraining order. Appellant sought to enjoin enforcement of Inglewood, Cal., Municipal Code § 6471 (1972) by respondent City of Inglewood.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a private postal system, sought to enjoin enforcement of Inglewood, Cal., Municipal Code § 6471 (1972), which regulated peddling, solicitation, and distribution of advertising literature on residential property. The trial court issued an order denying the preliminary injunction and dissolving the temporary restraining order. On review, appellant sought an injunction against enforcement of the ordinance by respondent City of Inglewood and a declaration that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the trial court order, holding that appellant made no showing that § 6471, either on its face or as applied, prohibited anything other than purely commercial distribution of literature. As such, the ordinance was a valid exercise of the municipality's police power and did not trample on U.S. Const. amend. I rights. The court also held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's request for a preliminary injunction, after properly weighing the improbability of appellant's ultimate success in establishing a right to a permanent injunction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order refusing to grant a preliminary injunction and dissolving a temporary restraining order in an action by appellant, a private postal system, seeking to enjoin the enforcement of respondent City of Inglewood's ordinance regulating peddling, solicitation, and distribution of advertising literature on residential property. The court held that the ordinance was a valid exercise of respondent's police power.

 

654 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hernandez


Crim. No. 25040


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


43 Cal. App. 3d 581; 118 Cal. Rptr. 53; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1337



November 29, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 23, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-297533, Jack E. Goertzen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed her conviction before the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for possession of heroin, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to possession of heroin, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, after her motions to quash a search warrant and to suppress evidence were denied. Defendant was placed on probation on the condition that she serve one year in jail. Defendant sought review, and argued that the affidavit alleged no information from which the magistrate could reasonably infer that the suspect was keeping drugs at her apartment, the information was stale, and the officers unduly delayed executing the warrant. The court affirmed the judgment, concluding that the inference that the suspect was storing contraband at the apartment listed in defendant's name was not so unreasonable as to invalidate the warrant. The 12-day delay in obtaining the warrant was on the fringe of unreasonableness; however, absent abuse of discretion, the question was primarily for the issuing magistrate. The eight-day delay in executing the warrant was not unreasonable because defendant, who had the burden of proving the invalidity of the search, failed to show that there had been a change in circumstances relating to probable cause between the issuance and the execution of the warrant.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for possession of heroin was affirmed. Probable cause supported the inference that the suspect was storing drugs at defendant's home, and there was no abuse of discretion in finding that the information furnished to the magistrate was not stale. Although the warrant was not executed until eight days after it was issued, defendant failed to show that there had been a change in circumstances relating to probable cause.

People v. Carr


Crim. No. 10899


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


43 Cal. App. 3d 441; 117 Cal. Rptr. 714; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1328



November 25, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 72588, Walter F. Calcagno, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment sentencing appellant to prison is, therefore, reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted defendant of burglary and vehicle theft.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to theft and second degree robbery and was put on probation. Defendant was then charged with two new violations, burglary and vehicle theft. After his plea of guilty, defendant was sentenced to state prison and execution of the sentence was suspended subject to three years probation and one year in county jail. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the court did not have jurisdiction to revoke probation having lost jurisdiction under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.2 upon failure to revoke probation. The trial court, upon knowledge, either had to issue commitment within the time limits of the statute or lose jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the revocation of defendant's probation and held that the court did not have jurisdiction. The court found that the trial court failed to issue commitment within the limits of the statute.

 

656 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Adams


Crim. No. 7489


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


43 Cal. App. 3d 697; 117 Cal. Rptr. 905; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1348



November 25, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied December 9 and 10, 1974, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 23, 1975. Richardson, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Placer County, No. 41018, Leland J. Propp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Placer County (California) convicting him of receiving stolen property under Cal. Penal Code § 496. Defendant contended that the refusal to subpoena certain witnesses violated his rights, he was not afforded adequate time to prepare for trial on the amended information, his attorney was incompetent, § 496 was unconstitutional, and the sentence imposed was unconstitutionally excessive.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 496 after he had been arrested for receiving property stolen for him by two children who accompanied him to the store. He appealed, arguing the court erred in refusing to subpoena the children, he had insufficient time to prepare after the information was amended from "receipt" to "possession" of stolen property, his attorney was incompetent, § 496 was unconstitutional, and his sentence was cruel and unusual punishment. The court affirmed the conviction. Defendant had a right to present witnesses, but the defendant had access to another witness that was not a child, namely, himself. The record showed the children would have perjured themselves had they testified. The amendment to the information was inconsequential, and defendant further waived the issue by not complaining at trial. Section 496 was upheld as not violating the separation of powers doctrine because the district attorney had discretion by law and under the statute. Finally, the sentence was appropriate because defendant used children to steal, a practice the court found abhorrent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction, holding defendant's rights were not violated by the absence of child witnesses who might not have helped his case. Further, he had adequate time to prepare for trial, and his attorney was effective although he made tactical decisions with which defendant disagreed. The sentence imposed was appropriate for someone who used children to steal for him.

People v. Escarcega


Crim. No. 12602


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


43 Cal. App. 3d 391; 117 Cal. Rptr. 595; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1323



November 22, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 29, 1975. Tobriner, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 56331, Albert F. DeMarco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The "judgment of conviction and sentence" are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), convicting him of grand theft in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 484, 487, and assault with a deadly weapon under Cal. Penal Code § 245(a). He claimed that evidence of knives and a jacket was obtained from his car in violation of his rights under U.S. Const. amend. IV, and that the trial court erred in a jury instruction on assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped by police who were following his car on radio information after they saw the driver and the passenger change seats. The police found knives in plain sight, and they seized a stolen jacket. Defendant was convicted by jury of grand theft under Cal. Penal Code §§ 484, 487 and assault with a deadly weapon under Cal. Penal Code § 245(a). He claimed that his rights under U.S. Const. amend. IV were violated by an illegal stop. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court properly refused to instruct on Cal. Penal Code § 417 as a lesser offense necessarily included within the charge of assault with a deadly weapon. The court held that no rule prevented a policeman from following a vehicle on a public highway for any reason, and no unreasonable search or unconstitutional violation of privacy was involved. When the police observed the changing of seats in the moving vehicle, probable cause arose for the detention or arrest of the drivers for reckless driving under Cal. Veh. Code § 23103, and driving without a valid driver's license, under Cal. Veh. Code §§ 12500, 14601. The court found the trial court did not err and did not abuse its discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of grand theft and assault with a deadly weapon. The court held that defendant's Fourth Amendment rights had not been violated by the police when they stopped his automobile after observing the driver and the passenger changing seats. It held that the trial court did not err in refusing to instruct the jury on a lesser offense included in a charge of assault with a deadly weapon.

Estate of Ginochio


Civ. No. 33996


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


43 Cal. App. 3d 412; 117 Cal. Rptr. 565; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1325



November 22, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 40192, Norman A. Gregg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner illegitimate child appealed the order in favor of respondent, wife of decedent, in the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), denying his motion to set aside a will's admission to probate. Petitioner claimed that he was the decedent's heir under Cal. Prob. Code § 255, was entitled to notice of hearing under Cal. Prob. Code § 328, and his rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV or Cal. Const. art. I §§ 11, 21 were violated.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner illegitimate child was found to be the decedent's child in a paternity action. When the decedent died, respondent, wife of decedent, admitted his will to probate. Under the will, the decedent's estate was left to respondent and their daughter, and a clause excluded any other children. Petitioner was not given notice of the will's admission, and he filed a motion to set aside the probate court's order. The probate court denied the motion, and petitioner appealed, claiming that he was decedent's heir under Cal. Prob. Code § 255, was entitled to notice of hearing under Cal. Prob. Code § 328, and that the probate court's failure to give notice was a jurisdictional flaw that deprived it of the power to proceed. The court affirmed, holding that, in light of the clear language of § 255, petitioner's contention that a judicial determination of paternity was equal to the public acknowledgment of the child, was contrary to law. Petitioner's claim that § 255 violated his rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV or Cal. Const. art. I §§ 11, 21 failed because the statute was a reasonable state law. Thus, petitioner was not entitled to notice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the motion of petitioner illegitimate child to set aside the probate court's order in favor of respondent, wife of decedent, admitting the decedent's will to probate. The court held that even though there was a judicial determination that the decedent was petitioner's father, petitioner was not the decedent's heir, and was not entitled to notice of the admission of the will to probate.

People v. Label


Crim. No. 12140


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


43 Cal. App. 3d 766; 119 Cal. Rptr. 522; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1354



November 22, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 19, 1975.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Lake County (California), which convicted him of unlawful administration of a restricted dangerous drug and sentenced him to prison. Appellant contended the recipient of the injection administered by appellant was his accomplice and that her testimony was unreliable.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of unlawful administration of a restricted dangerous drug and sentenced to prison. The court affirmed the judgment of conviction. The court held that the recipient of an injection was not a participant in the criminal act of administering, and that instructions concerning the necessity of corroboration of her testimony as an accomplice was not required. The court held that evidence seized pursuant to a search warrant was admissible because appellant's motion to suppress was untimely and the affidavit in support of the warrant furnished internal guarantees of its reliability. The court found that the trial court's grant of immunity to a witness, limited to specific times and criminal acts, did not deprive appellant of his confrontation rights. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying probation because there was no showing of bias and the probation officer's recommendation was not binding. Finding no prejudicial error, the court concluded that appellant was not deprived the effective assistance of counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which convicted appellant of unlawful administration of a restricted dangerous drug. The court held that the trial court's evidentiary rulings, including the admission of testimony of the recipient of the injection administered by appellant, were proper.

CEEED v. California Coastal Zone Conservation Com.


Civ. No. 13691


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


43 Cal. App. 3d 306; 118 Cal. Rptr. 315; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1320



November 19, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 199819, Robert P. Kneeland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, associations and unions, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which upheld the constitutionality of the Coastal Zone Conservation Act of 1972, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 27000 et seq. in appellants action for declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: The Coastal Zone Conservation Act of 1972, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 27000 et seq., was approved by the voters to preserve the coastal zone. Plaintiffs, associations and unions, challenged the act as unconstitutional. The trial court found the act to be constitutional, and the court affirmed on appeal. The court held that the state need not afford property owners notice and a hearing prior to enacting an interim permit system to control land use which threatened harm to the state's resources pending development of a comprehensive plan. The plan imposed no restriction on land use, because the conservation commission created by the act was only empowered to adopt a plan for submission to the legislature. The act was not an invalid state intrusion into municipal affairs under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(a), because the state was not precluded from regulating land use when necessary to further the state's interest. Moreover, the act did not interfere with the right to travel because it was not discriminatory, it imposed no residence requirement, and it exacted no penalty for exercising the right. Finally, the act did not violate fundamental rights of minority groups.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the Coastal Zone Conservation Act of 1972 was constitutional because the act sought to further legitimate governmental goals and did not violate constitutional rights.

Gutknecht v. City of Sausalito


Civ. No. 32394


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


43 Cal. App. 3d 269; 117 Cal. Rptr. 782; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1317



November 19, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Marin County, Nos. 58466, 58463, E. Warren McGuire, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Each party is to bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff food take-out businesses appealed from a judgment by the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which upheld as constitutional a business license ordinance passed by defendant city. Defendant challenged the trial court's limitation on the retroactive application of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff food take-out businesses challenged the validity of defendant city's business license ordinance and sought a refund of the license taxes paid. The trial court upheld the ordinance and denied the refunds, except for a portion resulting from a retroactive application. The court affirmed, concluding that the ordinance was not unconstitutional for vagueness or a denial of equal protection. The court held that the ordinance provided a standard by which to judge whether food was "take-out" and that the term was sufficiently clear and unambiguous. The court held that the separate classification of take-out food businesses was not discriminatory or a subterfuge for eliminating take-out businesses from the community. The court found that the classification had a natural basis and was reasonably related to the intent to generate sufficient revenue to cover removal of litter attributable to the take-out businesses. The court held that the trial court properly limited retroactivity of the tax to the then current tax year.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which upheld defendant city's business license ordinance on constitutional grounds and limited a refund of the license taxes paid by plaintiff food take-out businesses to a portion resulting from an improper retroactive application of the ordinance.

Coe v. Davidson


Civ. No. 31851


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


43 Cal. App. 3d 170; 117 Cal. Rptr. 630; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1311



November 18, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 16, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 422165, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, registrar of voters, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted plaintiff political candidates' petition for a writ of mandate requiring him to accept plaintiffs' declarations of candidacy for the Alameda County Central Committee with a loyalty oath purged therefrom, without attempting to remedy the oath by severing or rewording the unconstitutional portion of the oath.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff political candidates petitioned for a writ of mandate requiring defendant, registrar of voters, to accept plaintiffs' declarations of candidacy for the office of county central committee with the loyalty oath stricken therefrom. Defendant had refused to accept the declarations because they were not substantially in the form prescribed by Cal. Elec. Code § 6491 with the oaths deleted. The trial court granted the writ, ordering defendant to accept the declarations as amended, finding the oath violative of U.S. Const. amends. I, XIV where it required plaintiffs to state "I am not knowingly a member of any organization engaged in such an attempt (to overthrow the government)." The trial court refused to allow defendant to change or sever the language of the oath, as it would involve "legislating" on the part of the court. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, concluding that the trial court was under no duty nor did it abuse any discretionary authority by failing to allow defendant to alter the oath, as the entire oath was unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, finding no error in the trial court's failure to add curative language to the oath or to construe it to say something that it obviously did not. Moreover, the court concluded that not only was the second part of the oath unconstitutional, but the entire oath was fatally infirm because of its vagueness and its susceptibility of being applied to both lawful and unlawful activities alike.

 

663 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Frazier v. Tulare County Bd. of Retirement


Civ. No. 1908


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


42 Cal. App. 3d 1046; 117 Cal. Rptr. 386; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1289



November 8, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 26, 1974, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 8, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 73446, Jay R. Ballantyne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal following the granting of the motion for summary judgment is reversed; the cause is remanded for a trial on the issues raised by the pleadings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant designated beneficiary sought relief from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which denied appellant's claim for death benefits under Cal. Gov't Code § 31780 and granted summary judgment in favor of respondents retirement system and surviving spouse.


OVERVIEW: Appellant designated beneficiary brought suit to recover death benefits payable through respondent retirement system and against respondent surviving spouse as a result of decedent's death before his retirement. The retirement board determined that because decedent was still married at the time of his death, respondent surviving spouse and not appellant was entitled to death benefits. Upon review, the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondent surviving spouse from which decision appellant sought relief. Appellant argued that Cal. Gov't Code § 31780, in effect at the time she was named beneficiary, entitled her to a lump sum death benefit as the named beneficiary. Section 31780, later amended, provided death benefits should be payable to a surviving spouse. The court reversed the decision of the trial court and held that under the circumstances, to apply either section of the code defeated decedent's right to designate his beneficiary without giving him any new comparable advantage and constituted an unreasonable impairment of a vested contractual right between decedent and respondent contrary to U.S. Const. art. 1, § 10, and Cal. Const. art. 1, § 16.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court granting summary judgment for respondents retirement system and surviving spouse and held that an employee's right to accrue benefits for a third person constituted a contract between employee and a retirement system which was entitled to protection from arbitrary infringement. The court remanded for trial on the issues raised by the pleadings.

 

664 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


English Manor Corp. v. Vallejo Sanitation & Flood Control Dist.


Civ. No. 31682


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


42 Cal. App. 3d 996; 117 Cal. Rptr. 315; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1286



November 7, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Solano County, No. 49822, Victor M. Castagnetto, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For similar reasons, we find no merit in English Manor's contention that the fee, as it relates to convalescent hospitals, is discriminatory. The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant convalescent hospital sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which dismissed without leave to amend an action that challenged the sewer connection fee that was established by Vallejo Sanitation and Flood Control District, Cal., Ordinance 11, in favor of respondent district.


OVERVIEW: Appellant convalescent hospital desired a sewer hookup for its facility. The connection fee was established by Vallejo Sanitation and Flood Control District, Cal. Ordinance 11, and was determined on a per bed basis. Appellant challenged the connection fee amount and claimed that Cal. Heath & Safety Code § 5471 established the proper fee schedule. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that dismissed the action, in favor of respondent district. The court reasoned that because a specific provision, such as Ordinance 11, governed a general one, such as § 5471, Ordinance 11 provided the proper measure of the connection fee. Additionally, the court noted that respondent obtained its powers from the Vallejo Sanitation and Flood Control District Act, 1952 Cal. Stat. ch. 17, p. 351, codified at Cal. Water Code ch. 67 app., and not from § 5471. The court determined that, under the presumption that ordinances were constitutional, Ordinance 11 was constitutional because the sewer connection fee that was based on a per bed basis was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant's challenge to a sewer connection fee, in favor of respondent district. The court held that because a special provision would govern a general one, the local ordinance established the proper connection fee and that the fee, which was determined by the number of hospital beds, was constitutional and reasonable.

 

665 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dubose


Crim. No. 12215


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


42 Cal. App. 3d 847; 117 Cal. Rptr. 235; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1271



October 31, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 26, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 85545, Raymond J. Arata, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted him of violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021, possession by a felon of a firearm capable of concealment upon the person. Defendant argued that the statute was unconstitutional, or, alternatively, that the court erred in overruling defendant's objection to cross-examination regarding his earlier possession of the same gun.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021, possession by a felon of a firearm capable of concealment upon the person. On appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of the statute and argued that the trial court committed error in overruling his objection to the state's cross-examination as to his earlier possession of the same gun here involved. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that the constitutionality of § 12021 had been established in a 1925 case and nothing in modern developments in application of the equal rights provision and the due process requirement had vitiated the rationale underlying the prior case. The court stated that the strong governmental interest in minimizing the danger to public safety arising from the free access to firearms that could be used for crimes of violence clearly outweighed the almost frivolous "burden" suffered by a convicted felon denied the "right" to carry a concealable weapon. The court also rejected defendant's arguments regarding cross-examination, finding that the state's questions were directly relevant to defendant's blanket denial on direct of having the gun at any other time.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that the statute was declared constitutional in 1925. The court rejected defendant's argument that modern developments in equal rights and due process laws vitiated the rationale underlying the 1925 case. The court rejected defendant's argument regarding the state's questions about his earlier possession of the gun, as the cross was directly relevant to defendant's denial on direct of having the gun at any other time.

 

666 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


Crim. No. 24999


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


42 Cal. App. 3d 706; 117 Cal. Rptr. 88; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1259



October 28, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A274992, Raymond Choate, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of grand theft-person in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 486 and 487 (2) and his sentence to state prison, contending that the imposition of a state prison term for the taking of property of a value of less than $ 200 from the person of another violated U.S. Const. amend. VIII as well as the Cal. Const. proscription against cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of grand theft-person in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 486, 487 (2) and sentenced to state prison. He appealed his conviction on the basis of proportionality and bias of the sentencing judge. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the imposition of a state prison term for the taking of property of a value of less than $ 200 from the person of another did not violate U.S. Const. amend. VIII or the Cal. Const. proscription against cruel and unusual punishment. The court noted that, where the theft was from the person, the possibility of violence and harm to the victim was greatly increased and observed that California's maximum sentence for theft from the person was not grossly out of proportion with sentences for a similar crime in other jurisdictions. The court likewise rejected defendant's argument that the sentencing judge was biased because he considered defendant's failure to appear for sentencing as well as his patently false explanation for not appearing in determining an appropriate punishment. The court ruled that both were proper items of consideration in determining the sentence as they bore upon defendant's receptivity to rehabilitation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence, holding that defendant's sentence was not unconstitutionally disproportionate to his crime. The court noted that California's maximum sentence for theft from the person is not grossly out of proportion with sentences for a similar crime in other jurisdictions. The court rejected defendant's argument that the sentencing judge was biased because that argument was not supported by the record.

Thole v. Structural Pest Control Bd.


Civ. No. 43346


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


42 Cal. App. 3d 732; 117 Cal. Rptr. 206; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1262



October 28, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 26, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 50315, David A. Thomas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court denying the peremptory writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner pest control operator sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a writ of mandate to set aside a decision of respondent Structural Pest Control Board suspending petitioner's license, pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 8620 et seq., as a pest control operator for 60 days for his failure to prepare required reports and other violations.


OVERVIEW: Structural pest control operators were licensed by the state, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 8560 et seq., and were subject to discipline by respondent Structural Pest Control Board (board), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 8620 et seq. Petitioner pest control operator was disciplined by respondent board which suspended his license for 60 days because of 2 dozen violations that involved services at 6 different residences. Some of the violations were failure to report conditions or to recommend corrective measures, with some of these violations considered to be grossly negligent acts. The trial court denied petitioner's writ of mandate to overturn respondent board's suspension, and the court affirmed the denial of the writ. The court found overwhelming evidence to support respondent's findings and further found that the statutes and regulations regarding structural pest control operations were not void for vagueness. The court found that gross negligence was a question of fact properly left to respondent's determination, and that petitioner's belated compliance with the contracted services was not a defense to the violations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the peremptory writ of mandate because evidence was overwhelming that petitioner pest control operator failed to inspect, to recommend, or to perform several required actions during inspections. The court found that respondent structural pest control board properly disciplined petitioner for violations, which were not unconstitutionally vague as written or applied.

 

668 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kirk


Crim. No. 12248


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


43 Cal. App. 3d 921; 117 Cal. Rptr. 345; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1366



October 21, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, Nos. MCR 1597, MCR 1598, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting probation is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which placed him on probation for offenses violating Cal. Penal Code §§ 484, 487; Cal. Ins. Code § 556; Cal. Penal Code § 548; and Cal. Penal Code § 148.5. Defendant contended that the trial court should have granted his motion for a mistrial to protect his right to a fair trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was placed on probation after being found guilty of several offenses violating the California Penal Code. During trial, defendant claimed that the trial court erred by admitting evidence obtained through an illegal search. Defendant also claimed that the trial court erred when it did not grant his motion for a mistrial. Defendant made the motion for mistrial after the court commented on the disappearance of the recordings pertaining to defendant's guilt. Defendant felt that such comments, though well intentioned, were of such a nature that the jury was prejudiced to the point that they would not be able to render a fair and just verdict. On appeal, the court affirmed the order of probation. The court held that the use of the recordings, and their subsequent disappearance in court, did not in any substantial way impinge upon defendant's right to a fair trial. The court believed that, after an examination of the entire cause and the evidence, it was not reasonably probable that a result more favorable to defendant would have been reached in the absence of the complained of error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting probation to defendant. The court held that the circumstances of the use of the recordings, and their subsequent disappearance, did not in any substantial way impinge upon defendant's right to a fair trial, because the entire case and the evidence supported the prosecution's case.

 

669 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Untiedt


Crim. No. 13114


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


42 Cal. App. 3d 550; 116 Cal. Rptr. 899; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1248



October 18, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 14, 1974, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 11, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Fremont-Newark-Union City Judicial District of Alameda County, No. 24309, John Schatz, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the municipal court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant dog owner appealed her conviction in the Municipal Court for the Fremont-Newark-Union City Judicial District of Alameda County (California), in which a jury found her guilty of violating Cal. Penal Code § 597f. Defendant appealed on grounds that § 597f was unconstitutionally vague and, therefore, void.


OVERVIEW: Defendant dog owner challenged her misdemeanor conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 597f, arguing that the words "without proper care and attention" in the statute were so vague as to be unconstitutional. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment of conviction. The court reasoned that the phrase "without proper care and attention," might by itself have conveyed little certainty of meaning but that read in the context of the entire section and its clear legislative purpose, the language was not so vague that men of common intelligence must necessarily have guessed as to its meaning and differed as to its application. The court therefore rejected defendant's contention that the statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which found defendant dog owner guilty of the misdemeanor of leaving animals without proper care and attention. The court found that although the law under which defendant was convicted might have contained certain words or phrases that were vague, the statute as a whole did have the required meaning, certainty, and lack of ambiguity and was not unconstitutionally vague.

 

670 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Perry


Crim. No. 6689


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


42 Cal. App. 3d 451; 116 Cal. Rptr. 853; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1238



October 11, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-30147, Daniel C. Leedy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As prayed by defendant, we reverse the judgment and remand for the pronouncement of judgment. That course will leave it open to the trial court, should it impose a sentence, to impose it either as to the conviction of a violation of Penal Code section 12021 or section 12020. The latter section carries a lesser maximum sentence than section 12021. It is conceded by defendant that a judgment imposing sentence for the section 12020 violation, and suspending execution for the section 12021 violation, could be accompanied by a finding that defendant suffered the prior felony conviction as alleged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which imposed concurrent sentences to prison after a jury had found him guilty of possessing a sawed-off rifle, and possession of a concealable firearm after he had previously been convicted of a felony, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 12020 and 12021.


OVERVIEW: After a jury had found defendant guilty of possessing a sawed-off rifle, and of possession of a concealable firearm after previously being convicted of a felony, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12021, defendant appealed the judgment which imposed concurrent sentences to prison. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment. The court held that where the prior conviction of a felony made criminal what was noncriminal in one not a felon, the minimum sentence may not be increased because of an indispensable prior conviction. The court also held that the trial court's imposition of concurrent sentences was error because, although defendant violated two statutes, he had only committed one culpable act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case for resentencing because the trial court erred by using a conviction which depended on defendant's status as a felon to increase his minimum sentence, and by imposing concurrent sentences for two convictions that arose out of a single culpable act.

Smith v. Evans


Civ. No. 14015


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


42 Cal. App. 3d 154; 116 Cal. Rptr. 684; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1213



September 30, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Butte County, No. 55115, Lucian B. Vandegrift, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The issues raised by the petition for writ of mandate to compel issuance of nomination papers became moot after trial court's denial of relief. The judgment will therefore be affirmed. Petitioners will recover costs on appeal. (See Zeilenga v. Nelson, supra, 4 Cal.3d at p. 724.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city council candidates challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which denied their writ of mandate, contending that the one-year residency requirement for city council candidates provided by respondent city's charter was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court denied petitioner city council candidates' writ of mandate, petitioners appealed. Although the court affirmed the judgment because the issue became moot, the court held that respondent city's one year residency requirement for city council candidates was not reasonably necessary as a practical means of achieving a legitimate governmental interest. Such a requirement was excessive to the needs of the election machinery; ineffectual as an assurance of knowledgeable candidates; and imposed a public disadvantage by interrupting the exercise of the political franchise. Therefore, respondent's residency requirement contravened the equal protection and the privileges and immunities clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of relief to petitioner city council candidates because the issues raised by the petition for writ of mandate became moot. However, the court held that respondent city's one year residency requirement for city council candidates violated the equal protection clause and the privileges and immunities clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.
