Blackburn v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 43068


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


42 Cal. App. 3d 175; 116 Cal. Rptr. 622; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1215



September 30, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 27, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 51533, Rodney K. Potter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a malicious prosecution action, plaintiffs sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained the defendants' demurrer without leave to amend and dismissed the action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were arrested for grand theft after defendant county employees filed a complaint. The charges were later dismissed for lack of probable cause. The defendants refiled and the charges were again, dismissed. Plaintiffs then filed an action for malicious prosecution. Defendants demurred and argued that the action was barred because defendants, as county employees, had immunity under Cal. Gov't Code § 821.6. The trial sustained the demurrer without leave to amend. Plaintiffs sought review and contended that the immunity provision was unconstitutional and denied them of due process. The court affirmed. It found that the immunity provisions of Cal. Gov't Code § 821.6 and Cal. Gov't Code § 815.2 (b) applied and that defendants were immune from civil liability. It further found that the provisions had been upheld on numerous occasions based on public policy and the necessity of public officers being able to perform investigations without fear of harassment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and found that statutory immunity for public employees in plaintiffs' action for malicious prosecution did not violate the plaintiffs' rights to due process under the law.

Hawk v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 32716, Crim. No. 11545


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


42 Cal. App. 3d 108; 116 Cal. Rptr. 713; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1210



September 27, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 25, 1974, and petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 11, 1974. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The judgments relating to the contempts herein referred to as Contempts Nos. 5 and 8 are annulled; the judgments relating to the remaining contempts are sustained. The petition for a writ of habeas corpus is granted in part, and petitioner is ordered discharged from any custody based upon the judgments relating to contempts herein referred to as Contempts Nos. 5 and 8. To the extent petitioner seeks habeas corpus relief in addition to that provided for herein, the petition is denied and the order to show cause is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, lawyer, filed petition for writ of habeas corpus and certiorari review of orders of the Solano Court (California) adjudging him in direct contempt of court and imposing sentence totaling 54 days in jail and fine of $ 3,200.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner represented a defendant in a criminal murder case before the trial court. The trial court found petitioner in direct contempt of court on multiple occasions and imposed jail time sentences and fines. The court found that the trial court had properly found contempt for: advising his client to disobey an order of the court, misconduct during voir dire, referring to his client by his first name and making reference to his friendship with his client, stating that the defendant was stripped of his presumption of innocence by the press with the help of the sheriff's office, humiliating a witness, repeating questions after an objection had been sustained, stating to the jury that the prosecutor had a reasonable doubt as to the defendant's guilt, improperly impeaching a witness, displaying an offensive personality, and failing to yield respectfully to the rulings of the court. The court found that the evidence did not support direct contempt findings for comments made by petitioner during his opening statement. The court granted habeas corpus relief in part; finding that petitioner should be dismissed from custody based on the judgments for contempt that were reversed.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the trial court's judgment of contempt on all but two of the incidents. The court annulled the finding of contempt based on petitioner's statements during his opening argument, and the court granted petitioner's writ of habeas in part.

Cummings v. Morez


Civ. No. 42965


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


42 Cal. App. 3d 66; 116 Cal. Rptr. 586; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1205



September 26, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of respondent Morez for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 21, 1974. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 92674, John T. Rickard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in favor of Mid-Century Insurance Company is affirmed without costs to either party. 4 The judgment in favor of William G. Morez, as special administrator of the estate of Percy Thornton, deceased, is reversed with costs on appeal to be recovered by the appellants.



4 Although Mid-Century Insurance Company was named as a defendant in the caption and various counts of the original complaint, demurrer was sustained and the insurance company was omitted as a defendant in the caption and body of plaintiffs' subsequent amended complaints, thereby working a discontinuance of the action as to it. ( Browner v. Davis, 15 Cal. 9, 11-12; Brittan v. Oakland Bank of Savings, 112 Cal. 1 [44 P. 339].) The jury trial proceeded solely against defendant special administrator and the jury's verdicts were in favor of "defendant" against the respective plaintiffs. However, the judgment on the verdict as entered by the clerk was in favor of the special administrator and Mid-Century Insurance Company. The appeal as filed is from the judgment entered "in favor of Defendants and against Plaintiffs, and from the whole and every part of said judgment." No mention or contention is made in any of the briefs regarding Mid-Century Insurance Company. Accordingly, we deem the appeal to have been abandoned as to said defendant insurance company. ( Munger v. Moore, 11 Cal.App.3d 1, 5, fn. 1 [89 Cal.Rptr. 323]; White v. Shultis, 177 Cal.App.2d 641, 648 [2 Cal.Rptr. 414].)

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a surviving passenger and the heirs of two other passengers, challenged a decision by the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which denied appellants relief in their action for personal injuries against respondent, the estate of the driver of an automobile.


OVERVIEW: A vehicle, owned and operated by respondent, and in which three passengers were riding, struck a guardrail and ran practically head-on into a bridge abutment. The accident resulted in the death of respondent and two of the passengers, and injury to the third passenger. Appellants, the surviving passenger and the heirs of the two deceased passengers, filed an action against respondent, the estate of the driver of the automobile. The trial court denied appellants relief. During the pendency of the appeal, the California Supreme Court rendered its decision in the case of Brown v. Merlo, 506 P.2d 212 (Cal. 1973), which declared the provisions of the automobile guest statute, Cal. Veh. Code § 17158, unconstitutional. In reversing the trial court's judgment, the court held that the Brown case applied retroactively to appellants' action. Moreover, the court held that appellants were entitled to rely on the Brown case, notwithstanding their failure to assert the unconstitutionality of § 17158 in the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, holding that case decided during the pendency of the appeal and which declared automobile guest statute unconstitutional, had retroactive effect. The court held that appellants' failure to challenge to constitutional validity of the statute during the trial did not result in a waiver of their right to assert the issue on appeal.

Long v. State Personnel Bd.


Civ. No. 14303


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


41 Cal. App. 3d 1000; 116 Cal. Rptr. 562; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 839; 13 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1322; 8 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9745



September 24, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 11, 1974. Richardson J., did not participate therein. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 229903, Lloyd Allan Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner chaplain challenged the denial of a writ of mandate from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), and the ensuing judgment in favor of respondent California State Personnel Board. Petitioner challenged the validity of a male-only certification on a number of specific grounds, all concerning sex discrimination in employment.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied petitioner chaplain a writ of mandate and rendered judgment in favor of respondent California State Personnel Board, upholding the validity of a male-only certification for a youth rehabilitation facility. Petitioner appealed on the basis of sex discrimination in employment. At issue was whether the male-only certification violated Cal. Const. art. XX, § 18. The court held that the male-only qualification in no way prohibited petitioner from pursuing the occupation of minister or chaplain. It merely prevented petitioner from pursuing that occupation at the facility in question. There was no conflict with art. XX, § 18. The court reviewed the action of respondent by applying the strict scrutiny standard and, at the same time, according to respondent a proper presumption of validity of its administrative action. The court concluded that respondent had demonstrated a compelling state interest in restricting the job of chaplain at the facility to men and not women. The position came within the bona fide occupational qualification exception of 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e-2(e). The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of a writ of mandate on the grounds that the male-only certification was within the bona fide occupational qualification exception of the Civil Rights Act. The interests of the public and those of the inmates were so overwhelming compared to those of petitioner chaplain and members of her class so as to constitute a compelling state interest in male-only certification, thus satisfying equal protection standards.

Weissbuch v. Board of Medical Examiners


Civ. No. 42575


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


41 Cal. App. 3d 924; 116 Cal. Rptr. 479; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 834



September 20, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 15, 1974, and the petition of the defendant and appellant for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 21, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 39572, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter is remanded to the trial court with instructions to issue a peremptory writ mandating Board to dismiss the proceeding and to vacate, annul and expunge its decision. Each party to bear his own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner doctor and respondent board each appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that commanded respondent, on writ of mandate, to set-aside its order under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 2384 and 2390 (1970) revoking petitioner's medical license for using marijuana, a narcotic under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11001 (1970), and remanded the matter to respondent for further proceedings.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner doctor was convicted of possession of marijuana, disciplinary proceedings were commenced before respondent board. At the time, marijuana was classified as a narcotic under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11001 (1970). Respondent found that use of a narcotic was per se unprofessional conduct under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 2384 and 2390 (1970), which made a physician's personal use of narcotics and a narcotic conviction itself misconduct. On writ of mandate, the superior court ordered respondent to set-aside the revocation and remanded the case for further proceedings. Both parties appealed. In reversing in part and remanding with directions to issue a peremptory writ compelling respondent to vacate its order and dismiss the proceeding, the court noted that § 11001 had been amended to eliminate marijuana as a narcotic and ruled that petitioner was entitled to the benefit of that amendment. The legislature could constitutionally provide for disciplining of physicians on the basis of personal use of narcotics, due to the physician's role in controlling dangerous drugs, but because marijuana had been declassified, its use was no longer per se misconduct.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded with instructions to issue a peremptory writ mandating respondent to vacate its decision and dismiss the proceeding because, pending review, the applicable statute had been amended to eliminate marijuana as a narcotic and petitioner was entitled to the benefit of that mitigating amendment, such that marijuana use no longer was per se professional misconduct.

People v. Malloy


Crim. No. 5871


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


41 Cal. App. 3d 944; 116 Cal. Rptr. 592; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 836



September 20, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petitions of the appellant and the respondent for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied November 21, 1974. Sullivan, J., and Clark, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-27896, Herbert S. Herlands, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. However, the Adult Authority is directed to grant parole consideration to defendant at such time as is otherwise appropriate under the laws of this state.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) convicting him of selling LSD in violation of the Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379, arguing that the court improperly considered his prior conviction for violating § 11379.


OVERVIEW: The people charged defendant with selling LSD in violation of the Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379, and alleged a prior conviction for violation of the same section. Defendant was found guilty as charged, and the allegation of the prior was found to be true. The court determined that the documentary evidence showed that defendant had pleaded guilty to the prior conviction. Knowledge of the contraband character of the items sold was reasonably inferred from the evidence without retrying the prior. Moreover, defendant never challenged his identity as the one who had suffered the prior conviction. Therefore, defendant's objection under Cal. Evid. Code § 352 should have been sustained. However, due to the overwhelming evidence of defendant's guilt, there was no reasonable probability that a verdict more favorable to defendant would have been reached had the evidence been excluded.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for selling LSD was affirmed because knowledge of the contraband character of the items sold was reasonably inferred from the evidence without retrying defendant's prior drug conviction.

People v. Vanley


Crim. No. 24259


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


41 Cal. App. 3d 846; 116 Cal. Rptr. 446; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 831



September 18, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 11, 1974, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 13, 1974. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-289819, Kathleen Parker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) whereby he was found not guilty by reason of insanity for vehicle theft pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 10851, and committed to a state hospital.


OVERVIEW: Driver left a sports car parked with the key in the ignition. Defendant, without permission, drove the car one block where he stopped for a red light, and where driver, who had pursued him on foot, caught up with him. Driver effected a citizen's arrest of defendant. Defendant was bound over on charges of grand theft auto and vehicle theft. He pleaded not guilty, and on defense's motion, two psychiatrists were appointed to examine him, who both found that defendant had been legally insane at the time of the commission of the offense and was mentally incompetent to stand trial. The trial court found that defendant was presently insane pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1368 and ordered him committed until his sanity was restored. Defendant appealed. The court reversed the judgment, holding that defendant did not enter his plea of not guilty by reason of insanity as prescribed by Cal. Penal Code § 1018, and therefore, his plea was invalid. The court further held that defendant's waivers of applicable constitutional rights relating to the insanity phase of the trial were inadequate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because defendant's plea of not guilty by reason of insanity was not entered as prescribed by statute, and thus, was not valid. In addition, defendant's waivers of applicable constitutional rights relating to the insanity phase of the trial were not adequate.

Larson v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 42761


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


41 Cal. App. 3d 360; 116 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 795



August 27, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 13, 1974, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 24, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-42843, Ernest J. Zack, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As so modified, the judgment is affirmed. Respondent shall recover his costs in this court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Real party in interest people sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a writ of prohibition barring respondent court from proceeding with a misdemeanor prosecution of petitioner suspect.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest people first charged petitioner suspect with a misdemeanor for burglary. After petitioner objected, real party resubmitted the charge as a felony. At a preliminary examination, the magistrate dismissed the felony complaint and ordered that the case proceed as a misdemeanor, on the original misdemeanor complaint. Defendant objected, arguing that his consent was required under the explicit language of Cal. Penal Code § 17(b)(5). The reviewing court agreed and ordered real party to either dismiss the count or amend the count to charge a felonious burglary. The advantage to a defendant of the speedier review of a municipal court-magisterial determination, by two, perhaps three, levels of judicial authority is of enough importance that he cannot be deprived thereof over his objection.


OUTCOME: A decision that granted a writ of prohibition barring respondent court from proceeding with a misdemeanor prosecution of petitioner suspect was affirmed as modified because he could not be deprived of the speedier review of a municipal court-magisterial determination over his objection.

Continental Cas. Co. v. State of California


Civ. No. 33770


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


41 Cal. App. 3d 259; 115 Cal. Rptr. 868; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 784



August 22, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 651209, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance company on behalf of sureties who had, upon nonappearance of their bailees, forfeited penalties assessed on bail, sought review of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco's judgment, contending the lower court had improperly sustained appellee's demurrer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant insurance company appeared in a class action on behalf of bondsmen who had paid forfeitures to the state bail bond which included a penalty assessment under California Penal Code § 13521. The bondsmen sought recovery of penalties paid and forfeited upon the nonappearance of their bailees when such penalty assessment was ruled unconstitutional by the California Supreme Court. The lower court sustained appellee's demurrer, and appellant sought review of that judgment. The court affirmed, finding the demurrer was properly sustained. The court determined that bondsmen entered a contract with their bailees which undertook the risk of nonappearance of the bailee, and presumably incorporated that risk in fees charged to their bailees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, finding the demurrer was properly sustained, because the sureties on behalf of whom appellant appeared undertook the risk that assessed penalties would be forfeited upon bailees' forfeitures of bail.

 

681 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Cheryl H. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 44374


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


41 Cal. App. 3d 273; 115 Cal. Rptr. 849; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 787



August 22, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ issue vacating the order of the superior court which restrains Cheryl from surrendering the child for adoption purposes and from placing it with an adoption agency.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minor sought a writ of prohibition to vacate an order of respondent Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which enjoined petitioner from surrendering control of her to-be-born child to an adoption agency after the father, a minor and a real party in interest, filed a complaint seeking a declaration of his rights in the child.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner minor was 16 years old and was pregnant out of wedlock. Father, a minor and a real party in interest, was 17 years old and filed a complaint seeking a declaration of his rights in the child. Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition to vacate respondent court's order enjoining petitioner from surrendering control of her to-be-born child to an adoption agency. The court granted petitioner's request for a writ of prohibition. The court held that under Cal. Civ. Code § 200, the father had no present custodial rights in the child as against petitioner. The court found that, while father was entitled to notice and hearing of any attempt to award custody to a nonparent under Cal. Civ. Code § 4600, his right to notice did not preclude petitioner from initiating proceedings to place the child for adoption. Significantly, the court found the father presented an unsympathetic claim for custody of the child. He previously did not have custody and took no steps to support or legitimate the child. Further, the father did not intend to take personal custody of the child or assume personal responsibility for the child's support. The court found no reason to delay adoption proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner minor's request for a writ of prohibition to vacate the trial court's order enjoining her from placing her to-be-born child with an adoption agency. The court held that the father, a minor and a real party in interest, had no present custodial rights in the child and that the father's right to notice of an attempt to give custody to a nonparent did not preclude petitioner from initiating adoption proceedings.

Hinckley v. Bechtel Corp.


Civ. No. 31265


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


41 Cal. App. 3d 206; 116 Cal. Rptr. 33; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 778



August 21, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 629309, Morton R. Colvin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff widow appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) in favor of defendants, decedent husband's former employer, in plaintiff's action seeking recovery under a group life insurance policy. Plaintiff sought benefits after decedent husband retired and was terminated from defendants' group insurance.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff widow, executor of decedent husband's estate, appealed from a trial court judgment for defendants. Decedent was employed by defendant corporation and retired but failed to convert the low cost group life insurance offered through defendants, corporation and insurer, into an individual policy within 30 days after termination of employment or within the period prescribed by Cal. Ins. Code § 10209(d). Plaintiff brought suit seeking damages for the monetary value of the group policies alleging breach of contract by defendants for failing to provide decedent with notice of termination from the life insurance policy plan and negligence by defendant corporation for failure to give notice. The trial court held for defendants and plaintiff appealed. The court held the breach of contract claim based on the employment contract and insurance contract were improper and that courts could not make agreements for parties or rewrite contracts because they operated harshly or inequitably as to one of the parties. The court held plaintiff failed to prove negligence as defendants owed no duty to decedent. The lower court judgment for defendants was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court in favor of defendants was affirmed.

 

683 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Allen


Crim. No. 11980


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


41 Cal. App. 3d 196; 115 Cal. Rptr. 839; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 777



August 20, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 17, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 15922, Robert J. Cooney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Finally, defendant contends that since the robbery and assault with a deadly weapon were part of an indivisible course of conduct with a single objective, he was subjected to multiple punishment, contrary to Penal Code section 654. The record indicates that while defendant was convicted and given concurrent sentences as to all three counts in the information, the sentences for assault with a deadly weapon and possession of a weapon by a former felon were stayed, with a stay to become permanent on his completion of the robbery sentence. The People, citing In re Wright, 65 Cal.2d 650, 655 [56 Cal.Rptr. 110, 422 P.2d 998], 6 People v. Niles, 227 Cal.App.2d 749, 755-756 [39 Cal.Rptr. 11], argue that since the sentences were stayed, defendant was not subjected to double punishment. The People also urge that since defendant possessed the weapon before and after the robbery, that count was a separate and distinguishable offense ( People v. Taylor, 2 Cal.App.3d 979 [83 Cal.Rptr. 119]). Penal [***2] Code section 654 prohibits concurrent sentences where the defendant's conduct, although violative of two penal statutes, constitutes an indivisible course of conduct with a single objective ( People v. Diaz, 66 Cal.2d 801, 807 [58 Cal.Rptr. 729, 427 P.2d 505]; People v. Medina, 26 Cal.App.3d 809, 824 [103 Cal.Rptr. 337]; People v. Solo, 8 Cal.App.3d 201, 208 [86 Cal.Rptr. 829]). Accordingly, the judgment is reversed insofar as it imposes a sentence for assault with a deadly weapon, and in all other respects, affirmed.



6 Wright, however, held, at page 656, that the appropriate procedure at the appellate level is to eliminate the effect of the judgment as to the less severely punishable offense insofar as penalty alone is concerned.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of orders of the Superior Court, Contra Costa County (California) that entered judgment, upon a jury verdict, convicting defendant of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, assault with a deadly weapon in violation of § 245(a), and possession of a concealable weapon by a felon in violation of § 12021, and that sentenced him.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, assault with a deadly weapon in violation of § 245(a), and possession of a concealable weapon by a felon in violation of § 12021. Subsequently, the trial court sentenced defendant to concurrent sentences as to all three counts and stayed the sentences for assault with a deadly weapon and possession of a weapon by a former felon. Defendant then sought review, contending the use of hair evidence deprived him of the U.S. Const. amend. V privilege against self-incrimination, a pretrial identification was improper, the improper impeachment of a witness deprived him of the right to confrontation, and he was subjected to multiple punishments for one course of conduct. On review, the court affirmed the convictions because none of defendant's asserted errors had merit. The court, however, reversed that part of the sentence which was based upon the conviction for assault with a deadly weapon because the robbery and assault were part of an indivisible course of conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of conviction but reversed the part of the sentencing order which was based upon the conviction for assault with a deadly weapon because defendant's allegations of error were meritless; however, the robbery and assault with a deadly weapon were part of an indivisible course of conduct with a single objective, and, thus, concurrent sentences were prohibited.

People v. Gilliam


Crim. No. 11581


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


41 Cal. App. 3d 181; 116 Cal. Rptr. 317; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 776



August 19, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 84155, Clayton W. Horn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted defendant of first-degree robbery pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 211.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped for a traffic violation and was found to have an outstanding traffic warrant. Defendant was arrested and informed of his right to post bail. Defendant could not post bail and was taken to the police station where the police learned that he matched the description of a robbery suspect. Defendant was given his Miranda rights and booked for the traffic violation and armed robbery. A booking search revealed a stolen credit card. Defendant denied knowing how it got into his pocket but later said that he had found it on the street. Defendant was convicted of first-degree robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211 and he appealed. The court affirmed and concluded that defendant was given a reasonable opportunity to post bail. The booking search was proper. Defendant's detention was reasonable. Defendant's statement about how he got the stolen credit card was obtained after his Miranda rights were given. There was no constitutional infirmity in admitting the credit card into evidence The evidence supported a finding that defendant was armed when he committed the robbery. Any impropriety of the state's comments at closing was not grounds for reversal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, and he was given a reasonable opportunity to post bail. The booking search conducted after he was arrested was proper. Defendant's statement about possession of the stolen credit card was obtained after he was given his Miranda rights. The evidence supported a finding that defendant was armed during the commission of the robbery. The state's comments during closing argument were not grounds for reversal.

Hunt-Wesson Foods, Inc. v. County of Alameda


Civ. No. 30182


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


41 Cal. App. 3d 163; 116 Cal. Rptr. 160; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 775



August 19, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 17, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 373096, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, County of Alameda and Assessment Appeals Board (Board), sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which reversed the decision of appellant board and found that respondent company was entitled to a tax reduction in its property tax assessment, and rendered a money judgment in favor of respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent company contested the decision of appellant Alameda County Assessment Appeals Board (Board) in its property tax assessment. The trial court, applying the independent judgment test, reversed appellant board's decision and rendered a money judgment in favor of respondent. The court found that appellant board obtained its authority from Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 9. The court found that appellant board, although a local agency, was to be considered an administrative agency, and that the proper standard for review of its decisions was the substantial evidence test. The court reversed and remanded the case for further review based on the substantial evidence standard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision which awarded a money judgment to respondent company based on a reduction of its property tax assessment, holding that the trial court should have applied the substantial evidence test to determine whether the valuation was correct, and not the independent judgment test which the trial court had applied.

7978 Corporation v. Pitchess


Civ. No. 42897


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


41 Cal. App. 3d 42; 115 Cal. Rptr. 746; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 764



August 9, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 2, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 57061, David A. Thomas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is reversed on both procedural and substantive grounds.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sheriff appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted plaintiff corporation's petition for a preliminary injunction enjoining defendant from enforcing Los Angeles County Licensing Ordinance No. 5860, §§ 2061, 2861.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sheriff appealed an order granting plaintiff corporation's petition for a preliminary injunction that enjoined defendant from enforcing Los Angeles County Licensing Ordinance No. 5860, §§ 2061, 2861. On appeal, the court reversed the order granting the injunction. The court held that plaintiff failed to show that the injunction was necessary due to irreparable harm. Rather, plaintiff merely asserted its desire to engage in the business of conducting dancing and presenting entertainment during hours prohibited by §§ 2061, 2861. The court also found that the preliminary injunction was not an appropriate remedy to prevent execution of a public statute by police officers. Finally, the court found that §§ 2061, 2861 were valid exercises of police power because a patent relationship existed between them and the public health, safety, morals, or general welfare.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting the preliminary injunction that enjoined defendant sheriff from enforcing a licensing ordinance. The court held that plaintiff corporation failed to show that the injunction addressed irreparable harm. The court found that the licensing ordinance was a constitutional exercise of police power.

Welch v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 44354


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


41 Cal. App. 3d 50; 115 Cal. Rptr. 729; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 765



August 9, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 3, 1974, and petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 17, 1974.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. A peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel the trial court (California) to grant his motion pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6 to disqualify the trial judge assigned to hear a motion to suppress evidence and trial on the merits on charges against him and his codefendants of possession of LSD, cocaine, amphetamine, and possession of marijuana for sale.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner and two codefendants were charged with possession of LSD, cocaine, amphetamine, and possession of marijuana for sale. Petitioner brought a motion pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6 to disqualify the trial judge assigned to hear a motion to suppress evidence and trial on the merits. The motion was denied, and petitioner brought a petition for a writ of mandate to compel the trial court to grant the motion, arguing that the denial of his motion to disqualify the trial judge violated his right to equal protection of the laws as the only reason he could not invoke § 170.6 was that a codefendant invoked it first, that he and his codefendant were not on the same side for purposes of § 170.6, and that the trial court prevented him from making a factual showing of adverse interest. The court ruled that § 170.6 did not arbitrarily discriminate against multiple parties, and that petitioner did not make a sufficient showing of adverse interest in the trial court. The court denied the petition, concluding that the trial court considered petitioner's motion several times and afforded him ample opportunity to file additional papers before it made a final ruling.


OUTCOME: Petition for a writ of mandate was denied because the burden was on petitioner to establish adverse interest in the trial court, and petitioner failed to make a sufficient showing of such adverse interest.

 

688 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Zeihm


Crim No. 24014


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


40 Cal. App. 3d 1085; 115 Cal. Rptr. 528; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 935



August 2, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 22, 1974, and the petition of respondent Coffman for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 10, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 281569, Newell Barrett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order setting aside the information is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed and set aside a repleaded charge, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 995, against defendants for conspiring to violate Cal. Penal Code § 288a, violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a, and sexual intercourse with a female under the age of 18 years. The rape charge had been dismissed in a preliminary hearing.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged in an information with conspiring to violate Cal. Penal Code § 288a, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a, and violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261.5 by commission of sexual intercourse with a female under 18 years of age. The charge of violating § 261.5 had been dismissed following a preliminary hearing by the magistrate, who found that the state failed to show that the requisite intent was present. As defendants had been committed to the trial court on the remaining charges, the district attorney repleaded the § 261.5 charge in the information, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 739. The trial court set aside the information under Cal. Penal Code § 995. As there was no factual dispute to have been resolved and the issue was one of law, the dismissed charge could have been pleaded in an information without the necessity of a new preliminary examination. The magistrate's dismissal of the § 261.5 count was based not on any evidentiary deficiency or the resolution of any factual dispute, but on an erroneous application of the law. Therefore, the charge of a violation of § 261.5 was not vulnerable to a motion under Cal. Penal Code § 995.


OUTCOME: The order that set aside the information was reversed. The magistrate's dismissal of the rape count was based not on any evidentiary deficiency or the resolution of any factual dispute, but on an erroneous application of the law. The same was true of the trial court's ruling on the motion to set aside the information. The rape charge as contained in the information was not vulnerable to such a motion.

People v. Duncan


Crim. No. 11224


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


40 Cal. App. 3d 940; 115 Cal. Rptr. 699; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 919



July 26, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-2407, W. Howard Hartley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order admitting defendant to probation is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), where he was admitted to probation following his conviction, by plea of guilty, to possession of cocaine. Appellant asserted error in that there was no cause of the customs agent to have searched the package, and that his plea was not entered into knowingly and intelligently.


OVERVIEW: Appellant had drugs imported to him and the package was intercepted by authorities. Appellant pled guilty to possession of cocaine and was put on probation. Appellant sought review and claimed that an affidavit contained insufficient facts from which a magistrate could have determined the reliability of the information, and that the original search of the parcel, transmitted as first class mail, was illegal because there was nothing to show that the opening agent had a real suspicion that the parcel contained contraband, or that it was opened by an agent authorized to do so. On review, the court found that the customs officers were authorized by law and regulation to open the package in question and determine its contents. The court determined that the information was not illegally acquired and there was no entrapment. Also, neither the filing of the charge nor the ruling of the trial court constituted an improper act which would invalidate the plea to another count. The order admitting appellant to probation was therefore affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order admitting appellant to probation following his guilty plea to possession of cocaine was affirmed because there were no reversible irregularities regarding the search and seizure of the contraband linked to appellant. Also, appellant's plea was found to be entered into by a knowing and intelligent act.

Berkeley v. Alameda County Bd. of Supervisors


Civ. No. 32992


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


40 Cal. App. 3d 961; 115 Cal. Rptr. 540; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 920



July 26, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 15, 1974, and on August 7, 1974, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 2, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 427871, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is modified by striking therefrom the words "and that the Complaint be, and the same is hereby, dismissed" and inserting in lieu thereof "and that judgment on the complaint is rendered in favor of defendants and against the plaintiffs." The remainder of the order is affirmed. Respondents to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant recipients challenged an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied their petition for a writ of mandate and dismissed their complaint for declaratory relief regarding the computation of grants of unrelated general assistance recipients.


OVERVIEW: Appellant recipients received general assistance from respondent county. Respondent calculated the amount of appellants' grants based upon a three-person household, although one of the recipients was unrelated to appellants. Appellants sought declaratory relief through a writ of mandate precluding respondent from computing the grants based upon unrelated recipients and required respondent to calculate the grants based upon need. The trial court denied appellants' writ and dismissed their complaint. Appellants challenged the judgment contending respondent's calculation scheme denied appellants of their due process rights by making improper presumptions, the ordinance discriminated against a class of recipients living together, as well as violated appellants' right to free association. The court affirmed the trial court's determination, however, modified the judgment, holding in respondent's favor. The court held the trial court improperly dismissed appellants' action without first deciding the merits of the case. The court reasoned the applicable ordinance simply set forth a classification scheme, was not discriminatory and did not deprive appellants of any of their rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and modified the trial court's order, struck the portion of the order dismissing appellant recipients' declaratory action and found in respondent county's favor. The court held that the ordinance for calculating the grants was not based upon presumptions rather constituted a classification scheme, which was not based upon need. Further, the court held none of appellants' rights were violated.

De Canas v. Bica


Civ. No. 42739


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


40 Cal. App. 3d 976; 115 Cal. Rptr. 444; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 921; 10 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P10,243



July 26, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 24, 1974. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. SM 11789, Marion A. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant farm workers sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) that dismissed their suit on a demurrer by respondent farm labor contractors. Appellants sued alleging that they were discharged and not rehired for farm work because respondents' labor supply consisted of illegal aliens that were employed in lieu of lawful residents. Appellants relied on Cal. Lab. Code § 2805.


OVERVIEW: Appellant farm workers sued respondent farm labor contractors after they were discharged from employment and were not rehired. Appellants contended that respondents used illegal aliens as labor in lieu of lawful residents of the United States. The underpinning of appellants' suit was Cal. Lab. Code § 2805 (§ 2805), which imposed criminal penalties for the employment of illegal aliens where it had an adverse effect on lawful residents. Thus, appellants sought to enjoin respondents from unfair competition. The trial court sustained respondents' demurrer to appellants' complaint on the basis that § 2805 was unconstitutional. Appellants sought review. The court affirmed. The court wrote that Congress had enacted a broad scheme for regulating immigration and naturalization, which included regulation of alien employment. 8 U.S.C. S. § 1101 et seq. The court found that § 2805 conflicted with the national law and policy even though, at the time, Congress had not imposed on private employers the burden of determining, in terms of the immigration laws, the status of prospective employees.


OUTCOME: Ruling in favor of respondent farm labor contractors, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that the statute relied on by appellant farm workers, which made it a crime to employ illegal aliens where it had an adverse effect on lawful residents, was unconstitutional. The court held that the statute impermissibly conflicted with national law and policy.

 

692 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Carrow


Crim. No. 11813


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


40 Cal. App. 3d 924; 115 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 917



July 25, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The order adjudging petitioner guilty of contempt of court is annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney filed a petition for habeas corpus relief from the trial court (California), seeking annulment of two incidents of charged contempt, for each of which he was fined and sentenced to five days in the county jail, with the sentences to run consecutively. Petitioner contended that his statement, which did not obstruct the administration of justice, did not rise to the level allowing for contempt.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney was representing a defendant in nationally publicized murder trial. The trial was long and exhausting for all parties concerned. Petitioner was first found in contempt by the lower court after he stated, Your Honor, I submit this trial is becoming a joke. The court held that petitioner's remarks, although ill-chosen, did not obstruct the administration of justice during the trial and did not insult and directly impugn the integrity of the lower court. Furthermore, the court found that the lower court's order failed to demonstrate with sufficient particularity that petitioner's conduct constituted legal contempt. In addition, the court found that petitioner's comments, referring to penalty during closing argument, were insufficient to find petitioner guilty of contempt where the lower court failed to specifically find that petitioner understood that any reference to the greatest possible punishment had been proscribed by the lower court's ruling. Therefore, in the absence of clear evidence and precise findings of affront to the lower court, the court annulled the order adjudging petitioner guilty of contempt of court.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the order adjudging petitioner attorney guilty of contempt of court, holding that petitioner's statements, although ill-chosen, did not obstruct the administration of justice during the trial and did not insult and directly impugn the integrity of the lower court. The court held that there was an absence of evidence to show that petitioner should be held guilty of contempt.

 

693 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ruster


Crim. No. 7279


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


40 Cal. App. 3d 865; 115 Cal. Rptr. 572; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 911



July 22, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 4091, James C. McDermott, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the revocation of his probation in the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), contending that the court had lost jurisdiction under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.2a, by failing to seasonably act on his demand for hearing, and that an eight-month delay in revocation proceedings deprived him of due process of law.


OVERVIEW: While defendant was on probation for three years for an earlier conviction, he was arrested and sentenced to prison for grand theft and forgery. Before defendant's second conviction, his probation officer had filed a request for revocation and the court had issued a warrant to show cause. While in prison, defendant directed a letter to the probation department in which he recited the subsequent convictions and sentence and, referring to the warrant lodged against him for probation violation, demanded a court hearing within 60 days or dismissal. The court held a hearing and revoked defendant's probation and defendant appealed, contending the trial court lost jurisdiction to impose his sentence and denied him due process by the delay in scheduling the hearing. The court affirmed and held that the defendant had not complied with Cal. Penal Code § 1203.2a because his letter was neither directed to, nor delivered to, the district attorney, nor was it signed in the presence of the warden and attested. The trial court's hearing schedule had met due process standards and no evidence of prejudice was presented by the defendant that would have warranted reversal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the revocation of defendant's probation because he had not complied with the strict requirements under the statute for demanding a sentencing hearing. The record had not supported an inference that the delay was purposeful or oppressive or deliberately obstructive. Defendant had presented no evidence to refute or mitigate the charge, nor did he claim his ability to defend had been impaired by delay.

 

694 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Silva v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 34674


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


40 Cal. App. 3d 733; 115 Cal. Rptr. 479; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 900



July 19, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 25, 1974. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied, and the heretofore issued alternative writ is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was charged by respondent Municipal Court for the Oakland-Piedmont Judicial District of Alameda County (California), with soliciting another to engage in lewd and dissolute conduct. The court issued an alternative writ of mandate that directed respondent to sustain petitioner's demurrer or, in the alternative, to show cause why it had not done so. Respondent chose the alternative, and presented questions for consideration.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged in respondent trial court with soliciting another to engage in lewd and dissolute conduct. The court issued an alternative writ of mandate directing respondent to have sustained petitioner's demurrer to the complaint or to show cause why it had not done so. The trial court chose the latter. Three questions were presented for consideration. Were the words lewd or dissolute conduct unconstitutionally vague? Had Cal. Penal Code § 647(a) contravened the U.S. Const. amend. I guarantee of freedom of speech? If constitutional fault was absent, what meaning was to be given to the words lewd or dissolute conduct in order that it could be determined what was lawful? There was no authority that held that the terms obscene and obscenity, as used in criminal statutes, were unconstitutionally vague or uncertain. U.S. Const. amend. I did not prevent the state from declaring to it to be criminal to commit an obscene act. The words lewd or dissolute conduct were found to have been that sort of sexual conduct which was grossly repugnant and patently offensive to generally accepted notions of what was appropriate and decent according to community standards.


OUTCOME: The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate was denied, and the issued alternative writ was discharged. The court found neither constitutional nor other fault with the statute making obscene conduct criminal. Therefore, respondent municipal court properly overruled petitioner's demurrer to the charge of soliciting another to engage in lewd and dissolute conduct.

 

695 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kipp v. Cozens


Civ. No. 32492


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


40 Cal. App. 3d 709; 115 Cal. Rptr. 423; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 898; 14 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 1453



July 18, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 12, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. P 22038, John M. Brenner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying preliminary injunction (against sale and registration of the vehicle) and declaring Commercial Code section 9503, Civil Code section 2982, subdivision (a)10(4), and Vehicle Code sections 5601 and 5909 to be constitutional, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), denying her injunction and writ of mandate as to the constitutionality of statutes which recognized the right of self-help repossession of motor vehicles, specifically Cal. Com. Code § 9503, Cal. Civ. Code § 2982(a) 10(4), and Cal. Veh. Code §§ 5601 and 5909.


OVERVIEW: At the time plaintiff's car was repossessed, two installments were due, and she would have been entitled to the title to the car upon making the payments. Plaintiff challenged the constitutionality of statutes that recognized the right to self-help repossession, including Cal. Com. Code § 9503, Cal. Civ. Code § 2982(a) 10(4), and Cal. Veh. Code §§ 5601 and 5909. The trial court denied relief to plaintiff on the ground that no state action was involved in the repossession. The trial court dissolved a temporary restraining order and respondents were free to sell the vehicle. On appeal, the court held that it was essential to plaintiff's case that the action complained of be considered state action. Plaintiff had to show a level of significant state involvement sufficient to characterize the repossession as state conduct. Upon consideration of the evidence, the court found no state action. Thus, the judgment denying preliminary injunction against sale and registration of the vehicle, and declaring the statutory sections constitutional, was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment denying a preliminary injunction and writ of mandate against sale and registration of a repossessed vehicle, and declaring the relevant statutory sections constitutional, was affirmed because plaintiff failed to show any state action in the repossession of her vehicle.

 

696 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Levinsohn v. City of San Rafael


Civ. No. 34720


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


40 Cal. App. 3d 656; 115 Cal. Rptr. 309; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 893



July 17, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The peremptory writ of mandate as prayed shall issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner residents filed for writ of mandate to compel respondent city to resume proceedings for annexation of an unincorporated area. Petitioners also sought review of Cal. Gov't Code §§ 35121, .1, the statutory authority under which respondent terminated annexation proceedings, to determine their constitutionality.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner residents were registered voters in an unincorporated area outside respondent city. Petitioner initiated procedures for respondent's annexation of the area. Respondent terminated annexation proceedings under authority of Cal. Gov't Code §§ 35121, .1 after landowners owning 50 percent or more of the assessed land value of the area protested the annexation. Petitioners sought review to determine the constitutionality of §§ 35121, .1, and filed a writ of mandate to compel respondent to resume annexation proceedings. The court found that under §§ 35121, .1 landowners owning half or more of the territory could stop annexation proceedings. Applying rationale from a case holding a similar statute unconstitutional, the court found that respondent could offer no compelling interest that would allocate power solely to landowners. Petitioners, as non-landowners, shared an equal interest with landowners in annexation. The court held that §§ 35121, .1 were unconstitutional and that they denied petitioners equal protection under the state and federal constitutions. The writ was issued.


OUTCOME: Petitioner residents' request for issuance of a writ of mandate to compel respondent city to resume annexation proceedings was affirmed. The statutes which allowed landowners owning 50 percent or more of the assessed value of the land proposed for annexation to terminate the proceedings by protesting annexation violated petitioners' equal protection of the law under the state and federal constitutions.

 

697 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Dolores Canning Co. v. Howard


Civ. No. 41729


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


40 Cal. App. 3d 673; 115 Cal. Rptr. 435; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 895



July 17, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-16928, Charles H. Church, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, labor commissioner and department of industrial relations, challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that granted respondent corporate employer of non-resident aliens' motion for summary judgment and held that Cal. Lab. Code § 2805 unconstitutionally encroached on federal law and violated constitutional guarantees of due process.


OVERVIEW: Respondent corporate employers of non-resident aliens filed an action in the trial court seeking a declaration that Cal. Lab. Code § 2805 was unconstitutional and sought to enjoin appellants, labor commissioner and department of labor relations, from enforcing its provision and regulations. The trial court granted summary judgment in respondents' favor, finding that the statute encroached upon the exclusive right of the U.S. Congress to regulate immigration and naturalization, and it failed to provide that degree of certainty required to meet the constitutional guarantees of due process. On review, the court affirmed summary judgment in favor of respondents and held that the statute was unconstitutional for two reasons. First, Congress in enacting the Immigration and Nationality Act of 1952 as amended in 1965, had expressed its judgment to have uniform federal regulations in matters affecting employment of aliens and nonimmigrants, thus barring state action in this field. Second, it encroached upon, and interfered with, a comprehensive regulatory scheme enacted by Congress in the exercise of its exclusive power over immigration.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of respondent corporate employers of non-resident aliens, and held that the statute was unconstitutional because Congress had exclusive power over immigration and had barred state action and had expressed its judgment to have uniform federal regulations in matters affecting employment of aliens and nonimmigrants, in enacting the Immigration and Nationality Act of 1952 as amended in 1965.

 

698 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


California Chiropractic Assn. v. Board of Administration


Civ. No. 14396


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


40 Cal. App. 3d 701; 115 Cal. Rptr. 286; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 897



July 17, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 227294, B. Abbott Goldberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) dismissing plaintiff's action against defendants seeking a declaration that Cal. Ins. Code § 10176.2 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sued defendants seeking a declaration that a provision of a group insurance policy issued to defendants was void on constitutional grounds. The policy provided reimbursement for medical expenses incurred by covered state employees, but excluded reimbursement for chiropractic treatment. Specifically, the policy excluded coverage for physical therapy unless such was provided by a medical doctor. Plaintiff asserted this provision, Cal. Ins. Code § 10176.2, authorized the state to discriminate against the chiropractic profession in the field of physical therapy. The trial court dismissed plaintiff's complaint, holding plaintiff's action did not constitute a cause of action, and plaintiff appealed. The court held § 10176.2 constituted a legislative determination that a limited alternative to the total exclusion of coverage for physical therapy should be permitted in medical insurance policies, and that such was in the public interest to do so. Therefore, the subject matter, where it dealt with economic and social problems, was one wherein courts had to bow to legislative experience and deference to legislative judgment. Thus, the court affirmed judgment for defendants.


OUTCOME: Judgment dismissing plaintiff's suit affirmed where the code section challenged by plaintiff as unconstitutional was not subject to judicial review.

 

699 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


U. S. Hertz, Inc. v. Niobrara Farms


Civ. No. 13662


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


41 Cal. App. 3d 68; 116 Cal. Rptr. 44; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 768



July 15, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tehama County, No. 16098, Stanley C. Young, Jr., Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the motion for a preliminary injunction is affirmed with costs to respondents. The stay of the foreclosure proceedings heretofore issued by this court is vacated and annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff buyer challenged an order of the Superior Court of Tehama County (California), which dissolved a temporary restraining order and denied its motion for a preliminary injunction, in connection with an allegation by plaintiff that it approved and accepted defendant seller's escrow instruction for purchase of agricultural property by reason of duress. Plaintiff sought compensatory damages, alleging acts of fraud by defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff buyer submitted an offer to buy agricultural property from defendant seller. Plaintiff, however, failed to raise the cash payment by the due date and escrow instructions were approved in writing by plaintiff. Defendant recorded notices of default and an election to sell the trust property. Plaintiff filed an action seeking compensatory damages and a permanent injunction, restraining defendant from proceeding with foreclosure of the property. The trial court dissolved plaintiff's temporary restraining order and denied its motion for a preliminary injunction. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's order. The court stated that the original contract between the parties terminated when plaintiff failed to raise the cash payment and that the true agreement was by the escrow. The court held that plaintiff failed to establish its right to injunctive relief because there was no allegation that plaintiff approved the escrow instructions by reason of duress and neither were its rights prejudiced by the one minute differential between the recording of the substitution and the notice of default. The court vacated and annulled the stay of foreclosure proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, denying the motion for a preliminary injunction with costs to defendant seller. The court, stating that the trial court did not abuse its discretion, held that the contract between the parties terminated when plaintiff buyer failed to raise the cash payment by the due date and the facts did not support a claim of duress. The stay of foreclosure proceedings issued was vacated and annulled.

 

700 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re J. T.


Civ. No. 33702


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


40 Cal. App. 3d 633; 115 Cal. Rptr. 553; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 889



July 12, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 87060, Francis W. Mayer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order adjudging the minors to be dependent children of the juvenile court and the order for their physical custody under the supervision of the probation officer are reversed with directions to dismiss the petition seeking a determination that the minors come within the provisions of subdivision (a) of section 600.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600(a), which adjudged appellant's minor children to be dependent children of the juvenile court and ordered their physical custody under the supervision of respondent probation officer.


OVERVIEW: Respondent probation officer filed a petition pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600(a) alleging that appellant mother evidenced immaturity and demonstrated a lifestyle contrary to the welfare and best interests of her minor children to make her inadequate to their care, and that appellant's children were in need of effective parental care and control. The trial court overruled appellant's demurrer, made conclusory findings, and ordered physical custody of the children under the supervision of respondent. On appeal the court reversed with directions to dismiss the petition. Although Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600(a) was not unconstitutionally vague because other definable sources sufficiently delineated the meaning of "effective parental care and control," the petition did not state facts required by Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 656(f) with sufficient particularity to support the allegations, and did not give appellant meaningful notice of the charges against her. Appellant was denied due process of law because the trial court's findings were conclusions of law, not findings of fact that apprised appellant of the court's reasoning and provided a basis for appellate review.


OUTCOME: The judgment finding that appellant mother's minor children were dependent children of the juvenile court and ordering their physical custody under the supervision of a probation officer was reversed with directions to dismiss respondent probation officer's petition. Appellant was not given meaningful notice of the charges against her, and the proceeding denied appellant due process of law.

Tilghman v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 43575


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


40 Cal. App. 3d 599; 115 Cal. Rptr. 195; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 886



July 11, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of the real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 2, 1974.



DISPOSITION: The order is annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of certiorari, asking the court to review an order of respondent court that refused to adjudge petitioner's former husband in contempt for failure to make spousal support payments.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner ex-wife brought an action alleging her ex-husband, real party in interest, was delinquent in his payment of spousal support in accordance with an integrated marital settlement agreement. After a hearing, respondent court refused to hold real party in contempt on the ground that the spousal support obligations in the agreement were contractual and were therefore not enforceable by contempt. Petitioner sought a writ of certiorari for review of respondent's decision. On appeal, the court held that a 1967 amendment to Cal. Civ. Code § 139 made child and spousal support payments law-imposed obligations that could be enforced by contempt proceedings, even though such support was provided for in an integrated agreement. Further, the court found that the constitutional prohibition against imprisonment for debt related to run-of-the-mill debtor-creditor relationships and that alimony, child support, and marital property settlements were not within the constitutional protection. The court noted that the requirement that the court find the obligor had the ability to pay and willfully refused served to protect the debtor. The court therefore annulled respondent court's order.


OUTCOME: The court annulled respondent court's order refusing to hold real party in interest in contempt for non-payment of spousal support because it held that spousal support payments were law-imposed obligations that could be enforced by contempt proceedings.

 

702 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cowsar


Crim. No. 12283


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


40 Cal. App. 3d 578; 115 Cal. Rptr. 160; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 884



July 10, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 50470, William J. McGuiness, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to give credit to appellant for the time he was hospitalized at the Atascadero State Hospital.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) convicting him upon his guilty plea of murder in the second degree after it determined appellant regained his sanity. Appellant contended that the trial court should have given him credit pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5 for the time he spent at the state hospital pursuant to the trial court's finding that he was insane under Cal. Penal Code § 1368.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the trial court convicting him upon his guilty plea of murder in the second degree after it determined that appellant regained his sanity. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in not crediting his sentence with the time he spent at the state hospital pursuant to the trial court's finding that he was insane under Cal. Penal Code § 1368. The court reversed and held that appellant should have received credit for the time he spent in the state hospital pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5. The court held that to distinguish between time served in jail and time served in a hospital pursuant to an insanity finding would be unconstitutional. Respondent offered no rationale for the disparity in treatment between defendants on the basis of the time of their mental illness, an event out of the control of the defendant. The court remanded with directions to give credit to appellant for the time he was hospitalized at the state hospital.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded for resentencing. Appellant should have been given credit for the time he served in a state hospital after the trial court found him insane. Respondent state offered no rationale for the disparity in treatment between a defendant in custody in jail and one in a state hospital. The court remanded with directions to give credit to appellant for the time he was hospitalized at the state hospital.
