People v. Evans


Crim. No. 7244


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


40 Cal. App. 3d 582; 115 Cal. Rptr. 304; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 885



July 10, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 5, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 55900, Jean Morony, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Butte County (California) that denied defendant's request for a jury instruction on self-defense and upheld a jury verdict that found defendant guilty of possession of a firearm by a felon in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was working as a trainee dispatcher for a cab company. A cab driver, who was on duty, but was sleeping on a couch, became irate because he was not given a call. Defendant dispatched the call to another driver. A shouting match ensued and defendant reached into a desk drawer and pulled out a gun. Defendant fired the gun, hitting driver in the cheek. Defendant was charged with assault with a deadly weapon and possession of a firearm by a person convicted of a felony. The trial court denied defendant's request for a jury instruction on self-defense and upheld a jury verdict that found defendant guilty of possession of a firearm. The court affirmed, holding that a felon had the right to defend himself, but he was prevented from the use of firearms. The right to bear arms could be regulated or entirely destroyed by the state pursuant to its police powers, for the public safety.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's possession of a firearm by a felon conviction. The court held that the right to bear arms could be regulated by the state pursuant to its police powers, for the public safety. A felon had the right to defend himself, but he was prevented from the use of firearms.

 

704 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Pearce


Crim. No. 12755


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


40 Cal. App. 3d 399; 115 Cal. Rptr. 222; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 868



July 2, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 31, 1974, and petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 28, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 66840, Joseph G. Wilson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated, the portion of the superior court's order crediting Pearce with the Alabama jail time served by him while resisting extradition to California, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of that portion of an order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California) that credited petitioner on his California prison terms with the time that he had spent in custody in Alabama while resisting extradition to California.


OVERVIEW: California's Adult Authority suspended petitioner's parole from prison. Petitioner was apprehended and placed in jail in Alabama because of his suspended parole. Petitioner successfully resisted extradition before he was returned to California. The Adult Authority allowed petitioner no credit on his terms of imprisonment. On his petition for a writ of habeas corpus, it was ordered that petitioner be credited on his California prison terms with the time that he had spent in custody in Alabama. The people appealed from that portion of the order. The court reversed. Applying Cal. Penal Code § 3064, which provided that from revocation of parole until a return to custody, the individual was deemed a fugitive, the court determined that petitioner was not entitled to credit for time served in Alabama. The language in § 3064, limiting credit for time served after a "return to custody," indicated that a parolee had to have been in California authorities' control before time served may be credited against his California sentence. Despite the fact that petitioner was held in Alabama solely on the California parole suspension, the court ruled that California had no actual control over him.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the order that credited petitioner with Alabama jail time served by him while resisting extradition. Although the Alabama jail time was served only because of the Adult Authority's order suspending his parole, service of that time had to have been deemed incidental to California's unsuccessful efforts to return him to prison. It could not have been deemed his "return to custody" of the California authorities.

 

705 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ruster v. Ruster


Civ. No. 32259


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


40 Cal. App. 3d 379; 114 Cal. Rptr. 812; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 866



July 1, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 641772, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that granted summary judgment in favor of defendant wife in plaintiff's action to recover death benefits payable to the beneficiary of the deceased employee of the State of California.


OVERVIEW: Decedent was an employee of the State of California when he was killed in an automobile accident in November 1971. Decedent had married defendant wife some two months before his death. Before that decedent lived with plaintiff mother for over ten years. In January 1963, decedent had designated his mother as beneficiary of his death benefits payable through his employment. In 1970 Cal. Gov't Code § 21205 was enacted, which automatically revoked a previous beneficiary of the death benefits after a member's marriage or other life event. The decedent had not made a new written designation of a beneficiary to his benefits, and the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of defendant wife, finding that the benefits were payable to her. The court affirmed the judgment. The court held that the benefit became automatically payable to defendant wife under Cal. Gov't Code § 21211. The court held that the automatic revocation of section 21205 did not unconstitutionally impair the parties' contractual rights, because it bore a material relation to the theory of the pension system and its successful operation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant wife and held that defendant, not plaintiff mother, was the appropriate beneficiary of decedent's death benefits. The court held that the automatic beneficiary designation set forth in the statute was constitutional and worked to the advantage of the management of the pension system.

People v. Wells


Crim. No. 11415


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


40 Cal. App. 3d 11; 114 Cal. Rptr. 777; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 844

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 18, 1974, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 21, 1974.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied his motions to quash the indictment against him and convicted defendant for two counts of assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, with several co-defendants, was indicted by the grand jury on two counts of attempted murder and two counts of assault with a deadly weapon upon a police officer. He was found guilty of the two assault counts and acquitted of the counts of attempted murder. Defendant's judgments were reversed with directions to the trial court to take evidence on the motion to quash the indictment before reconsidering the ruling on the motion. 708 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ryser


Crim. No. 7098


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


40 Cal. App. 3d 1; 114 Cal. Rptr. 668; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 843



June 24, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Justice Court for the Tahoe Judicial District of Placer County, No. 11-190, Robert A. Fugazi, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is reversed and the superior court is ordered to grant the petition to seal defendant's record.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Placer County (California) upholding the denial of his application for the sealing of his record of conviction for misdemeanor marijuana possession.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, following his successful completion of probation, appealed a lower court's decision upholding the trial court's refusal to seal his conviction for misdemeanor possession of marijuana. The court reversed, ordering defendant's conviction sealed. In so ordering, the court agreed with defendant's equal protection challenge, wherein it was noted that a minor like defendant convicted of a non-drug misdemeanor was permitted to have the record of that conviction sealed if it was a first offense and probation was successfully completed, but those convicted of drug misdemeanors were not eligible. Although recognizing that the government had a compelling interest in enforcement of its laws, the court found no rational grounds for distinguishing non-violent drug misdemeanants from other offenders. The distinction violated defendant's equal protection rights, with the remedy being to place defendant in the class of persons eligible for sealing. Accordingly, the court ordered defendant's record of conviction sealed.


OUTCOME: The lower court's order denying sealing was reversed, and defendant's record of conviction for misdemeanor possession of marijuana was ordered. The court held that any ruling to the contrary deprived defendant of his equal protection rights. Sealing was therefore required.

 

709 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re R. C.


Civ. No. 33488


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


39 Cal. App. 3d 887; 114 Cal. Rptr. 735; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1018



June 19, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 11, 1974. Rattigan, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 28, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 81112, Francis W. Mayer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile challenged his delinquency adjudication for arson, imposed upon him by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), contending that his adjudication was improper since it was based upon the uncorroborated testimony of two accomplices.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile challenged his delinquency adjudication for arson, contending that his adjudication was improperly obtained since it was based upon the uncorroborated testimony of two accomplices. The court rejected his contention, and affirmed his adjudication. It held that Cal. Penal Code § 1111's requirement of corroborating evidence of an accomplice's testimony did not apply to juvenile proceedings, and that inapplicability did not violate appellant's equal protection rights. Corroboration was not a requirement of due process, but was instead an additional safeguard for adult trials added by the state legislature. Since appellant's delinquency proceedings were fundamentally fair, the fact that he was not granted a special safeguard provided to adults was immaterial. Juvenile defendants also received certain protections that adult defendants did not receive. There was, therefore, no constitutional infirmity in appellant's delinquency adjudication, and it was accordingly affirmed.


OUTCOME: Appellant's delinquency adjudication was affirmed. The mere fact that it was based upon uncorroborated accomplice testimony was of no moment, since there was no constitutional requirement that accomplice testimony be corroborated. The requirement in adult trials of corroboration was based upon a statute, which did not apply to juvenile proceedings.

 

710 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fulk


Crim. No. 11975


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


39 Cal. App. 3d 851; 114 Cal. Rptr. 567; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1014



June 17, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-3289, Gerald E. Ragan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed and the cause is remanded with directions to proceed in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), convicting him of possession of marijuana and with driving under the influence of liquor or the combined influence of liquor and a drug. The district attorney determined that diversion under Cal. Penal Code §§ 1000 - 1000.4 was not applicable, and the trial court placed appellant on probation for two years for the marijuana charge.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged by information with possession of marijuana and with driving under the influence of the combined influence of liquor and a drug. After appellant's motion to suppress was denied and the district attorney determined that appellant was not eligible for diversion under Cal. Penal Code §§ 1000 - 1000.4, appellant pleaded guilty. The trial court accepted the district attorney's opinion that appellant was ineligible to be considered for diversion and sentenced him to probation. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction, holding the trial court properly denied the motion to suppress. The court reversed the sentencing order, holding that appellant was not ineligible for diversion simply because he was also being charged with driving under the influence of the combined influence of liquor and a drug and that he was therefore entitled to resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction, holding the trial court properly denied appellant's motion to suppress. The court reversed the order sentencing appellant, holding that he qualified for diversion and was therefore entitled to the benefits of being considered for diversion, including the investigation and report of the probation department and the exercise of the trial court's discretion.

People v. Rainville


Crim. No. 7312


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


39 Cal. App. 3d 982; 114 Cal. Rptr. 902; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1028



June 17, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 8, 1974, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 14, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 42695, Arthur B. Broaddus, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of probation) 3 is affirmed.



3 On count I (presenting a false claim) defendant was placed on probation for one year and "imposition of judgment and sentence" was accordingly suspended. Conditions of probation were that "defendant be confined to the Sacramento County Jail for a period of three (3) months and . . . comply with all rules and regulations" thereof and "conduct himself in a proper manner" while in confinement. On count II (perjury) he was sentenced to the state prison for the term prescribed by law. It does not appear from the clerk's transcript that there was a pronouncement of judgment. However, execution of the prison sentence was suspended for one year and defendant was placed on probation subject to the conditions that he was to be confined in the county jail for one year and, again, conform to jail rules and regulations and be of good conduct while in confinement. The probationary jail terms were made concurrent. The clerk's minutes and the order of probation do not specify that the term of probation on this count was also one (1) year but it is implied that the term of probation is coterminous with the suspension of execution of sentence.

These orders were made on August 22, 1973. Probation will expire within the next six weeks. In the remote event of a revocation of probation, if defendant is returned to court for further proceedings, a problem of double punishment would arise. At argument, the Attorney General conceded that there was a single criminal objective in defendant's course of conduct. In these circumstances, although both sections 72 and 118 of the Penal Code were violated by that conduct, defendant may be punished for only one violation. ( People v. Beamon (1973) 8 Cal.3d 625, 637 [105 Cal.Rptr. 681, 504 P.2d 905].) The Beamon guidelines (pp. 637-640) must be observed in the event of any future sentencing proceedings.

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of probation entered by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), after a jury returned a verdict finding him guilty of presenting a fraudulent claim in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 72 and of perjury, a violation of Cal Penal code § 118.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of presenting a fraudulent claim in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 72 and of perjury, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 118 in connection with the filing of a claim for the California homeowner's property tax exemption for his residence. Defendant signed a statement to the effect that no one with whom he shared ownership of the home had received governmental assistance over the prior 12 months. In fact, defendant's wife had been receiving assistance. At trial, the issue was whether defendant knew that his wife had an ownership interest in the property; defendant claimed he lacked such knowledge. After the jury returned a verdict of guilty, defendant appealed. The court ruled that the substance of a special instruction that defendant maintained should have been given to the jury by the court, sua sponte, was adequately covered in other instructions. Further, the court held that defendant was not a party who could challenge the constitutionality of the homeowner's tax exemption statute, because he was charged under a general statute, § 72, and was thus precluded from attacking the validity of the statute through which he had sought benefit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's order of probation, because the trial court had not erred in failing to give a special instruction to the jury, the defendant could not attack the constitutionality of the homeowner's property tax exemption statute, and the judgment was otherwise proper.

Orange County Employees' Assn. v. Board of Administration


Civ. No. 12831


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


39 Cal. App. 3d 825; 114 Cal. Rptr. 528; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1012



June 14, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 203606, Walter W. Charamza, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city employee challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied his peremptory writ of mandate to order respondent agency to pay him a pension.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city employee worked as a custodian for 4 years, 5 months and 12 days before he was terminated. During his employment he contributed to the pension plan managed by respondent agency. Upon termination appellant requested retirement benefits. Respondent denied appellant's request on the grounds that he did not meet the new minimum five year service requirement of Cal. Gov't Code § 20393 (1971). The trial court denied appellant's petition for writ of mandate, and he sought review. The reviewing court reversed the decision holding that appellant's discharge, which resulted in his inability to fulfill the five-year service requirement, could not destroy his right to benefits. Appellant was eligible for retirement before the change in eligibility requirements was enacted, and the change could not eliminate that existing right and deprive him of his pension.


OUTCOME: The reviewing court reversed the trial court's denial of appellant city employee's request for a writ of mandate, and required respondent agency to provide pension benefits to appellant. Appellant was eligible for pension benefits before the eligibility requirements were statutorily amended, and could not be deprived of his existing right.

 

713 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re O'Connor


Crim. No. 7465


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


39 Cal. App. 3d 972; 114 Cal. Rptr. 883; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1027



June 13, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 2, 1974, and petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 7, 1974. Wright, C. J., and Tobriner, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a writ of habeas corpus requesting that respondent, the Director of Corrections for the State of California, return him to parole status following the revocation of his parole.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus against respondent director of corrections, alleging that his constitutional rights were violated during the course of his prerevocation and revocation parole hearings. His petition alleged, inter alia, that both his prerevocation and revocation hearings were not noticed and conducted within a reasonable period of time, and that he was unconstitutionally denied counsel at his prerevocation hearing. The court ruled that because petitioner's prerevocation hearing was noticed and held within two months of his being taken into custody, it met the test of reasonableness. As for the revocation hearing, the court held that it was incumbent on petitioner parolee to demonstrate that the timing was unreasonable or that he was prejudiced by the delay. Because the decision as to the need for counsel in cases where a request for counsel was made at a prerevocation or revocation hearing was within the sound discretion of the authorities who administered the parole system, there was no requirement that petitioner be appointed counsel, even where he withdrew his waiver of counsel the same day. The writ was denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's writ of habeas corpus, because none of petitioner's contentions were found to have merit.

 

714 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Gough v. Finale


Civ. No. 32615


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


39 Cal. App. 3d 777; 114 Cal. Rptr. 562; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1010



June 12, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 128902, Norman A. Gregg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bankruptcy trustee sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent debtors and creditors in appellant's action seeking declaratory judgment, permanent injunction, and other relief.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, as trustee in bankruptcy, initiated an action against respondent debtors and creditors seeking a declaratory judgment, permanent injunction, and other relief. Appellant challenged the validity of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24074, which attempted to grant priority rights to a certain class of private creditors of a license holder. The court held that the statute was invalid insofar as it conflicted with 11 U.S.C.S. § 104. Further, as to the claimed security interest of a respondent who sold his liquor store and liquor license to the debtors, respondent maintained that 11 U.S.C.S. § 11(a)(21), divested the trustee from the powers which he was attempting to assert because respondent was appointed escrow holder some ten months prior to the sellers' filing in bankruptcy. The court held that 11 U.S.C.S. § 11(a)(21) did not pertain, because respondent did not fall within the scope of its provisions. The court thus reversed the superior court's judgment as to the grant of summary judgment in favor of respondents.


OUTCOME: The trial court reversed the judgment of the superior court granting summary judgment against appellant trustee in bankruptcy for the reason that a state statute regulating liquor license transfers was unconstitutional insofar as it conflicted with federal bankruptcy law. The court also held that certain provisions of federal bankruptcy law did not operate to divest appellant trustee in bankruptcy of the powers which it was attempting to assert.

People v. Turner


Crim. No. 12427


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


39 Cal. App. 3d 682; 114 Cal. Rptr. 372; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1001



June 5, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 31, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 56064, Albert F. DeMarco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In order to determine whether the trial court unjustifiably denied the petitioner's application for bail we have examined the record in connection with such application. It consists of a short statement by the petitioner's attorney. 1 On that record we cannot say that the court abused its discretion in denying bail. Petitioner's application for bail before this court is accompanied by his attorney's declaration which, insofar as the petitioner's background is concerned, would appear to be in part hearsay. Since there is no showing that these matters were presented to the trial court in any form it is unnecessary to review their sufficiency. The application for bail is denied.



1 "We would ask for reasonable bail pending appeal. I would state that Mr. Turner when he was out of custody has made numerous court appearances always on time. Never, to my knowledge, had a failure to appear. Her remained throughout the trial proceedings. He was always present. It would be my impression from that knowledge that Mr. Turner would be present and would remain himself, in the area and available for any further processes of the court pending appeal. And I would ask the Court to grant reasonable bail pending that appeal."

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned the court for admission to bail on appeal after an order by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) denied his application after he appealed his conviction of rape with two prior convictions of forgery and receiving stolen property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of rape by force or violence under Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), with prior convictions for forgery under Cal. Penal Code §470 and receiving stolen property under Cal. Penal Code § 496. Defendant appealed from the judgment sentencing him to concurrent sentences in state prison. Defendant filed an application with the lower court for bail on appeal, and the lower court denied his application. Defendant then petitioned the court for admission to bail on appeal, and the court denied his petition, finding that unlike pre-conviction bail, admission to bail on appeal was a matter of discretion. The court found that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in denying bail to defendant because it did not unjustifiably deny defendant's application since the only evidence supporting the application was hearsay testimony by defendant's attorney. The court found that Cal. Const. art. I, § 6, conferring an absolute right to bail, applied only to pre-conviction bail.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's application for bail on appeal. The court found that the lower court did not unjustifiably deny defendant's application for bail because bail after conviction was a matter of discretion, which the lower court did not abuse.

Alco Plating Corp. v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 42505


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


39 Cal. App. 3d 948; 114 Cal. Rptr. 506; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1023



June 3, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 31, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 25711, Ben Koenig, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant metal platers challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which rendered a declaratory judgment in favor of respondent city in appellants' action seeking a declaration that they were to be taxed under Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 21.166, not Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 21.190.


OVERVIEW: Appellant metal platers filed a declaratory judgment action against respondent city, seeking a declaration that appellants were entitled to be taxed under Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 21.166, applying to wholesalers, instead of under Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 21.190, applying to independent contractors in a trade. The lower court granted judgment for respondent, and, on appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that appellants were not wholesalers any more than painters would be because appellants' work was predominately a service function and the furnishing of materials was incidental to its application. The court found that the taxpayer classification was not unconstitutional because there was a reasonable basis for distinguishing between, and classifying in different classes, businesses which were primarily engaged in manufacturing and selling or selling tangible personal property at wholesale on the one hand, and businesses which were primarily engaged in supplying a service on the other hand.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, finding that because appellant metal platers were in a service industry, the furnishing of the material to their customers was merely incidental to its application. Thus, the court found that respondent city had properly taxed appellants under the statute applying to those in any trade as an independent contractor, instead of under the statute applying to wholesalers.

Ascherman v. San Francisco Medical Society


Civ. No. 30453


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


39 Cal. App. 3d 623; 114 Cal. Rptr. 681; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 998



May 31, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 528678, Byron Arnold, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments in favor of the defendants American Society of Internal Medicine, Watts, Whitehall, Direct Wire Telephone Service Co., Novitsky, Webb and American Mutual Liability Insurance Company are affirmed and said defendants shall recover their costs on appeal from plaintiff. The judgments are reversed with respect to the defendants San Francisco Medical Society, Franklin Hospital, French Hospital, Hahnemann Hospital, St. Joseph's Hospital, Butler, Groper, Howard, Jones, Lindner and Monardo and said defendants shall pay one-half of the costs incurred by plaintiff on his appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff physician appealed judgments in favor of defendants, medical societies, hospitals, telephone service, and employees and management of those business entities, entered by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), in a case involving alleged arbitrary denial of hospital staff appointments.


OVERVIEW: The staff membership of plaintiff physician at various hospitals was removed after plaintiff allegedly insulted a local member of a national medical society. Plaintiff sued the local medical society, the hospitals that removed his membership, various doctors, and other medical-related service providers, alleging that defendants joined together to tortiously interfere with his right to practice medicine. After deadlocked jury was discharged, the lower court entered directed verdict for defendants on several counts pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 630. On appeal, the court found the lower court had erred in failing to advise the jury that an existing staff member was entitled to have his dismissal considered as required by hospital bylaws, and to a hearing even though the bylaws did not so provide. The power of a nonprofit hospital to pass on staff membership issues was a fiduciary power exercised for the public good, and a physician applying for membership was entitled to minimal due process. Failure of the lower court to instruct on plaintiff's right to a hearing, and the duty of the hospitals to follow their duly instituted procedures, was prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The judgments were reversed with respect to the hospitals, medical society, and certain doctors because failure of the court to instruct on plaintiff's right to a hearing, and the duty of the hospitals to follow their duly instituted procedures, was prejudicial to plaintiff. The court affirmed the judgments in favor of certain defendants who could recover their costs on appeal from plaintiff.

Western Contracting Corp. v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 42479


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


39 Cal. App. 3d 341; 114 Cal. Rptr. 227; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 970



May 22, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. 927659, 986775, Lester E. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in No. 927659 is modified by striking therefrom paragraph 10 declaring the release given by Western does not bar its claim for additional compensation under the contract and, as so modified, is affirmed. The judgment in No. 986775 is affirmed. Respondent State Board of Equalization only is to recover its costs on these appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractor challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied it declaratory relief, in favor of defendant state board of equalization, on the ground that exemption to increase in state sales and use tax under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6376 did not apply, and for defendant department of water resources that the contract did not allow additional compensation for an increased tax burden.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contractor was given a contract to construct a dam. The California legislature then increased the sales and use tax by one percent and provided an exemption under Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 6376 as applied to materials and fixtures. In two separate actions that were consolidated, plaintiff sought declaratory relief. As between plaintiff and defendant state board of equalization, the court held that the trial court's interpretation that the language and legislative history supported the interpretation of defendant board that § 6376 did not apply to equipment, tires, fuel, and other supplies consumed because the terms materials and fixtures had a set meaning in law. As between plaintiff and defendant department of water resources the court held the contract did not provide additional compensation because of the increased tax burden. The court held that plaintiff was not subjected to any unconstitutional impairment of its contract with defendant department because of the increased tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision, holding that contract to construct a dam did not provide for additional compensation of plaintiff contractor as a result of increased tax burden that was created by an increase in the state sales and use tax because new tax law applied to materials and fixtures and not equipment, fuel, and other material, as defined by a past court decision.

721 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Oaxaca


Crim. No. 24517


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


39 Cal. App. 3d 153; 114 Cal. Rptr. 178; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 955



May 15, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, Nos. 31417584, 31425095, Clarence A. Stromwall, Court Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his sentence imposed by the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), on the ground that the commissioner who sentenced defendant lacked authority to do so.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for probation violation, pleaded guilty, and was sentenced by a commissioner acting as a temporary judge. Upon receiving a prison sentence, defendant argued that the commissioner lacked authority to decide the matter, absent a court order with an official determination authorizing the commissioner to hear and determine the case. The court affirmed the commissioner's ruling, holding that under the facts of this case, the commissioner had the jurisdictional power to accept the plea and impose sentence, pursuant to a de facto stipulation that the commissioner could act as a temporary judge. Defendant was represented by an experienced public defender, participated in the plea bargaining, and accepted the sentence which was part of that plea bargain, without objecting to the commissioner's participation. Further, each of the three times defendant had appeared before the commissioner, he was given a copy of the terms and conditions of his probation, and indicated he had read, understood and agreed to comply with them. Thus, the court found that plaintiff waived any grounds of disqualification.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence imposed by a commissioner acting as a temporary judge, holding that the commissioner had de facto authority to rule in the matter under facts establishing that defendant had appeared two prior times before the commissioner without complaint, was represented by counsel, participated in the plea bargaining, and understood the penalties for violation of his probation.

San Joaquin County Employees' Assn., Inc. v. County of San Joaquin


Civ. No. 13978


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


39 Cal. App. 3d 83; 113 Cal. Rptr. 912; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 948; 86 L.R.R.M. 2942



May 10, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 108697, William Biddick, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged a decision of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which granted a judgment on the pleadings in favor of respondent employees' association and held that appellant was required to meet and confer with respondent on the issue of retroactive pay raises pursuant to the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3500 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Respondent employees' association filed a complaint for declaratory relief against appellant county that sought a determination of whether appellant was required to meet and confer in good faith with respondent regarding retroactive pay raises. Both parties filed for a judgment on the pleadings. The trial court ruled in favor of respondent. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed the trial court and held that appellant was permitted to grant retroactive pay raises and was required to meet and confer with respondent in good faith. The court held that the provisions of the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3500 et seq., permitted flexibility in employee-governmental relations and that the element of retroactivity was a necessary aspect of the relations. Retroactive pay was not unconstitutional per se and was not in excess of the Board of Supervisor's authority under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 1. The court held that retroactive pay raises were not a constitutionally prohibited gift of public money for which services were already rendered and paid. The court held that appellant had a duty to meet and confer in good faith with respondent on the issue of retroactive pay raises.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court that granted a judgment on the pleadings in favor of respondent employees' association and held that appellant county was required to meet and confer with respondent on the issue of retroactive pay raises under the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act. The court held that retroactive pay raises were a necessary aspect of employee-governmental relations and were not constitutionally prohibited.

 

723 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Sherman M.


Civ. No. 42198


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


39 Cal. App. 3d 40; 113 Cal. Rptr. 847; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 943



May 8, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 3, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A7795, Jack T. Ryburn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged a ruling of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent county's petition, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 232(c), that her four-year-old son be freed from appellant's custody and control that he might be adopted. The foster family with whom the child had been living sought to adopt him.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother's child had lived with her for one month after his birth; her addiction to alcohol prevented her from adequately caring for her child, and he consequently became a dependent of the juvenile court under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600(a), because he had no parent capable of properly caring for him. His foster parents with whom he had lived since infancy filed a petition for adoption; the trial court ordered that the child be freed from appellant's custody and control so that he might be adopted pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232(c). Appellant sought review and alleged that § 232(c)'s term of "habitually intemperate" was vague and therefore void, and therefore, the statute could not be made constitutionally definite by judicial construction. The court affirmed the lower court's ruling and held that the term "habitually intemperate" was not so vague as to deprive appellant due process of law because the separate words were simple words with simple meanings and the controlling statute's purpose was to protect children.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling that appellant mother's son should be freed from her custody and control so that he might be adopted and rejected her plea that the term "habitually intemperate" was unconstitutionally vague and therefore void. The court held that the separate words were simple and had simple meaning and that the underlying statute's purpose was to protect children.

People v. Peck


Crim. No. 5463


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


38 Cal. App. 3d 993; 113 Cal. Rptr. 806; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1113



May 2, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 10, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-27515, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained pursuant to a telephonic warrant and convicted defendant pursuant to a guilty plea for possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §11530, alleging issuance of the warrant violated Cal. Penal Code §§ 1525- 1526 and that §§ 1525-1526 were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's residence was searched pursuant to telephonic warrant granted during a three-way conversation with an officer, the prosecutor, and a municipal court judge, during which the officer made oral statements to the judge and the judge authorized the officer to sign the judge's name on the telephonic warrant. The officer issued a warrant on which he printed the judge's name but signed his own name. The officer's statement was transcribed thereafter. The search yielded marijuana and other contraband. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress the evidence and defendant was convicted for possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §11530. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction and held the telephonic search warrant was proper under Cal. Penal Code §§ 1525- 1526 because there was no requirement that the officer's statement be transcribed before the warrant was issued; the statutes were not unconstitutional because neither the U.S. Const. amend. IV nor Cal. Const. art. I, § 19, required that presentation of an affidavit or a declaration precede issuance of a warrant; and a telephonic warrant did not require special or unusual facts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the motion to suppress and the conviction for possession of marijuana and held the issuance of the telephonic warrant was proper because transcription of the officer's statements was not a condition precedent to issuance of the warrant; and the applicable statutes were not unconstitutional because neither the Fourth Amendment nor the state constitution required a declaration to precede issuance of a warrant.

 

725 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Mitchell v. Nat'l Auto. & Casualty Ins. Co.


Civ. No. 31931


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


38 Cal. App. 3d 599; 113 Cal. Rptr. 391; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1079



April 15, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 13, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 637-808, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed judgment from Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) dismissing his complaint to recover bail bond fees after the court sustained demurrers to the complaint with leave to amend.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed a complaint to recover bail bond penalty assessments charged by defendant insurers under Cal. Penal Code § 13521. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrers with leave to amend, and upon plaintiff's failure to amend within the time allowed, the court entered a judgment of dismissal in favor of defendants. On appeal, plaintiff argued the penalty assessment was an illegal and unconstitutional overcharge in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 6, which prohibited excessive bail. The appellate court found that defendants had charged the fees under a statute that was constitutional at the time the fees were assessed. The fact that the statute was subsequently found unconstitutional did not create a basis for plaintiff's claim. Defendants' reliance upon the statute was in accord with the understanding of the law prevailing at the time, and that understanding controlled the rights of the parties. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed judgment where defendants' reliance upon statute was in accord with the understanding of the law prevailing at the time and, therefore, subsequent change in construction of the statute was given prospective rather than retrospective effect.

McKie v. County of Ventura

Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, mobile home owners and a nonprofit corporation, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), that held that a local zoning ordinance was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, mobile home owners and a nonprofit corporation, brought an action against respondents, Ventura County and others, that challenged the constitutionality of a local ordinance that restricted the use and location of mobile homes. The lower court held that the ordinance was constitutional. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment because the ordinance at issue amounted to a classical exercise of the zoning power, or the allocation of certain uses and certain kinds of construction to specified areas within the county. The court found that respondents had not acted in an unconstitutional manner when it required that different types of housing be maintained in separate zones.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court that held that a local zoning ordinance was constitutional was affirmed because the ordinance was a proper exercise of respondents', Ventura County and others, zoning authority.

 

727 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Johnson


Crim. No. 5175


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


38 Cal. App. 3d 228; 113 Cal. Rptr. 303; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1049



March 28, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 15, 1974, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 26, 1974. McComb, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-23219, Gilbert Harelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction, after a jury trial in the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187.


OVERVIEW: In defendant's trial for first degree murder, the trial court denied his motion to discover the identity of the state's forensic evidence experts. The court reversed and held that the trial court denied him information that would have been valuable to him in cross-examining and impeaching the state's witnesses and arguing that the state had not proven its case beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that defendant could be prosecuted again for first degree murder, but he could not receive the death penalty on retrial because he did not receive it in the first trial. The court also held that jurors who would never impose the death penalty could not be excluded from the guilt phase of the trial on retrial. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to quash the indictment, which he made on the grounds that the grand jury selection process excluded certain sections of society and thereby deprived him of equal protection and due process. At the time defendant offered the motion, he was required to show standing in order to assert a claim of class exclusion from grand jury service and he could not show that he was a member of the allegedly excluded groups.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction of first degree murder because the trial court erred in denying his motion to discover the identities of the state's experts. The trial court's refusal to allow the discovery denied him valuable information that he could have used to cross-examine, impeach the expert who testified against him, and argue more vigorously the state's failure to prove his guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.

Paule v. State Pers. Bd.


Civ. No. 42212


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


38 Cal. App. 3d 32; 113 Cal. Rptr. 38; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1034



March 21, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 16, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 35361, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a peremptory writ of mandate. Appellant sought an injunction restraining respondent State Personnel Board from filling a position until appellant and persons similarly situated were permitted to take the examination.


OVERVIEW: Appellant applicant was denied a peremptory writ of mandate by the trial court and appellant sought an injunction from the court restraining respondent State Personnel Board from filling position of Staff Counsel III until appellant and persons similarly situated were permitted to take examination. Respondent had opened position, but had limited the position to eligible members of resources department, and appellant was in different department. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court holding that pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code §§ 18950 and 18951, appellant did not have a constitutional right to take the promotional examination and that due to the absence of a showing that appellant's constitutional rights were infringed, appellant failed to overcome the presumption that the agency had regularly performed its duty. The court also held that respondent had exercised its statutory duty of discretion by providing for promotional examinations within departments to the exclusion of others. The court stated that there was nothing to indicate that the discretion exercised by respondent was arbitrary, capricious, or lacking in evidentiary support.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court holding that the trial court did not abuse its discretion and that appellant applicant did not have a constitutional right to an open promotional examination. The court stated that absent a showing that respondent State Personnel Board was infringing upon a constitutional right of appellant, there was no requirement that respondent justify its actions.

People v. Johnson


Crim. No. 7033


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


38 Cal. App. 3d 1; 112 Cal. Rptr. 834; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1032



March 20, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 10, 1974, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied May 22, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 24561, Norman C. Sullivan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Both judgments are modified to provide that the sentences shall be concurrent with each other and concurrent with any incompleted sentences. Both judgments are further modified by striking the section 12022.5 declaration from each of the separate sentences imposed upon the defendants and by adding at the end thereof: "Defendant was charged and found to have used a firearm in the commission of the foregoing offenses within the meaning of Penal Code section 12022.5, to wit, a hand gun." As so modified, both judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant and codefendant sought the review of the judgments of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which entered jury verdicts convicting defendant and codefendant of murder, robbery, and assault with a deadly weapon.


OVERVIEW: After a jury trial, defendant and codefendant were each convicted of murder, robbery, and assault with a deadly weapon after they robbed a store, which resulted in the murder of the store's owner. Defendant and codefendant were sentenced to life imprisonment, and the jury found that a firearm was used during the commission of the crime. On appeal, defendant and codefendant argued that the lower court erred in not allowing expert testimony on the credibility of the state's witnesses. The court affirmed but modified the convictions holding that the use of expert testimony was not allowed under Cal. Evid. Code § 780 for the impeachment of witness credibility. The court also held that the lower court erred in not declaring that any prior incomplete sentences that defendant and codefendant may now be serving were to be concurrent with their life sentences because Cal. Penal Code § 669 mandated that prior incomplete sentences run concurrent with and be merged into life sentences.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the lower court, which entered jury verdicts convicting defendant and codefendant of murder, robbery, and assault with a deadly weapon, but the court modified the judgments to provide that the sentences shall be concurrent with any incomplete sentences; state law required that all prior convictions of defendant and codefendant be merged into their life sentences for the murder convictions.

People v. Guillen


Crim. No. 23353


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


37 Cal. App. 3d 976; 113 Cal. Rptr. 43; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1191



March 15, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 8, 1974, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 8, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-514617, August J. Goebel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his convictions from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for arson under Cal. Penal Code § 447a and attempted arson under Cal. Penal Code § 451a.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of for arson under Cal. Penal Code § 447a and attempted arson under Cal. Penal Code § 451a. Defendant alleged a denial of a fair trial on procedural matters. The court affirmed the convictions and held that the lower court properly ordered a bifurcated trial in which the introduction of any evidence on the prior conviction would be postponed until after the verdict. The court held that defendant did not have to personally consent to the procedure, because it spared the defendant of a defense, was a trial tactic, and the order bifurcating the trial was no more than one directing the order of proof which was largely within the discretion of the trial judge. The court held that defendant was not denied effective counsel for defendant's cited reason that his present counsel knew too much about him and his record, and the lower court properly denied a motion to substitute counsel on those grounds. Further, defendant was not denied a fundamental due process right to testify on his own behalf based upon his counsel instructing him not to testify, and it was proper to give the Allen charge to the jury with the other jury instructions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions, and held that a bifurcated trial in which the introduction of any evidence on the prior conviction was postponed until after the verdict was proper. Further, defendant did not need to personally consent to the procedure, because it spared defendant a defense, was a trial tactic, and was no more than directing the order of proof, which was largely within the discretion of the trial judge.

 

731 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Goldberg v. Barger


Civ. No. 41808


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


37 Cal. App. 3d 987; 112 Cal. Rptr. 827; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1192



March 15, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 33401, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The denial of a license by respondent Commissioner to petitioner under subdivisions (b) and (d) of section 1668 of the Insurance Code was valid. The judgment of the superior court denying mandamus is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied his writ of mandamus in which he sought to compel respondent insurance commissioner to issue to petitioner a life and disability insurance agent license.


OVERVIEW: Respondent insurance commissioner, after a hearing, denied petitioner's application for a life and disability insurance agent license on the basis that petitioner was not of good business reputation pursuant to the Cal. Ins. Code §1668(d), and that issuance would be against public interest pursuant to Cal. Ins. Code. § 1668(b). Among other allegation of misconduct, petitioner had a pending indictment in another state. Petitioner argued § 1668(d) was unconstitutional because the standard of using an applicant's reputation was not rationally connected to the fitness of the applicant to act as an insurance agent. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court which denied petitioner's writ of mandamus. The court determined that the standard used in § 1668(d) was not one of merely reputation but business reputation. The more definite standard bore a rational relationship to the inquiry of a person's competency to engage in the business of selling insurance. The quality described was ascertainable, and the existence of a good business reputation was a fact capable of being determined.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court which denied petitioner's writ of mandamus because respondent insurance commissioner had a reasonable basis for denying petitioner a license to act as a life and disability agent in the public interest.

Niles Sand & Gravel Co. v. Alameda County Water Dist.


Civ. No. 32099


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


37 Cal. App. 3d 924; 112 Cal. Rptr. 846; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1185



March 14, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 9, 1974, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 8, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 403003, 401131, Lyle E. Cook, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order entered on January 7, 1972, and the judgment entered on March 7, 1972, are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant excavating companies challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which found in favor of respondent county water district in appellants' action for damages related to the flooding of their pits by respondent. Appellants claimed that it constituted a "taking" or "damaging" of their property for a public use, which was compensable by way of inverse condemnation under Cal. Const. art. I, § 14.


OVERVIEW: Appellant excavating companies brought an action against respondent county water district for damages related to the flooding of their sand and gravel pits. Appellants claimed that respondent's actions constituted a "taking" or "damaging" of their property for a public use, which was compensable by way of inverse condemnation under Cal. Const. art. I, § 14. Appellants challenged the trial court's judgment in favor of respondent. The court affirmed the judgment and held that the trial court correctly applied the correlative rights doctrine and correctly used the terms "servitude," to describe the appellants' obligation imposed by the doctrine as landowners, and "public," because a public agency held the right to enforce the servitude. The court held that there was no "taking" or "damaging" of appellants' property and that they were not entitled to inverse condemnation relief. The court also held that Cal. Const. art. XIV, § 3 provided the police power to regulate use of a property right for the public benefit and authorized respondent's actions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for respondent county water district in appellant excavating companies' inverse condemnation action for the flooding of their sand and gravel pits, and held that respondent's activities were carried out in the exercise of its police powers and that the effect upon appellants' property was not a compensable taking under the state constitution.

 

733 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ayer v. Boyle


Civ. No. 30980


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


37 Cal. App. 3d 822; 112 Cal. Rptr. 636; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1175



March 11, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 140207, Joseph A. Branson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed as to respondent Boyle; as to respondent Buscaglia, the judgment is reversed for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff widow appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which entered a nonsuit in her wrongful death action on the basis of the airplane "guest statute," Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 21406.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, aircraft restorer and owner, were restoring an airplane. Prior to licensing, defendant aircraft restorer took plaintiff widow's husband up in the plane, which crashed and killed plaintiff's husband. The trial court granted a nonsuit in plaintiff's wrongful death action based upon the airplane guest statute, Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 21406, which provided that, except for intoxication or willful misconduct, an airman was not liable for injuries to nonpaying guests. The court held that the justifications for the statute, the protection of hospitality and the elimination of collusive lawsuits, were not rational grounds for the differential treatment between paying and nonpaying guests. The statute violated the equal protection guarantees of the state and federal constitutions. The judgment was reversed as to defendant aircraft restorer, because taking up plaintiff's husband in the absence of the required licensing, which violated Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 21411, created a rebuttable presumption of failure to exercise due care. The judgment was affirmed as to defendant owner because there was no evidence that he was aware that defendant aircraft restorer would take a guest up.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part the trial court's judgment, which nonsuited plaintiff widow's wrongful death action against defendants, aircraft restorer and owner, and held that defendant airplane restorer could have been held liable because the airplane guest statute was unconstitutional. The court affirmed as to defendant owner because there was no evidence that he knew that defendant airplane restorer might take a guest up in the unlicensed plane.

 

734 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ogo Associates v. City of Torrance


Civ. No. 42060


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


37 Cal. App. 3d 830; 112 Cal. Rptr. 761; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1176



March 11, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 5254, David N. Eagleson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the cause is remanded to the superior court for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs seller and developer appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied for failure to exhaust administrative remedies their petition for a writ of mandate to compel the defendant city to issue plaintiff developer a permit to build a federally financed 86-unit apartment project for persons with low incomes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant seller sold its property to appellant developer to build low-income apartments financed by the federal government. For the previous eight years, appellants' property had been classified as R3, a zoning that would have allowed construction of the apartments. Appellant developer obtained its financing and applied for a building permit, but before the permit was issued respondent city enacted an emergency ordinance imposing a moratorium on building permits and dwelling-unit. The trial court held that enactment of the moratorium ordinances was proper and that appellants were not entitled to the relief sought because appellant developer had not satisfied all conditions for issuance of a building permit prior to the time respondent enacted its moratorium ordinances and appellants had not exhausted their administrative remedies by applying to respondent for a variance from the rezoning ordinance. The court reversed the lower court and held that the possibility of relief from a general policy did not exist because of the unusual circumstances and thus, appellants were not required to pursue futile administrative remedies in order to invoke the jurisdiction of the courts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court and remanded for a new hearing and findings of fact and conclusions of law on all contested issues because appellant seller and developer were not required to pursue futile administrative remedies in front of respondent city in order to invoke the jurisdiction of the court after respondent issued a moratorium on building permits specifically to prevent appellant developer from obtaining a permit.

San Francisco Street Artists Guild v. Scott


Civ. No. 32355


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


37 Cal. App. 3d 667; 112 Cal. Rptr. 502; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1165



March 1, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 22, 1974. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 637024, Charles S. Peery, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant street artists challenged a judgment in favor of respondent police department and the dissolving of a preliminary injunction by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California). Appellants contended that a city ordinance which forbid unlicensed peddling on the sidewalks violated their rights under U.S. Const. amend. I, and was an unconstitutional grant of unlimited discretion to respondents.


OVERVIEW: Appellant street artists sought declaratory and injunctive relief in the trial court to obtain permits to sell their wares on city streets. Respondent police department exercised its discretion to deny such permits under San Francisco, Cal., Police Code § 869, the Charter of the City and County of San Francisco, Cal., § 3.537, and the Municipal Code of the City of San Francisco, Cal., § 26. The trial court ordered judgment for respondents and dissolved a preliminary injunction. Appellants challenged the ordinances as violating their rights under U.S. Const. amend. I, and as unconstitutional grants of unlimited discretion. The court affirmed. It upheld the trial court's finding that U.S. Const. amend. I rights were not involved. Appellants had no vested right to conduct private business on public streets. The court held that the power delegated to respondents was constitutional, and there was a presumption that they acted properly. Finally, the court noted that declaratory relief was not appropriate for judicial review of administrative actions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. It held that the First Amendment rights of appellant street artists were not violated by city ordinances which granted respondent police department the discretion to deny them peddlers' permits to sell their wares on city streets. It held that street peddling did not involve First Amendment rights, and that respondents had properly exercised the constitutional right to police the streets.

Kornblum v. Newark Unified Sch. Dist.


Civ. No. 31534


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


37 Cal. App. 3d 623; 112 Cal. Rptr. 457; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1160; 8 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 220; 8 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9667



February 28, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 24, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 416417, M. O. Sabraw, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying plaintiff a peremptory writ of mandamus is reversed, and the cause remanded with directions to the trial court to issue its peremptory writ of mandate compelling defendant to reinstate plaintiff as a teacher in its district.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teacher appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), denying her writ of mandamus to compel defendant school board to reinstate her as a teacher following termination of employment for failure to comply with compulsory maternity leave policy.


OVERVIEW: Defendant school board had a policy requiring that pregnant teachers give 90 days notice of the birth date of their child and requiring teachers to stay off work for at least 90 days after the birth of a child. Plaintiff teacher did not give the 90 days notice and was terminated. The trial court denied plaintiff's request for a peremptory writ of mandamus, requesting reinstatement under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5. The court reversed the trial court and remanded with instructions that the trial court issue the writ compelling defendant to reinstate plaintiff. The court held that the 90 day compulsory maternity leave violated plaintiff's due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because the restriction was irrational and arbitrary. The court held that the 90 day notification policy was constitutional because it facilitated administrative planning. Because the court was unable to sever the unconstitutional portion of defendant's policy from the constitutional part, the court held that the plaintiff's due process rights were violated and that she was entitled to reinstatement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the case to the trial court to issue a peremptory writ of mandamus to defendant school board compelling it to reinstate plaintiff teacher. The court held that the unconstitutional compulsory maternity leave was not severable from the constitutional 90 day notice requirement and that because the two policies could not be separated that plaintiff was entitled to the writ.
