C. R. Fedrick v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 40930


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


38 Cal. App. 3d 385; 120 Cal. Rptr. 434; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1061; 20 Cont. Cas. Fed. (CCH) P82,957



February 27, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 20, 1974, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 24, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 921 001, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated hereinabove, the portion of the judgment appealed from is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State Board of Equalization appealed the portions of a judgment entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of plaintiff corporation. Plaintiff, who was a subcontractor furnishing services at a federal air force base in California, sought a refund of sales and use taxes. Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 6384, 6381, 6202, 6094.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation filed a complaint against defendant State Board of Equalization, seeking a refund of sales and use taxes levied in conjunction with its subcontracting work at a federal military base. Defendant appealed those portions of the trial court's judgment which found in favor of plaintiff, and claimed that plaintiff owed taxes under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 6384, 6094 and 6202. The court held that under the Buck Act , 4 U.S.C.S. § 105 et seq., the state had the constitutional power to impose sales and use taxes on contractors for work performed at the federal facilities involved in the case within state statutory limits, with the same effect as though such area was not a federal area, because Congress had expressly consented to such tax and thereby waived federal sovereign immunity. The court reversed the portions of the judgment appealed from and concluded that plaintiff's tangible personal property was used in construction of improvements of real property in the state and was subject to taxation under § 6384, and that the trial court erroneously exempted the property under § 6381 by characterizing it as machinery or equipment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the portions of the judgment appealed from and concluded that the findings of the trial court that certain property of plaintiff corporation was "machinery and equipment" was erroneous. The property should have been characterized as personal property used for improvements of real property, which was taxable under the statutes.

 

738 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Georgia-Pacific Corp. v. County of Butte


Civ. No. 14034


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


37 Cal. App. 3d 461; 112 Cal. Rptr. 327; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1147



February 22, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of Defendant and Appellant for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 17, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 52533, Jean Morony, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed with each party to bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which held that the formula used by defendant in assessing plaintiff corporation's timberlands was illegal and directed that a new hearing be conducted by defendant's Assessment Appeals Board. Plaintiff cross-appealed from the portion of the judgment that ordered a complete rehearing on its application for a reduction of the assessment.


OVERVIEW: When defendant county assessed taxes against plaintiff corporation's timberlands according to the capitalized income approach, instead of the more common comparative sales approach, plaintiff brought an action to recover excess tax payments. Defendant appealed the trial court's judgment that the capitalized income approach formula was illegal, and plaintiff cross-appealed the order for a new hearing by defendant's Assessment Appeals Board. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that defendant's capitalized income tax assessment formula was unconstitutional, because it contemplated not only the economic factor of existing mature trees, but also the value of immature growing timber, which was exempt from taxation under Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 12 3/4. Additionally, the court found that, when the uncontested value of taxable mature trees was added to the value previously determined for the bare land, a double taxation resulted, which was expressly forbidden by Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 102. Further, the court found that a new hearing was required in order to determine a more appropriate and proper method of valuation by defendant to conform to constitutional requirements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the tax assessment formula was illegal and the order for a new hearing, holding that defendant county's capitalized income approach method of appraising plaintiff corporation's timberlands was unconstitutional, and that a new hearing was necessary to determine a more appropriate and proper method of valuation by defendant.

 

739 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Baldwin


Crim. No. 5437


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


37 Cal. App. 3d 385; 112 Cal. Rptr. 290; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1140



February 20, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 17, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-28110, Robert W. Conyers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which dismissed an action charging defendants with a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a, after sustaining a demurrer to the information, without leave to amend, upon the ground that the facts did not constitute a public offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with an act of oral copulation that took place in a restroom, within view of a plain-clothes police officer, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 288a. The trial court dismissed the action after the sustaining of a demurrer to the information, without leave to amend, upon the ground that the facts did not constitute a public offense. On appeal, the court reversed. The court rejected defendants' contentions based upon the nonestablishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I because it was not uncommon that the original source of the ideas expressed was so remote that subject awareness of the source was absent and the opinion expressed seemed to be endemic in the public consciousness, and so it was with legislation that had a religious derivation. The court held that defendants did not have standing to assert the constitutional rights of married couples or consenting adults acting in private because they were neither married nor acting in private. The court held that the practice of oral copulation in a public place was not yet declared to be a fundamental right under U.S. Const. amend. IX.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, which dismissed an action charging defendants with an act of oral copulation in public in violation of the relevant statute because defendants did not have standing to assert the constitutional rights of married couples or consenting adults acting in private and the establishment clause was not violated solely because the legislation had a religious derivation.

 

740 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Freeny


Crim. No. 23302


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


37 Cal. App. 3d 20; 112 Cal. Rptr. 33; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1117



January 30, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 25, 1974, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 17, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A275948, E. Talbot Callister, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of sale of heroin, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352; two counts of possession of heroin for sale, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11351; possession for sale of cocaine; and possession of marijuana, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11357. Appellant contended evidence obtained pursuant to a search warrant should have been suppressed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested as a result of his illegal activities. During the arrest of appellant, heroin was found under the floor mat of his car. The police officers, fearing that evidence at appellant's home would be destroyed, decided to go to his home. When the officers arrived they knocked on the door, and believing the women inside were trying to destroy evidence, the officers gained entry to the home and discovered drugs inside. The officers then stayed in the home until a search warrant was obtained. Once it was obtained they searched appellant's home and discovered contraband. Appellant was charged and convicted. Appellant sought review of the decision alleging that the search was illegal. The court affirmed, holding that the officer's intrusion into appellant's home was no greater than that otherwise permissible, the fact that its purpose was in part to prevent the destruction of evidence while a warrant was obtained did not render it constitutionally defective. The court ruled that under the totality of the circumstances, there was probable cause to arrest appellant. The court held the lower court did not err in denying appellant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of the sale of heroin, two counts of possession of heroin for sale, possession for sale of cocaine, and possession of marijuana, because the police entry and occupancy of his home without a warrant did not invalidate a later search pursuant to a warrant. The court held that the lower court did not err in denying appellant's motion to suppress evidence disclosed in the search.

 

741 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Munoz v. City of San Diego


Civ. No. 12744


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


37 Cal. App. 3d 1; 112 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1115



January 29, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 17, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 334049, Eli H. Levenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff elector appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which dismissed the action by plaintiff against defendant city seeking a declaration that the portion of the city charter that fixed councilmen's salaries was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 21, 24.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff elector sought a declaration that the portion of the city charter which fixed the salary of councilmen at $ 5,000 per annum was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 21, 24. Plaintiff's claim was that the salary had become so inadequate as the result of inflation that it economically discriminated against electors who have families to support and, in effect, imposed a property qualification for holding that public office. The trial court dismissed the action after sustaining defendant city's demurrer without leave to amend. The court affirmed. The court accepted as true plaintiff's allegations that the salary allowed did in fact discriminate against electors who were heads of families. The court stated that it nevertheless had to affirm the judgment of dismissal because the complaint raised no justiciable issue because the power to fix salaries had been reserved to the electorate under Cal. Const. art. I, § 5. The court held that no court had the power to fashion a remedy which would compel the electorate to amend the charter so as to provide for an increase in pay in order to eliminate the alleged economic discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of dismissal in favor of defendant city in plaintiff elector's action to declare the portion of the city charter that fixed councilmen's salaries as unconstitutional due to economic discrimination. The court held that the complaint raised no justiciable issue as to which the court could provide a remedy because the power to fix the salaries had been constitutionally reserved to the electorate.

People ex rel. State Lands Commission v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 33340


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


36 Cal. App. 3d 727; 111 Cal. Rptr. 733; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 716



January 17, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of the real party in interest City of Albany for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 27, 1974.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue ordering respondent court to vacate so much of its order of May 9, 1973, as purports to order the petitioning commission to conduct a rehearing of the matter under review in the action pending in the trial court and to proceed with the trial of that action in accordance with the views expressed herein. The petition insofar as its seeks writs or prohibition and certiorari is dismissed as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the California State Lands Commission sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which, in response to a motion for summary judgment filed by intervenor, a development corporation, denied the motion, found that the commission was not composed as required by law when it revoked land uses, and remanded to the commission for rehearing.


OVERVIEW: The state granted lands to real party in interest the City of Albany, and later amended the legislative grant to allow other uses and to require that the real party in interest substantially improve the lands within ten years, providing for termination of authorization for other uses if petitioner, the California State Lands Commission determined that the city had failed to improve the lands. At a commission meeting, which an assistant of a member attended in his stead, petitioner adopted a resolution finding that the city had failed to substantially improve the land grant as required by 1961 Cal. Stat. 1763, § 1, p. 3767. In its suit with real party in interest against petitioner, intervenor development corporation filed a motion for summary judgment. The trial court found that the commission had not been composed as required by law at the meeting, denied plaintiff intervenor's motion for summary judgment, and remanded the matter to the commission. On appeal, the court granted petitioner's request for a writ, holding that under Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 6105, the quasi-legislative commission's decision was properly adopted by the votes of two commissioners.


OUTCOME: The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate to petitioner, the California State Lands Commission, ordering the lower court to vacate its order insofar as it required petitioner to conduct a rehearing of the land use revocation matter and to proceed with retrial of that matter because respondent's resolution adopted by two members of the commission was legal where the proceedings were quasi-legislative.

City of Orange v. Valenti


Civ. No. 13132


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


37 Cal. App. 3d 240; 112 Cal. Rptr. 379; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 1129



January 17, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 192714, James F. Judge, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court correctly sustained the demurrer without leave to amend in regard to the first, second and third causes of action. The demurrer was also correctly sustained in regard to the fourth and fifth causes of action, but leave to amend should have been granted. The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipality sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), that dismissed appellant's request for an injunction compelling respondents, State of California and lessors, to comply with Orange County, Cal., Municipal Code §§ 9125.1 and 9125.2, Orange County, Cal., Ordinance 23-72 and 24-72, and the California Environmental Quality Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant municipality challenged the dismissal of its request for an injunction ordering respondents, lessors and the State of California, to comply with certain ordinances enacted by appellant in response to the lease between the respondents, Orange County, Cal., Ordinance 23-72 and 24-72, pre-existing parking requirements, Orange County, Cal., Municipal Code §§ 9125.1, 9125.2, and the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq. The court affirmed denial of an injunction for enforcement of the parking requirements and the ordinances, holding that the lease was a sovereign activity that could not be limited by local regulation without consent of the state, which consent could not be inferred by lease terms requiring compliance with local laws, and that enactment of the ordinances was clearly discriminatory. The court reversed as to CEQA, holding that a CEQA environment impact statement was required, despite questions of enforceability of possible recommendations of the report due to sovereign immunity, but remanded for a factual determination of whether events had rendered the requested injunctive relief ineffectual.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant municipality's request for an injunction directing respondents, the State of California and lessors, to comply with local ordinances, as a violation of state sovereignty. The court reversed the dismissal of the request to compel respondents to prepare an environmental impact report required by state law, and remanded for a hearing on whether events had rendered an injunction ineffectual.

Veterans of Foreign Wars v. State Of California


Civ. No. 13851


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


36 Cal. App. 3d 688; 111 Cal. Rptr. 750; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 712



January 11, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 208315, George E. Paras, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, military veterans' organization and others, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied an injunction prohibiting defendants, State of California and state agencies and officials, from further diversion of funds from the Veterans' Farm and Home Building Fund of 1943, Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 988, and a judgment for reimbursement of monies previously taken.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, military veterans' organization and others, had unsuccessfully sought an injunction against defendants, State of California and state agencies and officials, prohibiting further monetary diversions from the Veterans' Farm and Home Building Fund of 1943 (Fund), Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 988, and a judgment for reimbursement of diverted funds. The parties had stipulated that the legislature had been appropriating funds from the Fund since 1965 for the purpose of defraying the expenses of county veterans' service offices. The court reversed the denial of the injunction, holding that the annual appropriation was inconsistent with the purpose of successive veterans' bond acts, and violated Cal. Const. art. XVI, § 1, which prohibited repeal of the bond law in order to prevent the legislature from making substantial changes in a scheme or design which had induced voter approval of a bond law. However, the court directed plaintiffs to cure procedural problems in the complaint on remand in order to proceed with the demand for reimbursement of diverted funds.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of an injunction prohibiting defendants, State of California and state agencies and officials, from further diversion of funds in the Veterans' Farm and Home Building Fund of 1943, as the diversion was contrary to the purpose of the bond laws and unconstitutional, but directed plaintiffs, military veterans' organization and others, to cure procedural problems in claim for reimbursement on remand.

In re Farr


Crim. No. 22815


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


36 Cal. App. 3d 577; 111 Cal. Rptr. 649; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 700



January 8, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Thus we conclude: (1) the petition and supplemental petition for habeas corpus do not state grounds for relief; and (2) execution of the judgment of contempt should be stayed to permit petitioner to institute proceedings in the trial court for relief from the provisions of the judgment in a manner consistent with this opinion. The petitions are denied. If the order of the United States Supreme Court releasing petitioner terminates prior to 60 days from the date of this opinion, execution of the judgment is stayed for that period.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner reporter sought review by habeas corpus of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found petitioner in contempt because of his refusal to disclose the identities of individuals who had provided him information in violation of that court's gag order in a criminal proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner reporter obtained information from attorneys in a high profile criminal case who were subject to an order not to release for public dissemination information regarding testimony to be offered in the trial. After the trial ended, the trial court ordered petitioner to disclose the identities of the individuals who had released the information to him. Petitioner refused to comply and was found in contempt. Petitioner sought review in state and federal courts, but the contempt judgment was not disturbed. Petitioner then filed for habeas corpus relief, claiming, among other things, that the judgment ordering his incarceration until he complied with the court order violated the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment. The court denied petitioner's request for habeas corpus relief, finding that none of his allegations stated grounds for relief, but stayed the execution of the order so that petitioner could seek a determination from the lower court that there was no substantial likelihood that his further incarceration pursuant to the contempt judgment would accomplish its intended purpose of coercing him to disclose the information.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner reporter habeas corpus relief, holding that the petition did not state grounds for relief, but the court stayed execution of the contempt judgment to allow him to petition the lower court for a determination of whether there existed a substantial likelihood that further incarceration would accomplish the purpose of the contempt order.

 

746 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Governing Board v. Metcalf


Civ. No. 39546


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


36 Cal. App. 3d 546; 111 Cal. Rptr. 724; 1974 Cal. App. LEXIS 697



January 4, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 995033, Leonard A. Diether, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant teacher appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared that plaintiff, the governing board of a school district, had sufficient cause to dismiss defendant on the basis of immoral conduct and evident unfitness for service as a teacher for being convicted of engaging in an act of prostitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant teacher was convicted of engaging in an act of prostitution on the basis of unconstitutionally obtained evidence. Plaintiff, the governing board of a school district, admitted that evidence at the trial of defendant's dismissal proceeding. Defendant alleged, on appeal, that his objection on constitutional grounds to the evidence should have been sustained, that his conduct was not immoral, and that it did not show unfitness to serve further as a teacher. The court concluded that whether the exclusionary rule could be applied to the dismissal proceeding was dependent on the purpose of the statute authorizing dismissal. The court held that even though a dismissal proceeding was punitive in nature, the primary purpose was to protect defendant's students and other children who viewed defendant as an example. Accordingly, the court held that the exclusionary rule did not apply to the dismissal proceeding of defendant and affirmed the judgment of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against defendant teacher, which declared that plaintiff, the governing board of a school district, had sufficient cause to dismiss him. The court held that the unconstitutionally obtained evidence, which led to defendant's conviction of prostitution, was admissible in defendant's dismissal proceeding, as the primary purpose of the proceeding was to protect defendant's pupils and not to penalize defendant.

 

747 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morgan


Crim. No. 1506


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


36 Cal. App. 3d 444; 111 Cal. Rptr. 548; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 671



December 28, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 117769, Jeremy C. Cook, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction by the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California) of robbery in the first degree and the enhancement of his sentence resulting from his use of a firearm during the commission of the crime.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of robbery in the first degree pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 211, and was found to have used a firearm during the liquor store robbery within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5. He appealed his conviction and his enhanced sentence. The court affirmed the judgment. As to appellant's contention that the enhanced sentence was cruel and unusual, the court held that appellant was not, as he contended, serving two consecutive life sentences, but rather one sentence of ten years to life. The court found this sentence to be within the legislature's broad discretion to specify punishment for crime. The court held that the imposition of the additional penalty of five years for the use of a firearm during the crime was not an unreasonable classification that denied appellant equal protection of the laws; it was clearly constitutional. The court held that the trial court committed no error by admitting into evidence a mug shot taken of appellant from a prior arrest. The appellant had admitted that he had been in jail before, so further evidence of criminal conduct was not prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction because allowing a photograph of appellant to be admitted into evidence was not prejudicial, and the enhancement of appellant's sentence due to the use of a firearm during the robbery was neither cruel and unusual nor an unreasonable classification that denied appellant equal protection of the laws.

Russell v. Carleson


Civ. No. 13757


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


36 Cal. App. 3d 334; 111 Cal. Rptr. 497; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 663



December 27, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 24, 1974, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 17, 1974. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 219590, Joseph G. Babich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, aid recipients and unrelated adult males, appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied plaintiffs a writ of mandate and injunctive relief in a class action suit filed against defendant welfare departments that challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11351.5.


OVERVIEW: Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11351.5 required cohabiting unrelated adult males (UAM) to make financial contributions, in amounts equal to the cost of an independent living arrangement, to families with whom they lived that received aid under the Aid to Families with Dependent Children Act (AFDC). Plaintiffs, aid recipients and cohabiting UAMs, challenged the constitutionality of the statute. The court affirmed the lower court's denial of a writ of mandate and injunctive relief against defendant welfare department's enforcement of § 11351.5. The court held that § 11351.5 did not violate due process or equal protection mandates, as § 11351.5 was reasonably related to the task of preventing the diversion of AFDC benefits from children to the UAMs and the fact that it dealt only with unrelated males rather than females was also reasonably related to the state's goal. The court also held that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11480, which allowed prosecution of UAMs who did not contribute financially, was a reasonable second method of preventing benefit diversion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of a writ of mandate and injunctive relief to plaintiffs, aid recipients and unrelated adult males (UAM) because the statute requiring financial contributions by UAMs to families with whom they lived that received benefits under the Aid to Families with Dependant Children Act was not unconstitutional, and was reasonably related to preventing the diversion of funds.

Van Nuis v. Los Angeles Soap Co.


Civ. No. 41628


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


36 Cal. App. 3d 222; 111 Cal. Rptr. 398; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 650; 39 Cal. Comp. Cases 88



December 21, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 13, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 956508, Julius M. Title, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders and the judgment are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor appealed from the judgment and orders of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denying its motions to set aside the settlement and to impress a lien thereon. Intervenor sought a lien against any settlement between plaintiff and defendants in the amount intervenor paid to plaintiff as workmen's compensation benefits pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code §§ 3850-3864.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff commenced this action to recover damages for personal injuries he sustained when he was struck by an automobile negligently operated by defendants. Defendants alleged that plaintiff was acting in the course and scope of his employment at the time of the accident, and that any judgment recovered by plaintiff should be reduced by the amount of payments made to him by workmen's compensation. Pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code §§ 3850-3864, intervenor filed a notice of lien in the action claiming that any settlement or judgment recovered by plaintiff was subject to a first lien for workmen's compensation benefits. It was stipulated that plaintiff settled his claim against defendants without intervenor's consent. The trial court entered a judgment of nonsuit on the complaint in intervention in favor of defendants since no evidence was presented as to defendants' negligence. The court determined that Cal. Lab. Code §§ 3859-3860 required the employer to bring an action against the third-party tortfeasor. The court affirmed the trial court ruling, and held that a judgment of nonsuit on the complaint in intervention was proper since no evidence of defendants' negligence was established.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, and held that the statute as amended provided that plaintiff may settle his claim against a third-party tortfeasor without the consent of his employer. Plaintiff's settlement was not subject to any lien based upon the employer's claim for reimbursement of compensation payments, since intervenor failed to assert a claim by way of an action against the third party.

Alex v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 41816


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


35 Cal. App. 3d 994; 111 Cal. Rptr. 285; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 772



December 13, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On January 9, 1974, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 7, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-17496, Charles H. Church, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judge challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained respondent county's demurrers to appellant's action for back pay and denied appellant's motion for a peremptory writ of mandate.


OVERVIEW: Appellant judge filed his candidacy to run for Congress and sought to take vacation time or sit as judge pro tempore; however, the judicial counsel advised him that he was required to take a leave of absence and could not work in any capacity pursuant to Cal. Const. art. VI, § 17. Appellant lost the election and returned to his judicial duties. He demanded back pay from respondent county and filed petitions for declaratory relief and for a writ of mandate. Respondent demurred; the trial court sustained the demurrers and denied the motion for a writ of mandate. Appellant challenged the judgment contending Cal. Const. art. VI, § 17 violated his equal protection rights as afforded by U.S. Const. amend. XIV, was impermissibly vague, and attempted to prescribe additional requirements to run for Congress. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. First, the court held that appellant's equal protection rights were not violated when applying the stricter constitutional standard to the aforementioned provision. Secondly, the court held the provision was not vague or fatally overbroad. Lastly, the court held the provision did not add another requirement to run for Congress.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment sustaining respondent county's demurrers to appellant judge's action for back pay and denying appellant's motion for a peremptory writ of mandate. The court held that the state constitutional provision relating to running for Congress was applied equally to all citizens, the provision was not overly broad or unconstitutionally vague, and did not add a requirement to run for Congress.

 

751 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lockheed Shipbuilding & Constr. Co.


Civ. No. 23939


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


35 Cal. App. 3d 776; 111 Cal. Rptr. 106; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 756



December 5, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 21, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 31, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 413019, George W. Trammel III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court certified the transfer of the decision of the appellate department of the superior court (California), which agreed with the municipal court's determination that sustained defendant corporation's demurrer to counts in a misdemeanor complaint for safety violations filed against it, on the theory that Cal. Lab. Code § 6315 as applied to orders and recommendations made under Cal. Lab. Code § 6313 were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A misdemeanor complaint was filed against defendant corporation that included charges of violations of safety orders and recommendations by the Division of Industrial Safety. Each order and recommendation was allegedly given by authority of Cal. Labor Code § 6313 and made criminal by Cal. Lab. Code § 6315. The municipal court sustained a demurrer to counts in the complaint based on the theory that § 6315 as applied to orders and recommendations made under § 6313 was unconstitutional. The appellate court agreed. The court affirmed the judgment against plaintiff state and held that the statutes were an attempt to authorize arbitrary, special legislation, and were thus unconstitutional. The court found that it was the joinder of the powers allegedly conferred by § 6313 with the criminal provisions of § 6315 that created the constitutional problem. Such joinder rendered the application of § 6315 to an order or recommendation made under § 6313 unconstitutional, as being violative of due process of law because there was no provision that required a hearing, and there was no provision for notice of their content to the affected person after they were made.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court against plaintiff state because the provisions of the California Labor Code that established authority and made criminal violations of the Division of Industrial Safety's recommendations as applied to defendant corporation were unconstitutional. The joinder of the two statutes did not provide a hearing, and there was no provision for notice of their content.

Leach v. Swoap


Civ. No. 13918


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


35 Cal. App. 3d 685; 110 Cal. Rptr. 62; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 746



November 29, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 221559, William A. White, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Director of the State Department of Social Welfare appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that granted a peremptory writ of mandate and found in favor of petitioner recipient of aid to the totally disabled.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner recipient of aid to the totally disabled required and received an attendant care grant. After he married a recipient, the county pursuant to state regulation reduced petitioner's attendant care grant. The fair hearing decision adopted by the Director of the State Department of Social Welfare (Director) found the county had correctly applied the regulation. Petitioner filed a petition for writ of mandate and declaratory relief seeking to have his attendant care grant increased and a retroactive award of benefits. The court ruled in favor of petitioner and granted the writ. The Director appealed and the court affirmed. The court rejected the argument that retroactive payments should not be awarded in the absence of a showing that services were performed and indebtedness therefor was created. The court also rejected the argument that there was a fundamental distinction between basic subsistence aid and services. Simply because an attendant care grant was restricted as to its purpose did not make it any less subject to the rule of retroactivity. The fact that petitioner survived without assistance did not wipe out the right to assistance that was vested at the time of need.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of petitioner recipient of aid to the totally disabled. The fact that an attendant care grant was restricted as to purpose did not make it any less subject to the rule of retroactivity. The court rejected the Director of the State Department of Social Welfare's argument that there was no basis for awarding retroactive payments for services in the absence of a showing that the services were performed.

 

753 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Jones


Crim. No. 11925


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


35 Cal. App. 3d 531; 110 Cal. Rptr. 765; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 731



November 21, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 31, 1974.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned for a writ of habeas corpus to challenge the trial court's imposition of a sentence of five years to life, which followed appellant's conviction on pleas of guilty to two counts of violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531, sale of marijuana.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought a writ of habeas corpus review of the sentence imposed by the trial court for defendant's violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531, sale of marijuana. Defendant claimed that the sentence of five years to life was cruel and unusual and disproportionate to the crime in violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 6. The court denied defendant's writ because the penalty was not out of proportion even though it was harsh and severe. The court held that marijuana was a dangerous drug, as the evidence seemed clear that use of marijuana could have the effect of aggravating or precipitating an underlying psychosis and could trigger amotivational syndrome. Comparison of the penalty of life imprisonment to other crimes with the same sentence demonstrated that the other crimes were typically more serious. The court held, however, that the danger in the use of marijuana and the greater availability of marijuana in states that border Mexico made the more severe penalty levied on the persons responsible for the problem reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence imposed for sale of marijuana and denied the petition for a writ of habeas corpus because the severity of the sentence was harsh, but reasonable when considered in light of the dangers of using marijuana and the increased availability of marijuana in California.

People ex rel. Dept. Pub. Wks. v. Adco Advertisers


Civ. No. 13767


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


35 Cal. App. 3d 507; 110 Cal. Rptr. 849; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 729; 4 ELR 20469



November 20, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 217124, J. Hilary Cook, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporation appealed from the summary judgment entered in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) in favor of plaintiff department of public works in an action for an injunction to compel the removal of a billboard under the Outdoor Advertising Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5200 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff department of public works filed a complaint for an injunction to compel the removal of defendant corporation's billboard. The trial court entered summary judgment declaring that the billboard was unlawfully placed and was being unlawfully maintained in violation of the Outdoor Advertising Act (Act), Cal. Bus & Prof. Code § 5200 et seq., and ordering defendant to cease maintaining the billboard and to remove it. Defendant appealed. The court rejected the argument that there was triable issue on whether the non-complying billboard was in fact a nuisance. The fact that the billboard failed to comport to the Act rendered it a nuisance as a matter of law because the Act defined non-complying billboards as such. A legislatively declared public nuisance constituted a nuisance per se against which an injunction could issue without allegation or proof of irreparable injury. The court rejected the argument that the compelled removal constituted an unconstitutional taking. Regulations regarding restrictions upon the use of property in an exercise of the police power for an authorized purpose did not constitute a taking without just compensation. Defendant's other contention failed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of plaintiff public works. The court found that each of defendant corporation's contentions as to alleged triable issues of fact lacked merit and failed as a matter of law. Further, defendant's contention as to the amortization period allowed by the Outdoor Advertising Act also failed.

 

755 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Quinn


Crim. No. 1598


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


35 Cal. App. 3d 473; 110 Cal. Rptr. 881; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 726



November 19, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] We conclude that the petition of John Emmett Quinn, Thomas and Richard Quimette, Patrick Warren Keefer, Robert Joseph Cloud, Harry Bennett Reynolds and Gerald W. Underhill for a writ of habeas corpus must be and the same is hereby denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner individuals sought a writ of habeas corpus to review a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which convicted petitioners for trespass, loitering, and fishing on county property.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner individuals were cited and convicted for violation of a county's ordinance that prohibited trespassing, loitering, or fishing on bridges. Petitioners sought a writ of habeas corpus to quash the conviction. They contended that the county ordinance violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 25, which guaranteed the right to fish on public lands and waters. The court determined that the constitutional right did not extend to property not owned by the state. County-owned property, such as the bridge, was not part of the public lands of the state, and the constitutional right to fish did not protect those fishing from county owned property. The county could exercise its police power to protect and preserve the safety and welfare of both pedestrians and persons traveling over the bridge. The conviction of petitioners was therefore appropriate, and their petition was denied.


OUTCOME: The petition was denied. Petitioner individuals were not entitled to habeas corpus relief after their convictions for trespassing, loitering, and fishing on county property, because petitioners' constitutional right to fish on state public lands and waters did not extend to property owned by the county.

Hughes v. County of San Diego


Civ. No. 12588


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


35 Cal. App. 3d 349; 110 Cal. Rptr. 754; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 716



November 15, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 27, 1974. Tobriner, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 311714, Charles W. Froehlich, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In his action for injuries sustained in a fire, plaintiff prisoner appealed an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), that granted a non-suit in favor of defendant county on the grounds that Cal. Gov't. Code § 844.6, which granted defendant immunity from liability to prisoners, was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff prisoner sustained injuries in a county jail when other prisoners ignited the flammable mattress his cell. Plaintiff sued defendant county, asserting that flammable mattresses should not have been used in a properly operated jail. The court affirmed the judgment of non-suit entered by the trial court. The court held that the order was proper because plaintiff was a prisoner at the time of the accident, and defendant was immune from liability to prisoners pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 844.6. The court determined that the statute was not unconstitutional because it had been upheld on equal protection and due process grounds.


OUTCOME: Order granting non-suit in favor of defendant county was affirmed because the statute granting immunity to defendant from liability to prisoners was constitutional.

 

757 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stearns


Crim. No. 22986


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


35 Cal. App. 3d 304; 110 Cal. Rptr. 711; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 712



November 14, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 28, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 10, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 108631, Paul G. Breckenridge, Jr., and James Greely Kolts, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of voluntary manslaughter pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 192(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of voluntary manslaughter under Cal. Penal Code § 192(1) after he pleaded guilty. Defendant contended that his pretrial motion to suppress his identification as the perpetrator of the crime was erroneously denied because the identification was tainted by an unconstitutionally composed lineup. Defendant argued that the pretrial ruling with respect to the lineup was reviewable on appeal after his guilty plea by analogy with Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(m) because pretrial motions to suppress were recognized as a proper means of challenging the fairness of a lineup. The court affirmed and held that even though the challenge to the lineup was heard at a special pretrial motion, it was not a motion under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 because that section only applied to evidence sought to be suppressed as the product of a search and seizure. The court found that section 1538.5(m), which allowed an appeal after a guilty plea, was not applicable to other kinds of pretrial rulings, thus the fairness of the lineup became immaterial after defendant pleaded guilty and the trial court's decision was not reviewable on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which convicted defendant of voluntary manslaughter. The court held that the matter was not reviewable after defendant pleaded guilty because the statute under which defendant sought review only applied to evidence sought to be suppressed as the product of a search and seizure.

 

758 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brown


Crim. No. 22490


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


35 Cal. App. 3d 317; 110 Cal. Rptr. 854; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 714



November 14, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A175074, Charles H. Older, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The sentence on count VI is set aside and the cause remanded to the trial court with directions to resentence defendant on that count, not to exceed one year in the county jail. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found him guilty of unlawful sexual intercourse under Cal. Penal Code § 261.5, and of sodomy under Cal. Penal Code § 286. The trial court sentenced defendant to be imprisoned in the state prison on all counts, the sentences to run concurrently.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for unlawful sexual intercourse (USI) under Cal. Penal Code § 261.5, and for sodomy under Cal. Penal Code § 286. The court affirmed the conviction but remanded for resentencing on one of the USI counts and held that defendant was aware at the preliminary hearing that his victim was a minor and thus he was not prejudiced when the prosecution amended the information to allege USI. The court also held that the officer's testimony was properly admitted because the victim's two-week delay in reporting defendant's conduct did not affect the admissibility of the testimony. Moreover, Cal. Penal Code § 264 was constitutional and the fact that the jury chose to convict appellant of USI instead of forcible rape was attributed to the mercy and leniency of the jury. Further, the punishment for USI was not cruel and unusual because while defendant committed forcible rape, which is punishable by life imprisonment, the jury only convicted him of unlawful intercourse. Finally, the court remanded defendant's sentence on one of the USI counts because the trial court failed to follow the jury recommendation that defendant be sentenced to county jail on that count.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for unlawful sexual assault (USI) and sodomy, but remanded his sentence on one of the USI counts because the trial court failed to follow the jury's recommendation that he be sent to county jail on that count. The court held that defendant was not prejudiced by the amended information that added an allegation of USI and that the officer's testimony was admissible as to the victim's report of sodomy.

 

759 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bidart Bros. v. Elmo Farming Co.


Civ. No. 41872


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


35 Cal. App. 3d 248; 110 Cal. Rptr. 819; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 706



November 12, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 29, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 10, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 99775, J. Kelly Steele, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant principal sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which permitted respondent agent to set-off rents it collected on behalf of appellant against claims asserted by respondent under purchase price promissory notes and deeds of trust pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 440.


OVERVIEW: Appellant principal filed a lawsuit against respondent agent to recover rents collected by respondent on behalf of appellant. The rents were on lands sold to appellant by respondent but were subject to certain leases. The trial court permitted respondent to set-off the rents against claims asserted under purchase price promissory notes and deeds of trust. The court affirmed, finding that respondent was entitled to set-off rents against a purchase money obligation to satisfy taxes and interest due on the original trust deed pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 440 because respondent could infer from the conduct between it and appellant, as well as the tenor of the agreement, that it was authorized by appellant to do so. The court also noted that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 580b did not nullify the provision of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 438 and did not prevent set-off because a purchase money obligation was involved. The court also found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in permitting respondent to file an amended answer to conform to proof. Finally, the court noted that the evidence supported the findings and that the accounting to appellant was not unfair.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment. The court found that respondent agent was authorized to set-off rents against the purchase money obligation to satisfy taxes and interest due on the original trust deed between respondent and appellant principal because this authority could be inferred from the conduct of the parties as well as the tenor of the agreement even though there was no precise agreement concerning the application of the funds.

 

760 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Castorena v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 41084


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


34 Cal. App. 3d 901; 110 Cal. Rptr. 569; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 858



October 29, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 14, 1973.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. The petition for a peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Chicano citizens filed for a peremptory writ of mandate, seeking to enjoin respondent, the City of Los Angeles, from using a councilmanic redistricting plan for elections and seeking an order requiring respondent to adopt petitioners' redistricting plan or any other constitutionally valid reapportionment plan.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner Chicano citizens sought a writ of mandate, seeking to enjoin respondent, the City of Los Angeles, from using a councilmanic redistricting plan. The court denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate. The court held that petitioners' claim regarding the even-numbered districts, which were scheduled to hold elections two years later than the odd-numbered districts, was moot because the case was resolved after the first set of elections. The court held that the time limits for redistricting were directory rather than mandatory because there was no provision for their enforcement. The court held that there was no evidence of an unconstitutional dilution of petitioners' voting strength but merely a showing that the redistricting plan had failed to make the most of petitioners' potential political power. The court found that the record indicated the opposite of an attempt to dilute Chicano voting strength. The court held that the considerations of incumbency were legally neutral. The court held that the constitutionally valid redistricting plan did not have to be revised in order to compensate for past discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner Chicano citizens' request for a peremptory writ of mandate, because the redistricting plan adopted by respondent, the City of Los Angeles, was constitutionally valid and did not impermissibly dilute petitioners' voting power.

 

761 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Keefer


Crim. No. 22413


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


35 Cal. App. 3d 156; 110 Cal. Rptr. 597; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 700



October 26, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 19, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 94751, John A. Westwick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant criminal sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which entered a grant of probation made after his conviction by a jury of two counts of grand theft and one count of attempted grand theft.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged a conviction of two counts of grand theft and one count of attempted grand theft and an order granting probation. Defendant contended that the evidence was insufficient to support the convictions, the jury was incorrectly instructed, and the order granting probation was constitutionally defective. The court held that the techniques defendant employed in his business transactions, even though separate and distinct one from another, were sufficiently similar in nature to show corroboration. The crimes had progressed to the point that it was reasonable to infer that they would have been completed except for the intervention of the arrests, which were sufficient to establish an attempt. The court found that the jury instructions were sufficiently complete to enable the jury to resolve the issues presented to it for decision. The court further held that defendant could be compelled to give up a fundamental or constitutional right as a condition to the granting of probation. In depriving defendant from engaging in the furnace or heating business as a condition of probation, the trial court did not abuse its discretion. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction for grand theft and attempted grand theft, holding that the techniques used by defendant in his business transactions were sufficient to establish an attempt, the jury instructions were sufficiently complete, and defendant could be compelled to give up a fundamental or constitutional right as a condition of probation.

Council of Directors and Supervisors v. Los Angeles Unified Sch. Dist.


Civ. No. 41443


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


35 Cal. App. 3d 147; 110 Cal. Rptr. 624; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 699



October 24, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 999668, Peter S. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment denying plaintiff declaratory relief is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff council challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied plaintiff's request for declaratory relief and held that defendant district had properly effected a mass demotion of school administrators and supervisors.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff council filed for declaratory relief with respect to the mass demotions of certain promotional level certificated employees, generally school supervisors and administrators, that was effected by defendant district as a result of limited funds. The trial court denied plaintiff's request for declaratory relief. Plaintiff sought review. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court and held that defendant's demotion of supervisors and administrators was legal and effective. The court held that the termination and reassignment provisions under Cal. Educ. Code §§ 13447, 13448 did not apply to the supervisors and administrators because they were not considered permanent employees. The notices given to the demoted supervisors and administrators were sufficient because the superintendent was authorized to send out the notices under Cal. Educ. Code § 13443.6, and the notices were required to prevent the automatic renewal of the one-year employment contracts. The reassignments were properly made in accordance with the rules of the Board of Education of the City of Los Angeles for the release and reassignment of demoted promotional level certificated employees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court that denied plaintiff council's request for declaratory relief against defendant district and held that defendant had properly effected a mass demotion of school administrators and supervisors because the termination and reassignment procedures only applied to permanent employees, sufficient notice was provided, and the reassignments were properly made.

 

763 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Urich Oil Co. v. Crown Discount Dep't Stores


Civ. No. 41049


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


34 Cal. App. 3d 743; 110 Cal. Rptr. 316; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 844



October 23, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 936 373, Alfred P. Peracca, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order dissolving the attachment and exonerating the bond is reversed, and the case is remanded to the superior court for proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion. Appellant shall be entitled to reasonable attorney's fees for prosecuting this appeal in an amount to be fixed by the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant supplier challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent customer's motion to dissolve the attachment and to exonerate the bond that had been issued in appellant's breach of contract suit.


OVERVIEW: Appellant supplier filed suit against respondent customer for breach of contract and attached its bank account. The attachment was released on the filing of a bond. The trial court entered a judgment to which the parties had stipulated, and granted respondent's motion to dissolve the attachment and to exonerate the bond. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the order that dissolved the attachment and exonerated the bond. The court also awarded attorney's fees to appellant for prosecuting its appeal. The court found that respondent's election to have the bond exonerated concurrently with its motion to dissolve the attachment estopped it from denying that the order was an order to dissolve an attachment. The court also concluded that the three year limit for enforcing an attachment was inapplicable because the only time limit on the current case was a promise to pay appellant the judgment up to a set amount if it prevailed. The court further reasoned that the original attachment as applied to the case was unconstitutional, and that had respondent made its motions prior to judgment the order to dissolve the attachment and exonerate the bond would have been valid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order to dissolve an attachment and exonerate a bond as to appellant supplier's breach of contract claim against respondent customer, remanded the case, and awarded appellant attorney's fees. The court held that trying to exonerate the bond concurrently with a motion to dissolve the attachment estopped a denial that the order, which would have been valid if the motions preceded the judgment, was one to dissolve.

 

764 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Fuchs v. Los Angeles County Civil Service Com.


Civ. No. 40705


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


34 Cal. App. 3d 709; 110 Cal. Rptr. 311; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 841



October 19, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 7, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 14, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 999616, Charles H. Church, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied him any relief in his suit that sought a hearing in front of respondent civil service commission to object to the design, grading and conduct of his promotional examination.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee filed suit and contended that he was entitled to a hearing in front of respondent civil service commission to object to the design, grading, and conduct of his promotional examination. The trial court denied relief and petitioner appealed. The court affirmed judgment of the trial court and held that hearings not described in the statutes were to be granted at respondent's discretion and were not added by implication. All potential recipients of a governmentally bestowed benefit were not entitled to a hearing before the benefit could constitutionally be withheld. It was required that the importance of the benefit be weighed against the harm that resulted from its deprivation. Respondent's procedures were constitutionally adequate with respect to employee promotions. There was no indication that respondent acted arbitrarily or unfairly to deprive petitioner of his promotional opportunity. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it allowed respondents to augment the administrative record and denied petitioner the right to present additional evidence to the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment denying petitioner employee relief, and held petitioner employee was not entitled to a hearing before respondent civil service commission to object to the design, grading, and conduct of his promotional examination.

 

765 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Raigoza v. Sperl


Civ. No. 41036


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


34 Cal. App. 3d 560; 110 Cal. Rptr. 296; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 826



October 10, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 29, 1973, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 5, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 25262, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant debtor challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a preliminary injunction and found that garnishment of wages did not deprive debtor of her property without due process of law.


OVERVIEW: Appellant debtor sought declaratory and injunctive relief from respondent judgment creditor's garnishment of her wages. Debtor claimed that the garnishment of her wages deprived her of property without due process of law arguing that the judgment only declared the amount of money she owed not how the judgment was to be satisfied. The trial court denied the preliminary injunction. The court affirmed the denial of the preliminary injunction and found that the statutory scheme of the wage garnishment laws, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690.6, were constitutional. The court found that due process required an opportunity for a hearing, which appellant received in the trial court, before the deprivation of property occurred. The court found that appellant was entitled to an exemption if she could not provide for her necessities, but that it was not an arbitrary deprivation and did not impinge on appellant's constitutionally protected rights to require her to apply for and prove the exemption because it was a matter of legislative choice to have appellant prove her need for the garnished wages than to make appellees disprove appellant's need for it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant debtor's preliminary injunction because placing the burden on appellant to prove his entitlement to the property exemption did not impinge on a constitutionally protected right and it was eminently reasonable to place the burden on appellant as the person who knew best what was required for the needs of her family.

People v. Wilson


Crim. No. 22995


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


34 Cal. App. 3d 524; 110 Cal. Rptr. 104; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 822



October 9, 1973

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction of child molesting by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), contending that the trial court abused its discretion in ruling that he was ineligible for probation, and was likewise ineligible to be committed as a mentally disordered sex offender. Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6301.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a plea bargain, Cal. Penal Code § 1192.5, defendant pleaded guilty to child molesting, and admitted an alleged prior conviction. After a hearing, the trial court found that no unusual circumstances existed wherein the interests of justice demanded that probation be granted and ruled that defendant was ineligible for probation. Cal. Penal Code § 1203(d)(2), (e)(5). Defendant was sentenced to state prison. Defendant challenged the ruling of the trial court, which found that he was ineligible for probation, and was likewise ineligible to be committed as a mentally disordered sex offender. Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6301. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in ruling that defendant's case was not an unusual one in which the interests of justice required that probation be granted. Probation was a matter solely within the sound, but broad, discretion of the trial court.

767 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Allyn v. Allison


Civ. No. 41187


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


34 Cal. App. 3d 448; 110 Cal. Rptr. 77; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 815



October 2, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 29, 1973, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 5, 1973. Tobriner, J., Mosk, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 30519, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant female voters challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed their petition for writ of mandate to require acceptance of their registration to vote.


OVERVIEW: Appellant female voters inserted "Ms." before their names on the voter's registration form, although it was required that their names be preceded by "Miss" or "Mrs." Respondent voter registrar refused to accept the registrations and the lower court dismissed appellants' petition for writ of mandate to require respondent to process the applications. The court affirmed because the requirement did not impair the right to vote on the basis of sex since it was not an unreasonable condition for registration. It determined that the purpose of the requirement was to disclose whether an applicant had previously been registered under another name and the requirement was not a denial of equal protection because women changed their names when they married, but a man did not. The court held that certainty of identification of prospective voters was a legitimate concern of the legislature and that this was a reasonable attempt to identify voters. It held that even if compliance with the requirement resulted in disclosure of marital status, such compliance was not onerous or burdensome and a woman was not disadvantaged by disclosure because there was nothing private about the status of marriage.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the dismissal of applicant female voters' petition for writ of mandate requiring respondent voter registrar to accept their voter's registration forms because requirement they put "Miss" or "Mrs." before their name was reasonable method to determine if voter had been registered under another name, and identification of prospective voters was a legitimate concern of the legislature.

Fraijo v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 42124


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


34 Cal. App. 3d 222; 109 Cal. Rptr. 909; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 796



September 12, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] A peremptory writ of mandate will issue as prayed for.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to accept as timely filed defendant's motion under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 176.0 to prohibit the judge assigned to his case from presiding at his trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged in two separate informations with two counts of burglary, two counts of violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501, and two counts of violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11503. Defendant, in separate hearings, negotiated plea bargains in each case and had the matters set for a probation hearing. The day before the hearing the presiding judge, by written order, withdrew from the plea bargain, set aside defendant's pleas of guilty, entered not guilty pleas, and set the matters for trial. Defendant filed separate declarations of prejudice under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6, which respondent trial court found to be not timely filed because the trial judge had previously made a determination of a factual issue. On petition the court issued a writ of mandate that directed respondent to accept as timely filed defendants § 170.6 motion. The court found that § 170.6 prohibited entertaining the motion only after a determination of contested fact issues relating to the merits. The court held that the judge's evaluation of admitted facts, to determine whether to refuse or accept a proposed plea bargain, did not involve a fact finding function.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate and directed respondent trial court to accept as timely filed defendant's declaration of prejudice because respondent, in evaluating the proposed plea bargain, did not engage in a fact finding function related to the merits of defendant's case.

September 11, 1973
