DISPOSITION: The alternative writ issued herein is discharged. The petition is denied, without prejudice to petitioner's making a motion in respondent court, pursuant to Penal Code section 1033 and if he sees fit, for a change of venue from Santa Clara County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner criminal defendant sought review of the order of respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied his motion for in pro per status, to vacate an order changing venue, requesting leave change his plea, and an order dismissing the kidnapping and murder charges against him.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner prisoner was charged with kidnapping and murder under Cal. Penal Code § 209 and Cal. Penal Code § 187. In his first trial, a verdict was not reach and a mistrial was declared. Thereafter, on petitioner's motion, venue was changed to respondent court. Petitioner filed a motion requesting that respondent court reinstate in pro per status, returning the matter to the prior court for a venue hearing, or to vacate the order changing venue, requesting addition of a plea based on a former judgment of acquittal, and to restrain prosecution by the people or to dismiss the indictment based on constitutional issues. The court affirmed the lower court's denial of petitioner's motion without prejudice to file a motion for change of venue under Cal. Penal Code § 1033. The lower court properly determined that petitioner was not competent to represent himself, that jeopardy did not attach to petitioner's former judgment, and that the statutes at issue were not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of petitioner prisoner's motion without prejudice to petitioner's making a motion in respondent court for a change in venue. The court held that the lower court's denial of in pro per status, denial of petitioner's motion to enter additional pleas, discharge of the jury, finding that jeopardy did not attach, and interpretations of the penal code were proper.

 

770 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Residents of Beverly Glen, Inc. v. Los Angeles


Civ. No. 39554


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


34 Cal. App. 3d 117; 109 Cal. Rptr. 724; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 786



August 30, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 18, 1973, and the petition of respondent U.S. Plywood-Champion Papers, Inc., for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 31, 1973. Mosk, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 908678, Arthur K. Marshall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff residential association appealed the summary judgment granted in favor of defendants, city and corporation, by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that plaintiff lacked standing to bring its action to set aside a conditional use permit granted by defendant city to defendant corporation, and to have a section of the Los Angeles Municipal Code declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff residential association brought an action to set aside a conditional use permit issued by defendant city to defendant corporation, and to have a section of the Los Angeles Municipal Code declared unconstitutional. Defendants were granted summary judgment on the ground that plaintiff lacked standing. Plaintiff appealed and the court reversed the summary judgment. The court concluded that plaintiff did have standing because plaintiff showed it was an "interested" party by alleging it was a corporation whose members lived in the affected area and that its members would suffer injury if the challenged project, to be constructed under a permit pursuant to an allegedly unconstitutional ordinance, was allowed to proceed. The court held defendants' declarations were insufficient to sustain the summary judgment because plaintiff alleged its members suffered injuries, it was clearly inferable that plaintiff sought to represent those members, and that plaintiff had standing to seek redress for the injuries allegedly suffered by its members.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment and concluded that plaintiff residential association had standing to maintain the action based on the fact that plaintiff alleged its members would suffer injury if the challenged project, to be constructed under a permit pursuant to an allegedly unconstitutional ordinance, was allowed to proceed; and held that Plaintiff sought to represent its injured members.

 

771 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


American Fidelity Fire Ins. Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 40797


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


34 Cal. App. 3d 51; 109 Cal. Rptr. 545; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 779



August 27, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 24, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 973505, Robert W. Kenny, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, in favor of defendant, in plaintiff's action seeking declaratory relief for an accounting and refund of certain taxes paid and defendant's cross-complaint alleging plaintiff owed taxes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was a duly licensed insurance company that executed surety bonds guaranteeing the payment of use and sales taxes due from those persons primarily liable for the taxes. Plaintiff filed action against defendant, alleging that penalties assessed on bonds of defaulting taxpayers were unconstitutional. The court found that the constitutional requirement of notice was satisfied by notice to the taxpayer of the delinquency. Duplicate notice to the surety was constitutionally unnecessary. Plaintiff also argued that a taxing system that imposed penalties was unreasonable, since it punished the surety rather than the actual wrongdoer, the defaulting taxpayer. The court found that the bonding requirement was a reasonable method of securing payment of revenue to the state. The penalty provisions were aimed to insure payment of taxes and not punish taxpayers or their sureties, who were not compelled to write bonds, but did so for remuneration.


OUTCOME: Affirmed, since lack of notice to plaintiff, as a surety, did not result in plaintiff being required to pay more than the original obligation; defendant not constitutionally required to give duplicate notice to plaintiff, since notice requirement was fulfilled by notice to taxpayer of the delinquency.

 

772 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Nicolino v. Cozens


Civ. No. 32209


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


33 Cal. App. 3d 1024; 109 Cal. Rptr. 498; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 959



August 20, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 18, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. P-22010, John M. Brenner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff driver appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) rendered for defendant, the Director of the Department of Motor Vehicles (California). On appeal, the driver claimed that the Department was unauthorized to suspend his driving privileges under Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(c) where a trial court had struck a prior conviction without expressly deciding its validity and recommended that there be no suspension.


OVERVIEW: The Department had administrative sanctions that it had to impose. Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(c) allowed a trial court to order that privileges not be suspended under § 13352 where a licensee was being convicted for the first time. In this case, the trial court recommended no suspension after striking the driver's prior conviction without first determining its validity. The Department suspended his privileges anyway, and he sued. The superior court held for the Department. On appeal, the court affirmed. The mere striking of the prior conviction was not the equivalent of a finding that there was no prior conviction. The trial court first had to find that the conviction was constitutionally invalid before it could order no suspension. To permit the trial court to strike a prior conviction that it had not found to be invalid and order no suspension violated legislative intent by allowing the trial court to decide whether to impose suspension on a second conviction under Cal. Veh. Code § 23102. Also, Cal. Veh. Code § 13352 did not violate the separation of powers because it did not simply give the Department discretion over the imposition of suspensions but required them in specific cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Respondent trial court denied the motion, and petitioner appealed. V and XIV.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate or prohibition, holding that the California provision allowing for a bond or cash deposit, without a due process hearing before such a taking, was unconstitutional in that it violated the due process requirements of the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments.

 

774 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Enskat


Crim. No. 22506


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


33 Cal. App. 3d 900; 109 Cal. Rptr. 433; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 946



August 8, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 24, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 368 363, Mary E. Waters, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellate Department of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), on its own motion, certified for review the decision of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which instructed the jury in defendant's trial for pornography to disregard expert testimony of community standards if it found that the conduct was so patently offensive it violated any conceivable community standard.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of his conviction for violating Cal. Penal Code § 311.2 for exhibiting an alleged obscene film. The appellate department of the superior court, on its own motion, certified the issue of whether the municipal court erred in its jury instructions as to the standard of obscenity. The court affirmed appellant's conviction, holding that a recent United State Supreme Court opinion did not hold Cal. Penal Code § 311 unconstitutional, but rather left it to the states to see if their statutes measured up to the new requirements. Except for the requirement in § 311 that obscene matter had to be "utterly without redeeming social importance," § 311 had been previously construed substantially in accordance with requirements, and the jury was so instructed. Courts could not read the "utterly without redeeming social importance" element of the offense out of § 311 because to do so would be to invade the province of the legislature by redefining the elements of the underlying crime, which violates the separation of powers principles. The trial court properly instructed the jury that evidence of community standards was unnecessary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the "utterly without redeeming social importance" element of the obscenity standard, under which defendant was tried, was constitutional and the jury was thus properly instructed.

People v. Parker

In re Maston


Crim. Nos. 6879, 6903


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


33 Cal. App. 3d 559; 109 Cal. Rptr. 164; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 916



July 19, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 6, 1973. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petitions for habeas corpus are denied and the orders to show cause discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Inmates filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus contending that life imprisonment without possibility of parole was cruel or unusual punishment in violation of Calif. Const. art. I, § 6 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Inmates were convicted and sentenced to life without the possibility of parole for kidnapping, beating, robbing and raping a woman. They sought habeas corpus relief contending that in punishing kidnappers more severely than murderers, California was violating equal protection under the laws afforded by Cal. Const. art. I, § 6 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court denied habeas corpus relief, reasoning that while arguments against the augmented penalty were available, the social utility and deterrent effect of the California sentencing hierarchy scheme, as sanctioned by the legislature, were not so out of line with the scale of punishments throughout the nation that a court may call it either cruel or unusual.


OUTCOME: The court denied inmates' petitions for habeas corpus and discharged the orders to show cause, as the charge of denial of equal protection disappeared with the recognition that the augmented California sentence was not so out of line with the scale of punishments throughout the nation that a court may call it either cruel or unusual.

Del Paso Recreation & Park Dist. v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 13535


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


33 Cal. App. 3d 483; 109 Cal. Rptr. 169; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 909



July 18, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 7, 1973, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 26, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 212383, B. Abbott Goldberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff recreation and park district challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which sustained without leave to amend the general demurrer of defendant board and defendant district in an action attacking the validity of district reorganization under the District Reorganization Act of 1965, Cal. Gov't. Code § 56000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant board reorganized recreation and park districts under the District Reorganization Act of 1965 (act), Cal. Gov't. Code § 56000 et seq., which detached a portion of plaintiff recreation and park district and annexed the area to defendant district. Plaintiffs attacked the reorganization, alleging improper procedure. The superior court held in favor of defendants, and, on appeal, the court affirmed the judgment, rejecting plaintiffs' constitutional argument that it was denied the right to vote on the annexation. Cal. Gov't. Code § 56438 permitted reorganization without an election because sufficient written protests were not filed. The court found that plaintiffs waived a defect in the legal description. Plaintiffs' territory could be detached and annexed to defendant district without plaintiffs' consent because Cal. Gov't. Code 56004 was inapplicable to a major reorganization in light of other relevant provisions of the act. Defendant board was properly designated the conducting district. Because a recreation and park district was a district of limited powers, plaintiffs were not entitled to mailed notice of board proceedings. Plaintiffs failed to plead estoppel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that sustained without leave to amend the general demurrer of defendant board and defendant district. The court held that plaintiff recreation and park district did not satisfactorily raise issues of unconstitutionality or invalidity of the procedures followed in the district reorganization.

People v. Solomon


Crim. No. 23269


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


33 Cal. App. 3d 429; 108 Cal. Rptr. 867; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 903



July 13, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 11, 1973. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 723440, Harold J. Sinclair, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As construed section 647, subdivision (e) withstands constitutional challenge. The judgment of the municipal court dismissing the complaint is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the superior court (California), which reversed the judgment of dismissal of the municipal court and certified the cause to the court. The court ordered the cause transferred to it to reconsider the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code § 647(e).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with disorderly conduct pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 647(e). Defendant demurred and the trial court sustained it and entered judgment dismissing the cause. The appellate department of the superior court reversed the judgment of dismissal and certified the cause to the court. The court ordered the cause transferred to reconsider the constitutionality of § 647(e). The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the legislative power employed to compel identification was consistent with constitutional rights. The court construed the requirement of identification as primary and controlling, and interpreted the requirement of accounting for presence as wholly subordinate and adjunct to identification, as operative only to the extent it assisted in producing credible and reliable identification. The court further held that the presence in the statute of the third element of being under circumstances that infringed upon the public safety furnished sufficient protection against arbitrary enforcement to make the statute constitutional. Thus, § 647(e) withstood constitutional challenge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of dismissal of the municipal court, holding that as construed, the applicable statute making a person guilty of disorderly conduct withstood constitutional challenge. The statute was neither vague, incriminatory, nor arbitrary.

Montgomery v. Board of Retirement

Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District

Pacific Gas & Electric Co. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 13827


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


33 Cal. App. 3d 321; 109 Cal. Rptr. 10; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 894



July 6, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 1, 1973, and the opinion and judgment were modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: Let the writ issue directing the trial court to ascertain if any payment additional to that deposited is reasonably necessary for the proper security of the real parties in interest under Code of Civil Procedure sections 1254 and 1255b or whether the deposit heretofore made suffices, and after having made such determination and confirmation of the deposit of any additional amount so required, to issue a writ of immediate possession to petitioner. Real parties in interest to recover their costs in this proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner utility company filed a writ of mandamus to compel respondent Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California) to authorize immediate possession of the subject properties despite the pending appeal of real parties in interest condemnees.


OVERVIEW: The trial court signed a judgment of condemnation and ordered a specified sum to be paid to real parties in interest condemnees for the condemnation of an eight-mile strip of land running across real parties' property for petitioner utility company's high-voltage transmission lines to a nuclear power plant. Petitioner was the successful plaintiff in the underlying eminent domain proceeding. While real parties' appeal was pending, petitioner requested respondent superior court to authorize possession of the properties, as mandated by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1254. The court agreed that the language of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1254 was controlling and mandatory. The trial court had determined that petitioner was entitled to acquire the property and that petitioner had compensated real parties accordingly. Real parties accepted the compensation and appealed the condemnation. Thus, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1254(b), respondent was required to authorize petitioner to take possession and to use the property until the final conclusion of the litigation. The court held that respondent abused its discretion in denying the application.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner utility company's writ of mandate and directed respondent superior court to issue an order authorizing the immediate possession of the subject property pending the appeal of real parties in interest condemnees. Petitioner, as the successful plaintiff in the underlying eminent domain action, was entitled to immediate possession of the property during real parties' appeal.

 

781 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Porno, Inc. v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 40715


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


33 Cal. App. 3d 122; 108 Cal. Rptr. 797; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 879



June 27, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 27813, James G. Whyte, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which required the lower court to order the return to respondent of motion picture projectors which had been seized pursuant to search warrants issued by the lower court in an obscenity case.


OVERVIEW: In an obscenity case, appellants sought review of a decision requiring the return of respondent's motion picture projectors, which had been seized pursuant to search warrants. The city attorney, as counsel for appellant real party in interest, cited the rule that evidence of a crime was subject to seizure. It was theoretically possible that, in a prosecution under Cal. Penal Code § 311.2, the prosecutor could offer in evidence of the distribution of obscenity a film projector, as corroboration of the officer's testimony that he saw the offending films projected on a screen. But, while possible, appellants had made no contention that respondent's projectors were seized for that purpose. The judgment of the lower court recognized that the seizure of the projectors were not necessary or proper evidence of the commission of a crime. Therefore, the sole purpose of the seizure must have been to stop an illegal practice. The use of a search warrant for this purpose, in advance of an on-the-merits determination of guilt, was improper as a violation of due process.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court was affirmed because, although respondent's seized projectors could have been used in the commission of a misdemeanor, they were designed for and capable of use for lawful purposes. Seizure of the projectors, where they were not intended to be introduced into evidence, was improper.

 

782 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Hypolite v. Carleson


Civ. No. 32095


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


32 Cal. App. 3d 979; 108 Cal. Rptr. 751; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1033



June 18, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 16, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 424055, William H. Brailsford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to the trial court to grant the peremptory writ of mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, minors and their guardians, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Alameda County (California), which denied them declaratory and injunctive relief and a writ of mandate challenging a regulation issued by respondent Director of the Department of Social Welfare of the State of California for excluding certain children from participating in the Aid to Families with Dependent Children program.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, minors and their guardians, were denied benefits under the Aid to Families with Dependant children (AFDC) program, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 601-610 and Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 11200- 11488, on the authority of Cal. State Dept. of Social Welfare and Eligibility Assistance Standard Reg. 41-450.12 (regulation) issued by respondent Director of the Department of Social Welfare of the State of California. The regulation limited AFDC benefits to situations of continued absence, which excluded children whose parents maintained a home together although they had deserted the child. The trial court denied relief to plaintiffs challenging the regulation. On appeal, the court reversed because the regulation was void and unconstitutional. The court held that the provision of the regulation that continued absence did not exist, within the meaning of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11250, when both parents maintained a home together but the child lived elsewhere, was void because it arbitrarily and unreasonably created a classification of children, who were similarly situated with other children who received aid, contrary to the equal protection clause of U. S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court reversed because the regulation denying AFDC benefits to children whose parents had abandoned them but who maintained a home together elsewhere violated the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment by creating a dissimilar classification of children who were similarly situated with other children to whom aid had not been denied.

 

783 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kaylor v. Department of Human Resources Dev.


Civ. No. 31786


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


32 Cal. App. 3d 732; 108 Cal. Rptr. 267; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1013



June 1, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 25, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 421116, Robert H. Kroninger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying the petition for writ of mandate is reversed, with directions to the trial court to grant a writ of mandate directing the Board to set aside its order and to allow appellant's claim in the appropriate amount. The trial court will allow appellant costs of trial and of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee, having been discharged from his job and having been denied unemployment insurance by the Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, sought a writ of mandate to compel an order for payment. Unemployment benefits were denied based on Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1256.1. The writ was denied by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California). Appellant challenged the denial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee was cited for driving a vehicle without an operator's license. He was sentenced to pay a fine or to serve seven days in jail. He was unable to pay the fine and spent a week in jail. The company knew where he was, so his termination on the basis of involuntary absence was unavailable. The decision of the Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board that denied benefits was based on Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1256.1, which provided that absence from work because of incarceration, if one was convicted, constituted leaving work voluntarily without good cause. However, compulsion to serve a sentence, which was imposed only in lieu of a fine, which could not have been paid because of indigence, constituted an invidious discrimination in violation of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The Board, an administrative agency, could not have declared the sentence unconstitutional, but it had the jurisdiction to hold that § 1256.1 was to be construed as referring only to incarceration that was not a substitute for a fine that could not have been paid. Therefore, it was an abuse of its discretion for the Board to have denied unemployment benefits. The court reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgment that denied the petition for writ of mandate was reversed, with directions to the trial court to grant a writ of mandate directing the Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board to set aside its order. The Board had jurisdiction to liberally construe the challenged statute, and as indigency was the sole reason for defendant having to spend time in jail, it was an abuse of its discretion to deny the unemployment benefits.

 

784 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Katz v. Department of Motor Vehicles


Civ. No. 32200


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


32 Cal. App. 3d 679; 108 Cal. Rptr. 424; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1006



May 30, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 29, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 25, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 276618, James Duvaras, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant auto owner sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied a writ of mandate to compel respondent motor vehicle department to issue a personalized license plate containing sexually suggestive words.


OVERVIEW: Appellant auto owner applied to respondent motor vehicle department under Cal. Veh. Code §§ 5100- 5110 (1970) for a personalized license plate containing the words "EZ LAY." Respondent refused on the basis of language in Cal. Veh. Code § 5105 that permitted respondent to refuse applications that might carry connotations offensive to good taste and decency. The trial court denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to compel issuance of the plate, and the court affirmed. The statutory standard upon which respondent based its refusal to issue the plate was directed only at respondent's employees, did not impose any duty or criminal sanctions on appellant, and was not void for vagueness. Respondent's refusal left appellant free to put the requested words on his car, even in the frame surrounding the plate, and its impact on appellant's right of expression was negligible and incidental. The fact that respondent might have issued a few plates as offensive as the one requested by appellant did not establish that the statutory standard was inadequate or that respondent's refusal was an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying a writ of mandate to compel respondent motor vehicle department to issue a personalized license plate containing sexually suggestive words was affirmed. Respondent's refusal to issue the plate was not an unconstitutional abridgment of appellant auto owner's freedom of speech because he still had the right to express himself on his car.

Bozaich v. State of California


Civ. No. 1679


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


32 Cal. App. 3d 688; 108 Cal. Rptr. 392; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1007



May 30, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 29, 1973, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 25, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 210775, B. Abbott Goldberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff claimants appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted defendant state's motion to strike from the class-action complaint all references to plaintiffs other than the named plaintiffs. Plaintiffs had claimed to represent unidentified, similarly situated claimants in inverse condemnation proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Two named plaintiff claimants, purporting to represent a class of unidentified similarly situated claimants sued defendant state for inverse condemnation damages. The putative class members had not complied with the requirement of Cal. Gov't Code § 905.2 that a claim be filed under Cal. Gov't Code § 910 before bringing an action in inverse condemnation. The trial court granted defendant state's motion to strike from the complaint all references to plaintiffs other than the named plaintiffs. The court of appeal affirmed. The requirements for a class action of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 382 were not met because the independent factual issues which would have to be separately litigated would be so numerous that the maintenance of the alleged class action could not possibly serve the judicial process or the parties involved. The court also held that allowing plaintiffs' action to proceed as a class action would be inconsistent with the requirements of Cal. Gov't Code § 382 that each person asserting an inverse condemnation claim against defendant identify himself, and disclose the particulars of his claim, including a description of the specific loss incurred.


OUTCOME: The order striking from the class-action complaint all references to plaintiffs other than the named plaintiff claimants was affirmed. The action against defendant state for inverse condemnation damages did not qualify as a class action because numerous independent factual issues had to be separately litigated for each member of the alleged class. Moreover, the putative members of the class failed to satisfy statutory claim-filing requirements.

 

786 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sneed


Crim. No. 1301


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


32 Cal. App. 3d 535; 108 Cal. Rptr. 146; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 996



May 21, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 5, 1973, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 16, 1973. McComb, J., Burke, J., and Clark, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 7147, Donald R. Fretz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In the absence of the improperly admitted evidence, there was insufficient proof to sustain the conviction. The judgment must therefore be reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which entered a judgment of conviction for unlawful cultivation of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530.1. Defendant asserted that officer's observations of growing plants from a helicopter constituted an illegal search that tainted other evidence flowing from it and should have been suppressed under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of unlawful cultivation of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530.1. He was arrested after police used a helicopter to observe marijuana plants growing on his property and then proceeded to obtain consent from someone with access to the property. The court applied a reasonableness test and found that the helicopter search was an illegal search because police did not obtain a warrant and it did not fall within the class of exceptions to the warrant requirement. The court held that other evidence obtained from a ground search and defendant's subsequent confession was also tainted under the fruit of the poisonous tree doctrine and also should have been suppressed. Without the illegal evidence the court found that defendant's conviction was not supported by substantial evidence and reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for unlawful cultivation of marijuana. The court held that evidence obtained pursuant to a helicopter search of defendant's property and other evidence flowing from that search was improperly admitted. Without the illegal evidence, the court found that defendant's conviction was not supported by substantial evidence.

 

787 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Aguilar


Crim. No. 21575


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


32 Cal. App. 3d 478; 108 Cal. Rptr. 179; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 992



May 18, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 12, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-224398, Rafael H. Galceran, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found him guilty of second-degree murder, and found that he used a firearm in committing the offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed the man who had been carrying on an affair with defendant's wife. Defendant claimed self-defense, but with the exception of the body of the victim, nothing at the scene indicated that a fight between defendant and the victim preceded the shooting. The court affirmed defendant's conviction for second-degree murder, with a finding pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5 that he used a firearm in committing the offense. The evidence was sufficient to support the verdict. Questions referring to defendant's prior incidents with police were proper cross-examination of defendant's character witness, were addressed by limiting instructions, and were not prejudicial. The jury was not coerced to reach its verdict. Defendant withdrew requests for instructions on manslaughter, apparently for rational tactical reasons, and any error in omitting those instructions for the jury charge was invited. Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5 when applied to murder, and was not unconstitutional. Allowing evidence that defendant did not claim self-defense after his arrest was harmless error because the same information came in at various times without objection.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of second-degree murder was affirmed. The evidence was sufficient to sustain the conviction, the prosecution's references to defendant's prior misconduct was not error, the jury was not coerced, the omission of a manslaughter charge if error was invited, the additional sentence for use of a firearm was constitutional, and evidence that defendant initially failed to claim self-defense was harmless error.

 

788 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Los Altos Hills v. Adobe Creek Properties, Inc.


Civ. No. 30116


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


32 Cal. App. 3d 488; 108 Cal. Rptr. 271; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 993



May 18, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of all the appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 12, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 230546, 230577, Homer B. Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged the adverse judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that concluded that a municipal zoning ordinance that permitted non-profit recreational facilities but not for-profit facilities was not an unconstitutional deprivation of property without due process and that a comprehensive plan that eliminated all commercial uses of property did not constitute an equal protection violation.


OVERVIEW: Following a consolidated trial cases litigating zoning issues, the trial court issued a judgment concluding that the zoning ordinance at issue, which prohibited the operation of profit-making recreational facilities while permitting non-profit recreational facilities, did not constitute a deprivation of property without due process of law and that the comprehensive zoning plan, which eliminated the commercial use of property within city limits, did not violate equal protection rights. Appellant property owners sought review. In affirming, the appellate court concluded that the zoning ordinance properly distinguished between business and non-profit uses, and its purpose was not for revenue but to provide for the public health, safety, morals, and welfare of the community. There was a rational distinction between the attributes of the commercial and non-commercial recreational uses as well was the burdens each imposed upon the municipality and the residents residing within city litigation. Further, the zoning plan, although it eliminated all commercial use of property, was reasonable in object and not arbitrary. It was thus a justifiable exercise of police power.


OUTCOME: The trial court judgment deeming the zoning ordinance and comprehensive plan to be valid was affirmed, as a rational basis grounded in the community's welfare existed for the distinction drawn between the non-profit and for-profit uses of property and as the comprehensive plan's elimination of commercial uses of property was a justifiable exercise of police power where it was reasonable and not arbitrary.

Parking Authority v. Nicovich


Civ. Nos. 13401, 13552


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


32 Cal. App. 3d 420; 108 Cal. Rptr. 137; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 987



May 16, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 4, 1973, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 12, 1973. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of condemnation proceedings from the Superior Court of Sacramento County, California, in which his request for moving expenses was denied as part of the award of compensation for their real property, condemned in fee by respondent city parking authority.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owner sought review of the trial court's determination that he was not entitled to be compensated for moving expenses as part of his award for respondent city parking authority's condemnation of his property. Appellant argued that Cal. Gov't. Code § 7262 was recently enacted and applied retroactively to allow the court to award his moving expenses. The court affirmed and held that the statute could not be applied retroactively, as there was no such express intent therein. Further, the court held that specific language of Cal. Gov't Code § 7270 stated that no element of damages not in existence on the date of enactment of that chapter was created. Further, the court held that additional costs appellant requested were properly disallowed as they were not made necessary to secure just compensation for the taking accomplished by eminent domain, and the attorney's fees were not allowable in holdover proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the disallowing of moving costs in appellant's award of compensation for the taking of his property in eminent domain proceedings as not permitted by law.

 

790 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Grace


Crim. No. 5255


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


32 Cal. App. 3d 447; 108 Cal. Rptr. 66; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 989



May 16, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-26181, Gilbert Harelson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California) that denied his motion to suppress drug evidence. Following the denial of his motion, defendant pleaded nolo contendere to possession of dangerous drugs, a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11910 (now § 11350).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession of dangerous drugs, a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11910 (now § 11350). He made a motion to have the drug evidence suppressed. Following the denial of his motion, defendant pleaded nolo contendere to the charge, and was placed on probation. On appeal, the court reversed the conviction. When the officer learned that his initial reason for stopping defendant had no basis in fact, his business with defendant was concluded. The officer did not have reasonable cause to believe that the car was not equipped in accordance with the Vehicle Code, and he had no right to conduct an exploratory examination to discover a violation. There was nothing in the record that supported the legality of the pat-down or the finding that defendant voluntarily consented to the search that revealed the contraband. The fact that defendant had been stopped for a vehicle equipment violation did not provide reasonable grounds for the officer to believe that defendant was carrying weapons. The officer failed to meet his burden of establishing that defendant voluntarily consented to the second search.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction of possession of dangerous drugs, holding that the police officer had no right to conduct an exploratory examination to discover a violation. The court ruled that the total record revealed a series of unconstitutional transgressions that made the final search illegal.

Guardianship of Gridley


Civ. No. 31484


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


32 Cal. App. 3d 1053; 108 Cal. Rptr. 200; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1039



May 11, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied 32 Cal. App. 3d 1053 at 1065.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 157992, H. A. van der Zee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The "judgment" (order) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant guardian sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that ordered him to pay charges incurred by respondent state for providing care for his incompetent ward pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 7279.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was the guardian of an incompetent ward. Pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 7279, respondent state sought to recover expenses incurred in providing care to appellant's ward from the assets of the estate of the incompetent ward. respondent filed a petition that sought an accounting from appellant and the establishment of an equitable lien in favor of respondent for the charges incurred for the care, support, and maintenance of the ward. Appellant submitted the accounting but asserted respondent could not unilaterally fix the amount of charges. Appellant sought review of an order of the lower court that denied his request for a hearing. The estate of a patient in a state hospital for the mentally disordered was liable for the care, support, and maintenance of the patient. Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 7252. Pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 7277, respondent was allowed to collect care expenses. Because establishment of the rates were matters that the lower court could take judicial notice of, and appellant did not object, the rates were properly established in the trial court. The court found there was nothing to prevent the ward's estate from paying for her support.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the lower court that ordered appellant guardian to pay charges incurred by respondent state for providing care to appellant's incompetent ward was affirmed because the estate of a patient in a state hospital for the mentally disordered was liable for the care, support, and maintenance of the patient.

Vielehr v. State Personnel Bd.


Civ. No. 1707


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


32 Cal. App. 3d 187; 107 Cal. Rptr. 852; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1257



May 9, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 148449, Harold V. Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court denying the writ of mandate is reversed and the cause is remanded to the superior court for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state employee sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Fresno County (California) that denied his petition for a writ of mandate to reverse the decision of respondent state personnel board to terminate him for misconduct under Cal. Gov't Code § 19572(t). Appellant, a tax representative trainee, asserted that his conviction for possession of marijuana off the job was not sufficient to justify termination.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed a judgment that denied appellant state employee's request for a writ of mandate to reverse a decision of respondent state personnel board to terminate appellant for misconduct under Cal. Gov't Code § 19572(t). Appellant was a state tax representative trainee and was convicted of a charge of possessing marijuana while off the job. At his hearing, no evidence other than the conviction was introduced. The court stated that in order to save § 19572(t) from being unconstitutionally vague, it must be applied in light of the type of employment to determine whether or not an alleged failure of good behavior was of such a nature that it caused discredit to his agency or his employment, and that there must be a rational relationship between the goal which § 19572(t) sought to achieve and the failure of good behavior. Finding that there was no obvious relationship between possessing marijuana off the job and the duties of a tax representative trainee, the court concluded that the conviction alone was not sufficient to justify disciplinary action and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that denied appellant state employee's petition for a writ of mandate after appellee state personnel board terminated his employment after he was convicted of possession of marijuana. The court found that the conviction for marijuana possession, alone, did not justify discipline under the applicable statute. The case was remanded for further proceedings.

Realty Projects, Inc. v. Smith


Civ. No. 38744


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


32 Cal. App. 3d 204; 108 Cal. Rptr. 71; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 977



May 9, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 5, 1973, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above on June 5 and 8, 1973. The petition of appellants Realty Projects, Inc. and Zimmerman for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 6, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 976285, Charles H. Church, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mortgage brokers challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which denied their petition for a writ of mandate to direct respondent Department of Real Estate to set aside its decision and order suspending and revoking appellants' real estate licenses.


OVERVIEW: Appellants mortgage brokers used the deceptive practice of failing to disclose to prospective borrowers that, if loans were obtained that are outside the amounts of loans to which the statutory compensation limitation was attached, the borrowers would be subject to significant charges and broker's fees. Judgment was obtained against appellants and an order was granted by respondent Department of Real Estate to suspend and revoke their real estate licenses. Appellants challenged the ruling arguing that they were under no duty to disclose the difference in loan and the compensation therefrom, and that the decision was not supported by substantial evidence. The court noted that there was a limited statutory duty to disclose under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 10000, 10131(d), and 10176(a) and (i). The duty was one of fair and honest dealings imposed on all real estate licensees, and, because all mortgage brokers had to have real estate licenses, the duty applied. The court also found that there was substantial evidence to support the findings on all the subject loans and therefore affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The judgment suspending and revoking appellant mortgage brokers' real estate licenses was affirmed. Appellants had a limited duty of fair and honest dealings with the borrowers that mandated that they disclose the difference in the loan amounts obtained as regarded fees and costs to the borrower and compensation to the broker.

 

794 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


California State Employees' Assn. v. Flournoy


Civ. No. 40554


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


32 Cal. App. 3d 219; 108 Cal. Rptr. 251; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 978



May 9, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 5, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 982 941, Charles H. Church, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeal from the order sustaining general demurrers without leave to amend is dismissed. The judgment (order of dismissal pursuant to Code Civ. Proc., § 581, subd. 3) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, faculty members and academic employees, challenged the decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which dismissed their complaint, sustained respondents' general demurrers, and denied their petition for a writ of mandate and declaratory relief to compel salary increases for the fiscal year 1970-1971.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, faculty members and academic employees, filed a petition for a writ of mandate and declaratory relief seeking to compel respondent university system to grant salary increases for the fiscal year. The petition was denied, the complaint was dismissed, and respondents' demurrers were sustained. Appellants challenged the ruling arguing that the refusal to grant the wage increase amounted to a bill of attainder, that they had an implied-in-fact contract for the pay raises, and that it was an equal protection violation to deny the raises. The court found that the refusal was not a punishment for failure to control campus unrest due to anti-war protests. The court also found that the pay raises did not amount to an implied-in-fact contract because the raises were consensual and to compel the raises would remove the consensuality from the alleged contract. Additionally the court noted that only the legislature could provide the money for the raises and that the equal protection clause did not guarantee absolute equality with similarly situated parties. The court therefore dismissed the appeal to the sustaining of the demurrers and affirmed the judgment dismissing the action.


OUTCOME: The trial court's denial of appellants, faculty members and academic employees, petitions for writs and dismissal of their complaint regarding wage increases was affirmed because the refusal to grant the raises was not punishment, there was no implied-in-fact contract due to the absence of mutual consent to the raises, and because the equal protection clause did not guarantee absolute equality with other state workers.

In re Young


Crim. No. 11080


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


32 Cal. App. 3d 68; 107 Cal. Rptr. 915; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 966



May 3, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 31, 1973, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 28, 1973. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition is accordingly granted and the Adult Authority is directed to credit the 62 days of petitioner's presentence confinement to the term prescribed by the sentence pronounced on June 25, 1971 by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County in petitioner's case.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus, contending that the period of his incarceration, caused by his financial inability to furnish bail, between his arrest and his delivery to the department of corrections, should be credited against his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was arrested and charged with sale of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health and Safety Code § 11531, and due to his financial inability to furnish bail, was confined to the county jail for the period between his arrest and his delivery to the department of corrections. After his conviction, he sought review, contending that the period of his incarceration prior to trial should be credited against his sentence. The court granted the petition for a writ of habeas corpus. It held that Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5, that excepted prisoners delivered to corrections prior to its effective date from its provision that pretrial or probationary jail time shall be credited to a minimum prison term, was an unconstitutionally discriminatory deprivation of liberty that did not meet federal equal protection standards or the mandate of Cal. Const. art. I, § 11 that all laws have uniform operation. The court directed the trial court to credit the period of petitioner's presentence confinement to his sentence.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition and ordered that petitioner's pretrial period of incarceration should be credited against his sentence. The court held that excepting prisoners delivered to corrections prior to enactment of the statute was an unconstitutional discriminatory deprivation of liberty, and further that it failed to comply with the state constitutional mandate that all general laws have uniform operation.

Griswold v. County of San Diego


Civ. No. 11260


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


32 Cal. App. 3d 56; 107 Cal. Rptr. 845; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 965



May 2, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 28, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 328862, Robert O. Staniforth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case is remanded to the trial court with instructions to enter a judgment denying the writ.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) finding that appellant's redistricting ordinance was invalid as unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondents sued appellant county, seeking a writ of mandate challenging San Diego, Cal., Ordinance 3767(1) (Oct. 5, 1971) as unconstitutional. The reapportionment ordinance had the effect of deferring the right to vote at the next election for some voters. The trial court concluded that the ordinance was invalid as a violation of the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court reversed, holding that the reapportionment ordinance was based on a rational policy. The plan, based upon rational considerations, which achieved equality of population among the districts, corrected previously existing fragmentation of minority communities, and joined previously separated neighboring cities into a single district did not violate the equal protection requirements merely because it made substantial changes in existing district lines and thus deferred the right to vote at the next election for some voters.


OUTCOME: The order finding appellant county's redistricting ordinance invalid was reversed because the ordinance did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment where it was rationally related to appellant's attempt to achieve equality of population among districts, correct previously existing fragmentation of minority communities, and joined previously neighboring cities into a single district.

People v. Moore


Crim. No. 10577


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


31 Cal. App. 3d 919; 107 Cal. Rptr. 590; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1120



April 27, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 20, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 49152, Redmond C. Staats, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order dismissing the information is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff prosecution appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which dismissed an information that charged defendant with drug offenses because the prosecution could not proceed without contraband recovered on defendant's premises after the trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence under Cal. Penal Code §1538.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession for sale of a restricted dangerous drug, seconal, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11911 and possession of marijuana for sale under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530.5. The trial court dismissed the information after granting defendant's motion to suppress because there was an ambiguity on the face of the warrant; the filled-in portion referred to code sections dealing with restricted dangerous drugs while the printed portion referred to narcotics. The prosecution appealed. The court reversed the trial court judgment of dismissal and order granting defendant's motion to suppress holding that the error which occurred in the preparation of the search warrant was purely clerical in nature and did not in any way substantially prejudice defendant's rights. The court held that the fact that there was some error, omission, or ambiguity was insufficient to defeat the warrant's validity where reference could be made to the underlying affidavit. The court found that the issuing magistrate intended to direct a search for dangerous drugs and only by reason of clerical error failed to insert such direction on the face of the warrant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment of dismissal and order granting defendant's motion to suppress holding that the error in the warrant was purely clerical and did not in any way substantially prejudice the rights of defendant. The court held that the magistrate intended to direct a search for dangerous drugs, but erroneously used the printed form that referred to narcotics.

 

799 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Duskin v. San Francisco Redevelopment Agency


Civ. No. 30321


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


31 Cal. App. 3d 769; 107 Cal. Rptr. 667; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1107



April 23, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Francisco County, No. 625272, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed a judgment of dismissal of the Superior Court of San Francisco County (California) which sustained without leave to amend the demurrer of defendants, state agency and its officials, on the grounds of failure to state a cause of action. Plaintiff had brought suit against defendants for allegedly selling real property at less than market value and for failure to hold a public hearing with respect to the sale.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer brought suit against defendants, state agency and its officials, for allegedly selling real property at less than fair market value and for failure to give a public hearing with respect to the sale. After the trial court sustained defendants' demurrer for failure to state a cause of action, plaintiff appealed. Defendants' demurrer stated that pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526(a) plaintiff failed to state a cause of action because § 526(a) limited plaintiff's action to officers and agents of counties, towns and cities, and defendants were administrative arms of the state. In reversing, the court held that state officials could have been sued under § 526a. Moreover, plaintiff's complaint was not limited to, nor did not specify, § 526a. The court emphasized that taxpayers had the right to bring actions to enjoin the allegedly illegal expenditure of public moneys by state officials. In rejecting defendants' defense of laches, the court noted that since no prejudice appeared from the face of plaintiff's complaint, such a defense was an improper ground for the trial court to have sustained defendants' demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of dismissal that sustained defendants', state agency and its officials, demurrer based on plaintiff taxpayer's failure to state a cause of action because defendants could have been sued under the relevant statute, which did not limit plaintiff's action to local agencies and officials. Moreover, the trial court improperly sustained defendants' demurrer on the grounds of defendants' laches defense.

 

800 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Jeannie Q.


Civ. No. 39957


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


32 Cal. App. 3d 288; 107 Cal. Rptr. 646; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 982



April 18, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 10, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 28, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. 417883-417887, Edward F. Perry, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The judgments (orders) appealed from are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), declaring appellant's five children dependents of the juvenile court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600 based on the need for proper and effective parental care or control.


OVERVIEW: Petitions were filed to declare five children of appellant mother dependents of the juvenile court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600. A protective services worker contacted appellant's family while responding to a telephone call from school alleging bruises and cuts were found on one of the children. A caseworker arranged, with the cooperation of appellant, to have the children examined by medical providers. The examination showed two of the children were malnourished with signs of retardation. A neighbor testified to a number of incidents in which appellant had mistreated or threatened to mistreat her children. After taking testimony and other evidence, the court concluded that each of the petitions should be sustained as to Calf. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600(a) based on the need for proper and effective parental care or control. After a disposition hearing, the court ordered the children to remain in the protective custody for eventual placement in foster homes. Appellant argued that the children should have been provided with child protective services prior to removal. The court affirmed, finding that the court's obligation ran primarily to the welfare of the children.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed. Appellant mother's five children were properly declared dependents of the juvenile court, because some of the minors were the victims of severe malnutrition which had adversely affected them mentally, physically, and socially, thus further harm might have occurred if the state social welfare agency had first been required to offer protective services before taking steps to remove the children from appellant's home.
